# Likkutei Sichos


## דברים

## Sicha 1

## 1

A. Regarding the verse, "See, I have given the land before you; come and inherit the land, etc.", Rashi quotes from the text, "See, I have given," and explains: "You see with your own eyes; I am not telling you based on estimation or hearsay."

The simple intent of Rashi is that the word "See" does not mean (as we find in many places) "an expression of understanding" (similar to Rashi’s commentary on the verse, "See, You say to me, 'Bring up this people'" — "See, set your eyes and heart upon your words"), where Bnei Yisrael would need to set their eyes and hearts upon the words of Hashem, "I have given the land before you." Rather, it means, "You see with your own eyes — I am not telling you based on estimation or hearsay," meaning vision in its literal sense, vision with physical eyes.

This requires understanding:
Since at that time Bnei Yisrael had not yet arrived in Eretz Yisrael, the literal meaning of the verse seemingly forces us to interpret "See, I have given the land before you" as an expression of understanding and setting one's heart —[for how can it be said, "You see with your own eyes"—that Bnei Yisrael see with their eyes that Hashem gave them the land, when they had not yet arrived there !]—so how can it be said that the literal meaning of the verse forces us to interpret "See" in this verse as concrete vision?

Some commentators suggest that "He intended the killing of Sichon and Og, upon whom the kings were relying, and nevertheless, Yisrael conquered them easily," and thus Yisrael saw with their own eyes a conquest that was the beginning of the conquest of the land.

But on the face of it, this interpretation has no place (according to the literal meaning of the verse) —for the content of this verse is Hashem’s words to Bnei Yisrael at Chorev ("Hashem our G-d spoke to us at Chorev, saying, 'Turn and journey for yourselves, etc. See, I have given before you, etc.'"), and not after the conquest of the land of Sichon and Og.

## 2

As is explicitly stated in Rashi here, in the *dibbur hamaschil* that follows, "Come and inherit"—"If they had not sent spies, they would not have needed weapons," meaning the subject is the entry into the land (as it should have been) before the sending of the spies.

And the question returns to its place: How can it be said, especially according to the literal meaning of the verse, that at Chorev Bnei Yisrael saw with their eyes—and not "based on estimation or hearsay"—that "I have given the land before you" ?

B. Also, the lengthiness of Rashi’s language, "(You see with your own eyes, etc.) I am not telling you based on estimation or hearsay," requires explanation, for seemingly:
1) "You see with your own eyes" means concrete vision; why did Rashi need to add the negation as well—"I am not telling you based on estimation or hearsay" [and like Rashi’s commentary on the verse, "See that Hashem has given you the Shabbos"—"See, with your own eyes, that Hashem in His glory is warning you about the Shabbos, for a miracle occurs every Erev Shabbos, etc.," where Rashi writes only the positive ("See with your own eyes") and does not add the language of negation]?
2) In this very matter, why did Rashi use two terms—"estimation" and "hearsay"?
[And even though the source of Rashi’s language is the Sifri on the spot—as discussed many times, it is not Rashi’s custom on the Torah to copy the *drashos* of Chazal, but rather to explain the literal meaning of the verse, as we find in Rashi on this very verse, where in the *dibbur hamaschil* that follows, even though his general interpretation originates in the Sifri, Rashi changes the language of the Sifri, as will be discussed in section D.]

C. Afterwards, Rashi quotes from the text, "Come and inherit," and explains: There is no one to challenge the matter, and you do not need war; if they had not sent spies, they would not have needed weapons.

The commentators write that Rashi’s necessity to explain it this way is—"For otherwise, how could He say to them 'Come' when it was not yet conquered before them?" (Or—"For otherwise, why do I need 'Come'? He should have written 'Inherit' only, implying that when you come, you will inherit immediately without a challenger.")

But this interpretation is not understood: We find similar language—"And you shall come and inherit the land, etc."—later in Parshas Vaeschanan, and there it is impossible to interpret it as "There is no one to challenge the matter, etc., if they had not sent, etc.," for the subject there is Moshe’s words after the decree of the spies, to the generation that entered the land (who did need weapons and wars);
And it must be that the expression "And you shall come and inherit" can be interpreted as—that you will inherit the land after its conquest (and there is no necessity to interpret it as "When you come, you will inherit immediately," without a challenger).

## 4

So that they would not even need weapons to cast fear and dread upon the inhabitants of the land [similar to what we find in Rashi later regarding Moav, that even though He forbade them "war," nevertheless, "they were afraid of them and they appeared to them while they were armed"].

But according to this, the order of Rashi’s language is puzzling: First, he says "There is no one to challenge the matter," meaning that there would not even be a verbal challenge, and it must be that Rashi’s words were stated in the style of "this, and it goes without saying that"—"There is no one to challenge the matter, and (it goes without saying that) you do not need war"; and then Rashi continues in the style of "not only this, but that"—that not only "do you not need war," but "they would not have needed (even) weapons"!

C) Seemingly, Rashi should have started with "If they had not sent spies, there would be no challenger, etc."—for in practice, the conquest of the land was through wars (and it goes without saying that there were challengers), so why does Rashi say "If they had not sent spies" only at the end of his words regarding "weapons," and not before that regarding "there is no one to challenge the matter and you do not need war" ?
And the meaning of the matter is that the fact that there were challengers and wars is not because of the sending of the spies (and Rashi does not even need to explain why Yisrael did not merit this), and only regarding weapons did Rashi need to specify that the need for them is because of the sending of the spies.

D) What is the precision of Rashi’s language, "If they had not sent spies"—for seemingly he should have said "If not for the sin of the spies" or something similar, for the very sending of the spies was "according to your opinion" of Moshe, and "at the word of Hashem" —with the permission of the Ribono shel Olam.

E. And it is possible to say the explanation for all this:
Rashi’s intent in his commentary is to resolve a general difficulty in this verse: This verse comes in continuation of what was said before, "Hashem our G-d spoke to us at Chorev, saying, 'You have stayed long enough, etc. Turn and journey for yourselves and come to the mountain of the Emori and to all its neighbors in the Aravah, etc. The land of the Canaanite and the Lebanon until the great river, the river Peras'" (meaning, the entire Eretz Yisrael to all its borders). And if so, it is puzzling: after He had already commanded them to come to the land, what does the following verse add, "See, I have given the land before you; come and inherit the land, etc."?

And even though in this verse He adds to the promise of giving the land, so that Bnei Yisrael would not be afraid to come to the land—it is still difficult, for He should have started this verse with the promise of the land —"See, I have given the land before you; come and inherit, etc. (and afterwards continue with the details of the command) Turn and journey for yourselves and come to the mountain of the Emori, etc., in the Aravah, etc., until the great river, etc."

And therefore Rashi explains that the intent of the verse is not

## 5

to simply inform them again about the promise of the giving of the land—for that was already known and famous to all of Yisrael (as Bnei Yisrael sang at the time of the splitting of the Sea, "You will bring them and plant them in the mountain of Your inheritance"), but rather there is a innovation here (regarding this promise) that they did not know until now;
And on this Rashi explains that there are two innovations here:
(A) "See, I have given"—that Bnei Yisrael see concretely ("You see with your own eyes") that Eretz Yisrael is already given to them ("I have given" is in the past tense). For until then, the promise of the land was only in the manner of "hearsay" or "estimation": First in Mitzrayim, Yisrael were promised, "I will bring you up from the affliction of Mitzrayim to the land of the Canaanite, etc.", "And I will take you out, etc., and I will bring you to the land, etc." —"And the people believed" (as they were believers, the children of believers), which is only in the category of "hearsay" (receiving words) alone; and afterwards, after the miracles of the Exodus, and especially at the time of the splitting of the Sea, when "all the inhabitants of Canaan melted, terror and dread fell upon them, etc." (as in the song of Yisrael), Yisrael could have estimated (in the way of "estimation") that Hashem would certainly place the kings of Canaan into their hands and they would inherit the land through a miracle;
Whereas now—"See, I have given, you see with your own eyes, I am not telling you based on estimation or hearsay" (as below in section V).
(B) "Come and inherit"—since "I have (already) given the land before you" (not only in the way of a promise, but) in a manner that they can see this giving "with your own eyes," it is understood that their entry into the land would be without delay ("When you come, you will inherit immediately"), "there is no challenger, etc."

V. But it is still missing an explanation: What is the connection of this promise to the journey from Mount Chorev specifically (which is why it was said here specifically), and also—what is the reason that Moshe repeated it now, after forty years?
And the explanation for this—simply:
This speech of Moshe comes in continuation of the words of rebuke at the beginning of the Parsha —"Eleven days from Chorev by way of Mount Seir until Kadesh Barnea," and as Rashi says there, "Moshe said to them, 'See what you have caused; there is no shorter way from Chorev to Kadesh Barnea than the way of Mount Seir, and even it is an eleven-day journey, and you traveled it in three days... and the Shechinah was so troubled for your sake to hasten your arrival to the land, and because you spoiled it, He turned you around, etc., forty years.'"
Meaning: In this journey from Mount Chorev, Yisrael traveled with miraculous speed (brought about because "the Shechinah was troubled for your sake"), in order to hasten their arrival to the land.
And after Moshe said his words briefly and by way of allusion, he returned afterwards to tell about this journey in detail and more—"Hashem our G-d spoke to us at Chorev, saying, 'You have stayed long enough in this mountain, turn and journey for yourselves and come, etc.'"; and this is also what he added, "See, I have given before you

## 6

the land; come and inherit, etc.," for it can be said that in this he alluded to the wonders they saw at the time of this journey, that upon seeing the miraculous speed, that the Ribono shel Olam was hastening their arrival to the land, this is concrete vision of how (from the side of the Ribono shel Olam) the land was already given to them in practice, without any delay or obstacle, and only the entry into the land in practice was missing (except that through their sins, as told in the continuation of the verses, they caused themselves to be delayed in the desert for more than another 35 years).

VII. According to the above, the details of Rashi’s language in the *dibbur hamaschil* "Come and inherit" will also be explained:
Just as in the verse "Eleven days from Chorev," Rashi emphasizes the opposite from one extreme to the other that was caused by the actions of Yisrael, that not only were they only a short way from Eretz Yisrael (an eleven-day journey to Kadesh Barnea, which is on the border of Eretz Yisrael), but furthermore, that this short way itself they traveled with miraculous speed (in three days)—and in exchange for this miraculous speed in which the Shechinah "was troubled" to bring them to the land, they were delayed in the desert until the end of forty years,
Likewise in these verses ("Hashem our G-d spoke to us at Chorev, etc., turn and journey, etc., see I have given, etc.") which were also said as a rebuke, Rashi emphasizes the greatness of the speed in the content of the matter (meaning the absence of obstacles and delays of all types) that should have been in their entry into the land.
The explanation of the matter:
"I have given the land before you" includes two things: (A) Giving ownership in law, for even though the land was initially in the possession of Canaan and they could claim that the land is theirs, (in the language of Rashi at the beginning of his commentary on the Torah) "The entire land belongs to the Ribono shel Olam; He created it and gave it to whomever was right in His eyes according to His will... He took it from them and gave it to us." (B) Giving the land in practice, by the Ribono shel Olam driving out the nations before Bnei Yisrael and they inherit it and settle in it in practice.
And these are the two matters in Rashi—(A) "There is no one to challenge the matter," (B) "And you do not need war"—that in these two matters there would be no obstacles and delays from the side of the nations:
"There is no one to challenge the matter" means—a challenge in law, that the nations would claim that according to law the land is theirs. And it is possible to say that this also includes a challenge from the side of the children of Edom [for the way to Eretz Yisrael "from Chorev... until Kadesh Barnea" is "the way of Mount Seir," and as Rashi says that "if they had not sinned, they would have passed through the way of Mount Seir to enter the land"], for they too are the children of Yitzchak; and as we find that Bnei Yisrael needed to emphasize in their mission to the king of Edom to pass through his land—"You have no right to challenge the inheritance of Eretz Yisrael, just as you did not pay the debt";

## 7

And on this, the Ribono shel Olam promised them that "See, I have given the land before you," that we see concretely that the "Shechinah was troubled for your sake to hasten your arrival to the land," so it would no longer enter the minds of the nations to challenge the ownership of Yisrael over Eretz Yisrael.
However, even if there would not be a challenge "in law," this still does not rule out "prevention and delay" from the side of the inhabitants of the land, who even if they know that Bnei Yisrael are correct in law, nevertheless would not want to leave their land and homeland, etc., for the sake of Yisrael, and would not let Yisrael enter the land.
And therefore Rashi continues, "And you do not need war"—because the nations would turn away before Bnei Yisrael (like the Girgashi who stood up and turned away before them).

VIII. And now, since the journey from Mount Chorev to Kadesh Barnea was with miraculous speed that was above nature, it is reasonable to say that also in the continuation, "See, I have given, etc., come and inherit," there is a matter of a miracle that is above nature.
And therefore Rashi did not suffice with "You do not need war," for that could also be in a natural way, that when they see Bnei Yisrael with weapons ready for war, terror and dread would fall upon them and they would flee before Bnei Yisrael, but he adds—"If they had not sent spies, they would not have needed weapons," meaning, there is no need for any action from the side of Yisrael to cast terror and dread upon the enemy, not even to show themselves armed.
Such conduct—that they do not need any action in the way of nature to drive the nations out of Eretz Yisrael—was denied to Bnei Yisrael immediately upon the sending of the spies (even before Bnei Yisrael sinned, after the spies returned from touring the land). For the very fact that Bnei Yisrael came and said, "Let us send men before us and they will spy out the land for us, etc.", showed that their opinion was that the conquest of the land would be in the way of nature (which is why they needed to send spies to know "the way by which we shall go up", etc.); and since Bnei Yisrael did not rely on the Ribono shel Olam that He would bring them to Eretz Yisrael in a manner that is above nature (and consequently there is no need for any natural action), this caused that even when the Ribono shel Olam performed miracles for them in driving the nations out of the land, they needed a natural action, at least weapons, in order to cast terror and dread upon the enemy.
And this is the intent of Moshe’s rebuke—how the conduct was turned from one extreme to the other, that in exchange for not needing any action in the ways of nature, not even "weapons," the nations challenged the inheritance of the land (and as we find that the king of Edom did not let Yisrael even pass through his land and succeeded), and furthermore that Bnei Yisrael needed to fight with the kings of Canaan in order to inherit the land.

IX. And from here is a lesson for our time now:

## 8

The verse explicitly states, "As in the days of your coming out of the land of Mitzrayim, I will show him wonders," and if so, it can be said that also the promise of the redemption of the entire land to its borders in our final redemption is similar to "as in the days of your coming out of the land of Mitzrayim," that from the side of the Ribono shel Olam it will be in a manner of "there is no one to challenge the matter and you do not need war" and not even "weapons."
But this depends on Bnei Yisrael, for when they trust as they should in the Ribono shel Olam, and certainly that they do not refrain from saying (even before the nations) that the land is the eternal inheritance of the people of Yisrael, because the Ribono shel Olam, to whom "the entire land belongs," "took it from them and gave it to us"—then it is clear that "there is no one to challenge the matter," and furthermore, that "you do not need war," G-d forbid, and it goes so far that you do not even need "weapons,"
And we will merit with the speed of "wonders" the redemption of the entire land, the building of the Beis Hamikdash and the ingathering of the exiles—all this in a manner of "I will hasten it," speedily in our days, truly.

(From the Sichos of Shabbos Parshas Matos-Masei 5737, Shabbos Parshas Devarim 5742)

## Sicha 2

### 9

A. "How can I bear by myself... provide yourselves with men who are wise, understanding, and knowledgeable..." Rashi comments: "Understanding — those who understand one thing from another. This is what Arius asked Rabbi Yosi: What is the difference between the wise and the understanding? The wise man is like a wealthy money-changer; when they bring him coins to examine, he examines them, but when they do not bring him any, he sits and wonders. The understanding man is like a merchant money-changer; when they bring him coins to examine, he examines them, and when they do not bring him any, he searches and brings from his own."

This requires understanding:
Rashi already explained the difference between a wise man and an understanding man earlier (in Parshas Tisa), on the verse "See, I have called by name Betzalel son of Uri... and I have filled him with the spirit of Elokus, with wisdom and with understanding..." — "With wisdom — that which a person hears from others and learns; and with understanding — he understands a matter from his own heart out of the things he has learned." If so, why does Rashi need to repeat and explain, "Understanding — those who understand one thing from another"?

Even if, on the face of it, this is simply because a large number of parshiyos (a large amount of time) have passed since his first explanation — just as Rashi did not feel the need to explain here again that "wise" means those who "hear from others and learn" (simply because he relies on his commentary in Parshas Tisa), he should not have had to repeat that "understanding" means "those who understand one thing from another."

Furthermore, it is puzzling: In Parshas Tisa, Rashi is content with defining the terms "wisdom" and "understanding." Why did he need to add a parable here, and not just a parable, but a lengthy one, rather than the moral in brief? Conversely, if an additional explanation via a parable was necessary, Rashi should have written it in the place where he first explained the difference between wisdom and understanding, in Parshas Tisa, rather than waiting until Parshas Devarim.

There is also a need to explain:
1) What is added and innovated in the understanding of the difference between the wise and the understanding through the parable of the "wealthy money-changer" and the "merchant money-changer"?
2) On the contrary, seemingly, the parable of the "merchant money-changer" does not align with the moral ("understanding one thing from another"). "Understanding one thing from another" means that the understanding of the new "thing" is based on the first "thing" ("from a thing") that he heard from others. Whereas, "a merchant money-changer... when they do not bring him any, he searches and brings from his own," means (a) that he trades (as the simple meaning of "merchant"), and (b) from his "own" coins.
[Only the first part of the parable, the "wealthy money-changer" who "sits and wonders when they do not bring him any," aligns with the "wise man" who only "hears from others" and cannot add from his own, and therefore "sits and wonders when they do not bring him any."]
3) When Rashi mentions the name of the author of a statement, it is because knowing the author adds explanation, or resolves a question from a "sharp" student (as has been explained many times). What is added here by announcing the author of the parable — "This is what Arius asked Rabbi Yosi"?
Moreover, Rashi not only copied the author of the parable — "Rabbi Yosi" — but also takes care to preface it with "This is what Arius asked," meaning (a) that the parable was given as an answer to a question, and (b) that the questioner was "Arius" (and Rashi did not write "a certain non-Jew" or the like).

B. It can be said that the explanation for this is:
Rashi's intent here is to resolve a simple question in the sequence of the verses here. Initially, Moshe said, "Provide yourselves with men who are wise, understanding, and knowledgeable...", but in the continuation of his words, it says only, "So I took... men who were wise and knowledgeable", and "understanding" is not mentioned, as Rashi explains that this is because "I did not find understanding ones". One must wonder : Regarding the work of the Mishkan, Moshe Rabbeinu did find those with wisdom and understanding, as is explicitly stated in Parshas Vayakhel : "And Betzalel did... and every wise-hearted man in whom Hashem had given wisdom and understanding." It is explicit that the quality of "understanding" was found, and not only that, but it was (not only in Betzalel, of whom it was said (as above) "I have filled him... with wisdom and with understanding," but also) in "every wise-hearted man." Why, then, in the appointment of judges, did he say, "I did not find understanding ones" ?

[Especially since both the work of the Mishkan and the appointment of the judges occurred — according to Rashi — in close proximity to one another, for the parsha of the appointment of judges was (as Rashi says in Parshas Yisro) the day after Yom Kippur (or at least in close proximity to it), and at that same time was the commandment regarding the work of the Mishkan (as Rashi says in Parshas Tisa).]
It is necessary to say that the "understanding" required for judges is not the same as the "understanding" required for the work of the Mishkan.
To explain the superior quality of the "understanding" required for judges compared to the understanding required for the work of the Mishkan, Rashi brings the parable of the "money-changer," to tell you that an "understanding man" here (regarding judges) "is like a merchant money-changer," as follows.

C. Explanation of the matter:
In understanding "one thing from another," there are two (general) modes: (a) When one learns a specific subject, his learning is not in a way that he understands only what he "hears from others" and nothing more, but rather, from this subject, he understands by himself additional details that he did not hear explicitly. (b) "Understanding one thing from another" in the sense of a true innovation. This means that not only does he expand his understanding of the subject he heard by adding details, but on the basis of this "thing," he arrives at the understanding of truly new things.
This is the difference between those with understanding regarding the work of the Mishkan and the "understanding" judges. Even though in the work of the Mishkan there was a need for understanding, it was not only to hear from others but also to understand "a matter from his own heart out of the things he has learned". However, this is only an expansion of the understanding of the "thing" itself, from within itself, and not the understanding of a new thing. Whereas regarding judges, there was a need for "understanding ones" who have the power to understand (from a thing) a matter that contains a true innovation.
This is Rashi's intent in bringing the parable of the "merchant money-changer", for even "when they do not bring him any, he searches and brings from his own" — which explains that the intent here is for "understanding ones" who are not satisfied with what they understand and expand upon the thing they learned (like a money-changer who profits from what "they bring him"), but rather, through this, they understand truly new things — like the parable of the "merchant money-changer" who deals with his own when "they do not bring him any," and earns new profits.
The reason for the difference between the work of the Mishkan and the appointment of judges is understood simply:
Regarding the commandment for the work of the Mishkan, it is said, "According to all that I show you, the pattern of the Mishkan and the pattern of all its vessels," "their pattern which you are shown on the mountain." This was an explicit and detailed instruction in all the particulars of their construction. Therefore, simple "understanding" is sufficient to understand from seeing their pattern how to actually make them — and there is no need for understanding in the sense of innovation, since they are not innovating anything.
Whereas regarding judges, to judge Yisrael in all kinds of situations and conditions and throughout the entire time of judging, and to judge questions and matters that were not explicitly stated in the Torah — for this, there is a need to derive one matter from another, etc., to understand and deduce from the depths of the Torah in a way of true innovation — there must be "those who understand one thing from another" in a way of innovation.
[Especially according to Rashi on the verse before this, "For Yisrael were troublesome... he sees his opponent winning the case (and then) says, 'I have witnesses to bring, I have evidence to bring, etc.'" Certainly, Yisrael needed "understanding" judges who could deal with such "troublesome" litigants who come with claims and evidence that have just been innovated.]
— And such "understanding ones" (who understand one thing from another like a "merchant money-changer") — said Moshe Rabbeinu — "I did not find."

D. Based on this, Rashi explains another wonder regarding the whole matter of "I did not find understanding ones":
It is understood and simple that Moshe's request, "Provide yourselves with men who are wise, understanding, and knowledgeable for your tribes" — to appoint judges who possess these specific qualities — was in accordance with the will of Hashem. It is explicit in Rashi that Moshe's words, "I cannot bear by myself... How can I bear by myself..." — "I do not say this from myself, but from the mouth of the Holy One, blessed be He."
[And even though Rashi says that all "seven qualities" required for a judge "Yisro said" (and not only those listed in Parshas Yisro, "And you shall choose... men of valor, etc.") — it is obvious that Moshe would not have agreed to his words to change the order of the conduct of judging the people without the consent of the Holy One, blessed be He, as is explicit in Rashi on the verse, "I will advise you... and Hashem will command you" — "The King in His might, if He commands, etc." If so, certainly, regarding the details of the matters, there was the consent of the Holy One, blessed be He.]
Since this is so, it is puzzling: How is it possible that the Holy One, blessed be He, agreed that one of the conditions for a judge is that he must be "understanding," when there were no "understanding ones" at that time among Bnei Yisrael — "I did not find understanding ones"?
According to the above, it can be said that the title "understanding ones," as mentioned, includes two types, one above the other: (a) Simple understanding ones — "those who understand one thing from another" (as was the case with the workers of the Mishkan), (b) "Understanding ones" in the ultimate perfection — "like a merchant money-changer," in a way of innovation, as explained at length above. Rashi's intent in "I did not find understanding ones" is the higher type of "understanding ones" (which Rashi elaborated on previously, as above), but it is understood and simple that a judge must be (at least) of the level of a simple "understanding one," "those who understand one thing from another" as in the first mode mentioned above — and these were the ones Moshe intended when he demanded (with the consent of the Holy One, blessed be He) "Provide... and understanding ones" — and such understanding ones indeed existed, were found, and were appointed.
[However, in order to hint that Moshe did not find understanding ones in the perfection of a "merchant money-changer," the verse later omits the detail of "understanding ones" (even though the men Moshe appointed were also "understanding," and they lacked nothing but the perfection of this, as above)].
In this way, one can also resolve the words of the Rambam on this matter: In Hilchos Sanhedrin, at the beginning of Chapter 2, the Rambam rules, "They do not appoint to the Sanhedrin... except men who are wise and understanding, distinguished in the wisdom of the Torah, etc." This is not understood: Since our Sages said that "he did not find understanding ones" even in the days of Moshe, how did the Rambam rule that "they do not appoint... except men who are wise and understanding"?
Some explain that "this 'they do not appoint' that the Rambam says is where understanding ones are found, but if they are not found, it is not an impediment," similar to the Rambam's words later in the chapter, that "a court... must have seven things in each of them, and these are wisdom, humility, etc.," which "is simple that all this is not an impediment". But from the simple language of the Rambam, "They do not appoint... except... and understanding," it implies that this law (at the beginning of the chapter) is an impediment.
According to the above, it can be said that the Rambam's intent in "They do not appoint... except... and understanding" is for simple "understanding ones," which is a condition that is an impediment (in the Sanhedrin), and such "understanding ones" existed in the days of Moshe (and also afterwards); and the Gemara's statement that in the days of Moshe "he did not find understanding ones" refers to "understanding ones... like a merchant money-changer," as above.

E. However, there is still room for a "sharp (at least) student" to ask: Since we say that the fact that the verse hints (by omitting the word "understanding") that "I did not find understanding ones" refers to "understanding ones" in the ultimate perfection — it turns out that Moshe also intended such "understanding ones" in his request, "Provide yourselves with men... and understanding ones." And the question returns to its place: How did the Holy One, blessed be He, demand this condition when He knew that "I will not find (such) understanding ones"?
It must be said that because this trait in a judge is a most necessary matter, one must strive and do everything possible to find such "understanding ones" as well. Therefore, this condition was mentioned in the Torah (in Moshe's demand), even though "I did not find understanding ones," to emphasize that the ultimate perfection in a "judge" is when he also possesses this quality and trait.
Rashi prefaced, "This is what Arius asked Rabbi Yosi" — by which he hints at two matters:
1) The parable of the "merchant money-changer" was stated by Rabbi Yosi, who was distinguished in this quality and trait of "understanding" in a special way, as our Sages said, "When Rabbi Yosi died, understanding ceased." [As we find that Rabbi Yosi's quality is "his reasoning is with him", "his explanation is with him, to give a reason for all his words, for he would resolve his words", meaning learning in a way of reason and explanation (understanding)] — and therefore, Rabbi Yosi, "according to his approach," in accordance with his quality, was the one who recognized and emphasized the greatness of the matter of "understanding" in all its perfection, which is required as a condition for the judges of Yisrael (who are worthy of the name), and that is that they should be like the "merchant money-changer" in the parable.
2) On the other hand, the aforementioned question of the "sharp student" [why did the Holy One, blessed be He, demand that there must be "understanding ones" when He knew that understanding ones would not be found], is not a desirable question — and to this Rashi hinted by prefacing that "This is what Arius asked Rabbi Yosi", meaning, this is not a question of the Sages of Yisrael, but of a non-Jew, and furthermore, that this is the question of "Arius," who was a priest. This means that such a question (which is not about understanding the words of the Torah, but) about the conduct of the Holy One, blessed be He, can cause weakness, Heaven forbid, in matters of faith, etc..
This is what Rashi informs us by bringing "This is what Arius asked Rabbi Yosi," to warn the "sharp student" against such questions, even though it is not a foolish question, and the proof is that Rabbi Yosi answered this question, and furthermore — that the question was copied and established in the Torah (for only a very small number of the questions that the sages of the nations of the world asked the sages of Yisrael are explicitly mentioned in the Torah).

F. One should add regarding the hint and the secret:
In the name "Arius" are included the letters of "Ohr" (light), and it is in the Zohar that "Ohr" refers to wisdom. Based on this, it can be said that the matter of "Arius" was — wisdom of the "other side" (as is known that "Hashem made one opposite the other", that against a matter of holiness there is its counterpart in the "other side").
And this is "what Arius asked Rabbi Yosi" — that the "wisdom" of the other side "asks" what is the greatness of the elevation in "understanding ones," to such an extent that even though the Holy One, blessed be He, knew that "understanding ones would not be found," nevertheless He demanded that this quality also be present in the judges of Yisrael?
And to this Rabbi Yosi answered — that this is understanding of holiness, as above, when it is in the ultimate perfection of "understanding one thing from another," for it is specifically in this that there is the matter of innovation — "like a merchant money-changer."
One should connect this with the time we read Parshas Devarim — Shabbos Chazon (and according to the calendar of this year — on Tisha B'Av which falls on Shabbos and is postponed):
It is known that "How can I bear by myself" is similar to and a reflection of the matter of "How does she sit alone" (and this is the hint of Shabbos Chazon, the Shabbos before Tisha B'Av, in the weekly parsha). And since the matter of "Provide yourselves with men who are wise, understanding, and knowledgeable... and I will set them at your heads" comes to rectify the situation of "How can I bear by myself," it is understood from this that in the inwardness of matters, this hints at the nullification of the exile ("How does she sit alone") through the appointment of the judges.
One can say the explanation for this:
One of the main matters that will be innovated at the time of the true and complete Redemption is — that then "Hashem your G-d will expand your border", the conquest of the three lands: the Kenite, the Kenizzite, and the Kadmonite. And it is explained in the sefarim the difference in spirituality between the conquest of the seven lands (which were conquered at the time of the first and second conquest) and the conquest of the three lands of the future to come — that the seven nations are against the seven attributes of the other side, and the three lands that will be conquered in the future to come are against the intellects of the other side, which are divided into three: wisdom, understanding, and knowledge.
That one of the innovations of the future to come is —

that then the matter of the intellects will be in ultimate perfection (and in the language of the Rambam, that then Yisrael will be "great sages and know hidden things and will attain the knowledge of their Creator" — wisdom, knowledge, and understanding (attainment)), therefore they will then conquer the intellects of the other side, the land of the Kenite, the Kenizzite, and the Kadmonite.
And this is what Rashi hints at — "This is what Arius asked Rabbi Yosi," that the conquest, resolution, and nullification of Arius's question by Rabbi Yosi is the nullification of the wisdom of the other side and its transformation into holiness — a reflection and example of the conquest of the three lands of the future to come.

(From the Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Devarim, 5746)

## Sicha 3

## 18

[Some commentators explain that Rashi was troubled by the verse: Moshe was rebuking the Jewish people for what they had done, but as for their claim that Hashem hated them, they could not be held accountable for this, as it was merely a thought that arose in their minds, and there is no room for rebuke regarding thoughts. To this, Rashi answers that Moshe Rabbeinu rebuked the Jewish people for this very thing—because they hated Hashem (as Rashi continues, "But you hate Him; a common proverb says...").

However, this interpretation is also difficult, for on the face of it, logic dictates that there is indeed room to rebuke the Jewish people for this thought—that Hashem took them out of Egypt out of hatred, Heaven forbid—just as they were deserving of rebuke when Moshe reproved them for their lack of faith in Hashem’s ability to bring them into the land of Canaan (and one does not say regarding that, "it was merely a thought that arose in their minds and there is no room for rebuke"). Moreover, their statement, "Because of Hashem’s hatred for us, He took us out..." was in addition to their general lack of faith in Hashem’s ability, which only serves to aggravate their sin, making them all the more deserving of a specific rebuke for this statement.]

B. One must also analyze the precise language of Rashi:
1) At the beginning, Rashi says, "And He loved you." What does this come to teach us? Did it enter one's mind that Hashem does not love the Jewish people, such that Rashi needed to inform us that "He loved you"?
2) The simple meaning of the proverb, "What is in your heart toward your friend is what is in his heart toward you," is the concept of "As in water, face answers to face, so is the heart of man to man"—meaning that this is the reality. But in the application of the parable, this was only in the imagination of the Jewish people; because they hated Hashem, they imagined and claimed that Hashem also hated them (which is the opposite of the truth, that "He loved you").

Commentators explain that this proverb applies only to the nature of human beings, but the nature of Hashem is not so; even though you hate Him, He loves you.

[It can be said that this explains the precision of the term "a common proverb"—for this proverb is not appropriate regarding the Divine, and is only correct regarding the nature (and character) of human beings (and what the Jewish people said, "Because of Hashem’s hatred for us," was only in their own imagination).]

Yet, Rashi’s language remains somewhat strained. After all, the intent of the proverb in this context is only to explain the Jewish people’s statement, "Because of Hashem’s hatred for us," and not to describe things as they truly are. If so, he should have emphasized that this was only according to their imagination (like "you say" and the like), and not

## 19

to phrase it in a way that implies that this is the truth.
3) It is also somewhat difficult to understand the language of the proverb, "What is in your heart toward your friend" (the one who loves you)—when the application of the parable is about the opposite of love ("you hate Him," and for that reason they say "Because of Hashem’s hatred for us").

C. In the subsequent passage, Rashi quotes, "Because of Hashem’s hatred for us He took us out of the land of Egypt," and explains: "His taking you out was for hatred. It is a parable of a king of flesh and blood who had two sons and two fields, one irrigated and one rain-fed. To the one he loves, he gives the irrigated one, and to the one he hates, he gives the rain-fed one. The land of Egypt is irrigated, for the Nile rises and waters it, while the land of Canaan is rain-fed. And He took us out of Egypt to give us the land of Canaan."

The Mizrachi asks: "Why did they interpret this verse regarding rain-fed and irrigated land? If it is to show that His taking them out was for hatred, it would be a greater sign of hatred that He took them out to deliver them into the hand of the Emori to destroy them." This remains a difficulty.

Now, many commentators have answered that Rashi’s precision is from the words, "(He took us out) from the land of Egypt"—what does this come to teach? It should have said, "Because of Hashem’s hatred, He took us out to give us into the hand of the Emori" (or even without the word "took us out"). "Rather, there are certainly two complaints, and the word 'because of the hatred' applies to both," first—that the very act of taking them out of Egypt was "because of Hashem’s hatred for us" (as per the precision of Rashi's language, "His taking you out was for hatred"), and this is "like the parable of a king, etc." (that He took us out of an irrigated land and gave us a rain-fed one), and second—"to give us into the hand of the Emori to destroy us" (and what is written "to give" is like "and to give").

But seemingly this is not sufficient, for we find similar language many times (in the complaints of the Jewish people in the desert)—"Why did You bring us up from Egypt to kill me, etc., with thirst," "Why did You bring us up from Egypt to bring us to this evil place," "Why did You bring us up from Egypt to die in the desert?" The reason for this emphasis is understood simply, because part of the Jewish people's complaint was that they wanted to return to Egypt, where (in their opinion) the situation was better [similar to what is said in Parshas Behaaloscha, "We remember the fish that we ate in Egypt for free"], and likewise in our case regarding the spies, who argued (as is explicit in Parshas Shelach) "Let us appoint a leader and return to Egypt," "It is better for us to return to Egypt." Therefore, they complained, "Because of Hashem’s hatred for us, He took us out of the land of Egypt (where 'it is good for us') to give us into the hand of the Emori, etc."

And regarding Rashi’s own words, one must understand:

## 20

1) What does the parable add to the understanding of the application? The superiority of an irrigated field over a rain-fed field is not explained any better by the parable of "a king of flesh and blood who had two sons, etc." Why did Rashi not suffice with what he wrote at the end of his words: "(His taking you out was for hatred), the land of Egypt is irrigated, etc., and the land of Canaan is rain-fed, and He took us out of Egypt to give us the land of Canaan"?
2) How does Rashi know that the Jewish people intended here to add a new claim regarding the quality of the land itself—that "the land of Egypt is irrigated, etc., and the land of Canaan is rain-fed"? Is it not possible that their intention was only to repeat their old claim, "We remember the fish that we ate in Egypt for free" (as Rashi explains, "free from the mitzvos"), unlike the land of Israel, where their eating from the fruits of the land is dependent upon the fulfillment of Torah and mitzvos, as is explicit in the verse, "If you walk in My statutes, etc., I will give your rains, etc., and the land will give its produce, etc."?

D. It can be said that the explanation for all this is:
The point of these two interpretations of Rashi is the beginning of the verse: "And you murmured in your tents," which implies that the discussion is not about an open complaint in the camp, but rather secret grievances within the tents of the Jewish people, among themselves.

Based on this, it is explained why Rashi needed to interpret "And you murmured (in your tents)" as "slander... a person who spreads gossip"—which on the face of it is difficult: above, on the verse, "How can I bear, etc., your strife," Rashi explains, "Your strife—this teaches that they were murmuring," which implies that Rashi holds the word "murmuring" does not require explanation. Why, then, does he explain the word "murmured" here?

Rather, here Rashi’s intention is to emphasize that "murmuring" is not just any strife, but specifically strife connected with speech—"slander"—and it is "a person who spreads gossip." This is "and you murmured in your tents," that they did not create open strife, but spoke slander and spread gossip among themselves in secret, while they were in their tents.

But this is puzzling: is it not explicit above in Parshas Shelach that the dispute was in the form of strife and public complaint: "And all the children of Israel complained against Moshe and against Aharon, and the entire congregation said to them, 'If only we had died, etc., and why is Hashem bringing us, etc.,' and Moshe and Aharon fell, etc., before the entire assembly of the congregation of the children of Israel"—why does the verse say here "and you murmured in your tents"?

It must be that in our Parsha, the verse adds another type of complaint of the Jewish people (which was limited only to "your tents").

It can be said that on this basis, Rashi explained (in the entry "Because of the hatred") that when they said, "Because of Hashem’s hatred for us, He took us out of the land of Egypt," the Jewish people intended

## 21

an additional complaint, that "His taking you out was for hatred" (and not as the simple reading of the verse, that the entire continuation "He took us out, etc., to give us into the hand of the Emori, etc." is one complaint)—because the claim of "to give us into the hand of the Emori to destroy us," the Jewish people said publicly (as is recounted in Parshas Shelach), and it must be that "Because of Hashem’s hatred, etc." is a different complaint that was in the manner of "you murmured," that in their tents the Jewish people complained that "His taking you out was for hatred" (and as below in section 6).

E. But the explanation is still missing: what is the difference in the essence of this complaint of the Jewish people that it was only in their tents and not publicly?
To this, Rashi elaborates in his commentary (on the entry "Because of Hashem’s hatred for us")—the reason they expressed this complaint secretly in their tents is because the foundation of this complaint is rooted in the beginning of their words, "Because of Hashem’s hatred for us"—since it is revealed and known that this claim is not true at all, for "He loved you." Therefore, they could not claim this publicly, but only whisper to one another in their tents, in the manner of "slander" and spreading gossip.

Based on this, one can also explain the continuation of Rashi, "But you hate Him; a common proverb says, 'What is in your heart toward your friend, what is in his heart toward you'"—that this matter comes as a continuation of Moshe’s general rebuke regarding their conduct in the manner of "you murmured in your tents," meaning their spreading of gossip, which indicates the opposite of the truth, etc. (which is why they hid their words in their tents)—meaning, that in addition to the fact that their entire claim, "Because of Hashem’s hatred for us," is the opposite of the truth that "He loved you," there is also the added element that they claimed that Hashem hates them (that is) while they love Him (for if they had admitted that they hate Him, there would be no room for their complaint "Because of Hashem’s hatred for us, etc.")—at the very time that the reality was the exact opposite, that "He loved you" even though "you hate Him"!

And this is also Rashi’s intention in the "common proverb"—"What is in your heart toward your friend, what is in his heart toward you," which is directed at the simple meaning of the proverb—that the grievance you have in your heart toward your "friend" (that he hates you even though you love him) is the very grievance that is "in his heart toward you" (that you hate him even though he loves you).

F. With this, one can also understand Rashi’s words in the entry "Because of Hashem’s hatred for us He took us out of the land of Egypt—His taking you out was for hatred":
If the intent of the verse is only the "hatred" in that Hashem is bringing the Jewish people to the land of Canaan "to give us into the hand of the Emori to destroy us," it is not understood why they hid this complaint in the manner of "you murmured in your tents." After all, according to their perception that it is impossible to defeat the inhabitants of the land (as they are "a people greater and taller than us, etc."), there is room to claim that bringing them into the land (which will cause, in their opinion, the delivery "into the hand of the Emori to destroy us") is a sign of hatred, Heaven forbid??

This is what Rashi explained: that the essence of the claim "Because of Hashem’s hatred for us," because of which their complaint was in the manner of "you murmured in your tents," was that "His taking you out was for hatred," and this is (as per

## 23

Rashi)—there are two types of sin: the public complaint was on the part of the spies, who caused the lack of faith in Hashem’s ability, etc., among the rest of the Jewish people, and this sin was in the manner of incitement and "compulsion" from the outside. But the Jewish people, among themselves, did not reach the point of not believing in Hashem’s ability; rather, their sin among themselves, in their tents, was in their complaint "you murmured"—that they believed that Hashem would bring them into the land of Canaan and they would inherit it, but it is like the conduct of a king with his son who "hates" him, and therefore gives him "a rain-fed field."

H. Now, Parshas Devarim is always read on the Shabbos before Tisha B'Av, and in addition, there is a special connection between Parshas Meraglim and Tisha B'Av, for the weeping of the Jewish people was "on that night," on the night of Tisha B'Av.

And in this very thing—there is a special connection between this complaint of "Because of Hashem’s hatred for us" and Tisha B'Av, according to what is explained in the sefarim that "the generation of the desert who said 'Because of Hashem’s hatred for us, etc.' caused Tisha B'Av, as it is said, 'How solitary sits the city'—the initials spell 'enmity' (eivah)." The Tzemach Tzedek explains that this "also hints that the baseless hatred that existed in the Second Temple caused the destruction of the Beis Hamikdash, etc., while 'love your neighbor as yourself' causes likewise above that the souls of the Jewish people are called brothers and friends to the Place (Hashem), and through this 'love covers all transgressions'" (meaning Hashem’s love for the Jewish people).

Based on this, it can be said—in the "inner dimension of Torah" within Rashi’s commentary—that in his commentary on "Because of Hashem’s hatred for us"—"And He loved you," Rashi comes to encourage the Jewish people and to reveal that from the outset, the Jewish people were given the power to rectify this "hatred." For even in this state of "between the straits" (Bein HaMetzarim) which comes because of a deficiency in the avodah of the Jewish people, to the extent that the natural love of the Jewish people for their Father in Heaven is in concealment, and furthermore, that they do not feel Hashem’s kindnesses, and even to the point where they put darkness for light, etc.—even in this state, Rashi testifies that "He loved you," that there is no weakness at all, Heaven forbid, in Hashem’s love for the Jewish people.

And this is not merely a hidden and concealed love (for then there would be no room to rebuke the Jewish people for not feeling this love), but a revealed love.

And "As in water, face answers to face, so is the heart of man to man" (the Higher Man and the lower man)—that this love of Hashem for the Jewish people awakens in them a revealed love for Hashem, and immediately the Jewish people do teshuvah, "and immediately they are redeemed," in the true and complete redemption, speedily in our days, literally.

(From a sicha of Shabbos Parshas Devarim, 5734)


## ואתחנן

## Sicha 1

## 26

...separation from words of Torah or idleness, which is why one must review their learning properly so as not to forget.

It could be said that this is explicitly stated in the Rambam’s *Hilchos Talmud Torah*, where he writes: "Until when is one obligated to study Torah? Until the day of his death, as it is stated, 'And lest they depart from your heart all the days of your life,' and as long as he does not engage in study, he forgets." And: "When he has grown in wisdom and no longer needs to study the Written Torah or engage constantly in the Oral Torah, he shall read the Written Torah and the traditions at set times, so that he does not forget any of the laws of the Torah." [The reason the Rambam does not count this as a separate negative commandment in his enumeration of the mitzvos is because, in his view, it is part of the general positive commandment of *Talmud Torah* as it ought to be, as explained in the *Kuntres Acharon* of the Alter Rebbe.]

According to this, the difficulty remains in place.

G. On the face of it, one could say that "forgetting the Babylonian Talmud" does not refer to forgetting "any of the laws of the Torah," but only (in the language of Rashi) the "questions and answers" found in the Babylonian Talmud. In this, there is no prohibition, similar to what is explained in the Alter Rebbe’s *Hilchos Talmud Torah* regarding "pilpul" in the reasons for the laws, etc.—"which they did not review as much... and for this reason, all the pilpul of the Tannaim was forgotten."

## 31

[And similar to the statement of the Gemara (which was cited before the ruling that "anyone who causes one thing to be forgotten from his learning transgresses a negative commandment")—"Sometimes the nullification of Torah is its foundation." As has been explained elsewhere, sometimes it is precisely this "nullification of Torah" that is the way to reach its "foundation," so that the Torah becomes established and rooted within the person.]

If so, it is clear that there is no prohibition of forgetting Torah in this.

T. And this can be explained more deeply:

The reason that the very act of forgetting words of Torah is a matter of prohibition (and one transgresses a negative commandment the moment one forgets a word of Torah) is because the words of Torah are our life and the length of our days. The very fact that one forgets and removes one thing from his learning means that he is removing "his life" from himself, and therefore he transgresses a negative commandment.

This is also the explanation for why Scripture considers one who forgets a single thing from his learning as if he has forfeited his soul. For on the face of it, it is puzzling: is forgetting one thing from the words of Torah so severe that one would forfeit his soul?! Rather, this is not in the category of a punishment, but a consequence of the forgetting. Because the words of Torah are the life of his soul, and when one thing from the words of Torah is forgotten and removed from him, that life of his soul is removed from him. It is similar to the saying of our Sages, "What difference does it make to me if one is killed entirely or if half is killed?"—he "forfeits his soul."

From this, it is also understood regarding Rabbi Zeira: since his entire intention in those fasts was so that he would be able to understand the Jerusalem Talmud as it ought to be understood, there is no separation here, Heaven forbid, from the (ways of) Torah. On the contrary, this was the way for him to be able to study and unite with the Torah in a more elevated manner, in a manner of "light."

(From the sichos of Shabbos Parshas Terumah and Shabbos Parshas Vayakhel-Pekudei, 5740)

## 33

(cleaving to His ways).
However, the Rambam writes there (at the beginning of the explanation of the mitzvah of love): "And this is that we should think and contemplate His mitzvos, His statements, and His actions, until we attain Him and derive pleasure from this attainment to the ultimate degree of pleasure—and this is the obligatory love. (And he cites the Sifri, and continues:) Behold, we have explained to you that through contemplation, attainment will come into your hand, pleasure will arrive, and love will necessarily follow." He holds that the actual performance of the mitzvah of loving Hashem is the contemplation (of His mitzvos, etc.), and it is this that brings a person to the feeling of love in the heart.

Therefore, the Rambam omitted what the Sifri says, "and cleave to His ways," because he holds that the Sifri does not mean that this is part of the essence of the mitzvah of love, but rather that it is an additional matter that comes as a result of the love. The core of the Sifri’s words is a directive to a person on how to reach the state of love, and regarding this, it says that it is through contemplation of "these words, etc.," for "through this you will recognize the One who spoke and the world came into being."

Based on this, we can explain several differences between the language of Rashi and the language of the Rambam:

a) Rashi interprets the language of the Sifri, "In what way do we love, etc."—"And what is the love?"—meaning that it refers (not to the path to arrive at love, but) to the essence of love: how and in what manner the love of the Ribono shel Olam must be. In contrast, the Rambam interprets the language of the Sifri, "How does one love the Makom," as meaning: what is the path to reach the love of the Makom? As he writes in the *Sefer HaYad*: "And what is the path to His love and fear? At the time that a person contemplates, etc., immediately he loves."

b) Rashi writes, "for through this you will recognize the Ribono shel Olam," while the Rambam in *Sefer HaMitzvos* writes according to the version, "for through this you will recognize the One who spoke and the world came into being." This is because, according to Rashi, the love in this instance is the recognition of the Ribono shel Olam through the Torah, and the cleaving to His ways is through the mitzvos. Whereas according to the Rambam, who interprets that "we should think and contemplate His mitzvos, His statements, and His actions" (as he elaborates in *Sefer HaYad* there: "What is the path to His love... at the time that a person contemplates His wonderful, great actions and creations, and sees from them His wisdom, etc."), the contemplation is of the Ribono shel Olam as the Creator of the world—"the One who spoke and the world came into being"—and not only of His mitzvos and statements, the Giver of the Torah and the Commander of the mitzvos—Hashem (in general).

c) However, according to this understanding of the Rambam’s view (that the mitzvah of love is the contemplation that brings about the feeling in the heart), one must understand several changes between his language there and his language in *Sefer HaYad*:

## 34

In *Sefer HaMitzvos* he wrote (as above), "And this is that we should think and contemplate His mitzvos, His statements, and His actions," including also the contemplation "of His mitzvos and His statements." Yet in *Sefer HaYad* he wrote, "At the time that a person contemplates His wonderful, great actions and creations, and sees from them His wisdom, which has no value and no end," mentioning only the contemplation of His actions and creations (which are the "actions" he wrote of in *Sefer HaMitzvos*), and not "His mitzvos and His statements."

The Ra'avad writes regarding the words of the Rambam in *Sefer HaYad* (mentioned above): "These words are not according to the Midrash of our Sages, who said in the Sifri that through 'these words which I command you today,' which are the mitzvos, you will recognize the Creator and love Him, and not through His wonderful actions and creations. Perhaps one might say that by performing the mitzvos, his heart will be inclined to investigate that the Mitzvah is the Creator of the world, and through this, he will set his heart to contemplate His wonderful actions and creations, and see from them His wisdom, may He be blessed, and immediately he will love Him and desire to know Him and attain Him. And this is what our Sages hinted at in the Sifri with the statement, 'for through this you will recognize the Ribono shel Olam.'"

However, from the words of the Rambam in *Sefer HaMitzvos*, which includes the contemplation of His actions along with the contemplation of His mitzvos and statements (and concludes with the language of the Sifri mentioned above), it is implied that the contemplation of His mitzvos and statements is one matter with the contemplation of His actions (and not just that through this "his heart will be inclined to investigate, etc."). If so, it is difficult: why did the Rambam not mention the contemplation of His mitzvos and statements in *Sefer HaYad*?

And furthermore: at the end of the aforementioned *halacha*, when he explains the path to His love, etc., the Rambam brings the aforementioned words of the Sifri and omits that it is through "And these words... shall be upon your heart," through which one recognizes, etc.

## 35

d. Now, there is another change in *Sefer HaYad*—an addition in the definition of the mitzvah of love—compared to what he wrote in *Sefer HaMitzvos*:

In *Sefer HaMitzvos*, only the performance of the mitzvah is explained: the contemplation and the feeling of love that comes through it. However, in *Sefer HaYad*, it is implied that the matter of the mitzvah of love is (not only the attribute and feeling in the heart, but) also that "his soul is bound in the love of Hashem" (as the Rambam’s language in *Hilchos Teshuvah*) through knowledge and attainment, as the Rambam writes, "One does not love the Ribono shel Olam except through the knowledge with which he knows Him." And also in *Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah* there, he specifies, "At the time that a person contemplates, etc., he loves, etc., and desires... to know the Great Name." From his words, it is found that the knowledge of Hashem is not only a cause that brings one to the love of Hashem, but that it is also part of the content of the mitzvah.

According to this, it can be said that the Rambam also holds (similar to the view of Rashi) that the content of the mitzvah of love is not only the feeling in the human heart, but also cleaving (*deveikus*) to the Creator. However, he holds that this cleaving is not through cleaving to His ways (as in Rashi), but through the knowledge of Hashem—the recognition of the Ribono shel Olam.

In truth, this is explicit and precise also in the language of the Rambam in *Sefer HaMitzvos* there, where he wrote, "And this is that we should think and contemplate His mitzvos, His statements, and His actions, until we attain Him and derive pleasure from this attainment to the ultimate degree of pleasure. And this is the obligatory love." This means that the definition of the mitzvah is when his contemplation brings him to attainment and he derives pleasure from this attainment, which is—the recognition of the Creator.

Based on this, it is understood that when he copies, "And the language of the Sifri: 'Since it is said, "And you shall love Hashem your G-d," I do not know how one loves, etc.' (and he concludes) 'for through this you will recognize the One who spoke and the world came into being,'" his intention is not to explain the path to reach love through contemplation (as above in section 1), but to define the content of the matter of love—"you will recognize the One who spoke and the world came into being."

And this is what he continues: "Behold, we have explained to you that through contemplation, attainment will come into your hand, pleasure will arrive, and love will necessarily follow." It can be said that the intention of "and love will necessarily follow" is not to add another action or matter, but this is the explanation of his words, "attainment will come into your hand, and pleasure will arrive."

[Based on this, it has been explained elsewhere why the Rambam elaborated in *Sefer HaYad*, in the *Sefer Halachos*—in the "great principles" regarding the matters of contemplation that lead to the love and fear of Hashem. For according to the simple reading, this is difficult: matters of contemplation are only a preparation for the mitzvah of love, so why is there such an extensive explanation of them in a book of *halacha*? The *halacha* is that one must contemplate His actions and creations, but the explanations of His wonderful actions and creations, seemingly, are not part of the *halacha* of the love of Hashem?

But according to the above, it is understood that the extensive explanation by the Rambam regarding *Maaseh Merkavah* and *Maaseh Bereishis*

## 36

in *Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah* is not only for the sake of contemplation to reach the love of Hashem through it, but that it is part of the mitzvah of the love of Hashem itself, for the content of the mitzvah is the recognition of the One who spoke and the world came into being.]

e. However, the reason for the aforementioned change has still not been explained: that in *Sefer HaMitzvos* he mentioned "and we should contemplate His mitzvos and His statements," and in *Sefer HaYad* he did not mention this contemplation.

Also, an explanation is needed: in *Sefer HaMitzvos* there, he continues that "this mitzvah also includes that we should call upon all people to His service, may He be exalted, and to believe in Him. And this is because when you love a person, you will tell of his praises, etc. So too, when you love Him, may He be exalted, in truth, when you have reached the attainment of His truth, you will undoubtedly call the foolish and the simple to know the knowledge of the truth which you have known. And the language of the Sifri: 'And you shall love Hashem your G-d'—make Him beloved upon the creatures, as your father Abraham, etc.'"

And it requires understanding why he did not bring this matter in *Sefer HaYad* when he explained the mitzvah of the love of Hashem, when in general in *Sefer HaYad* he elaborates and details more of the particulars of the laws of the mitzvah than in *Sefer HaMitzvos*, which writes only the general point and the chapter headings of the laws of the mitzvah?

f. This will be understood by first noting the precision of the Rambam’s language in *Sefer HaYad* there when he begins to explain the mitzvah of the love of Hashem: "This honored and awesome G-d, it is a mitzvah to love Him and to fear Him, as it is said, 'And you shall love Hashem your G-d,' and it is said, 'Hashem your G-d you shall fear.'"

Seemingly, there are several questions here:

a) Why did the Rambam specify "This honored and awesome G-d," and not use the usual language, "the honored and awesome Name" (as he writes immediately after (in halacha 2), "to know the Great Name")? Furthermore, this is the language of the verse, "to fear this honored and awesome Name" (as is also brought by the Rambam in *Hilchos Nesias Kapayim* (regarding the caution in saying and learning the Ineffable Name): "All this is greatness for His honored and awesome Name").

b) From the Rambam’s language, "This honored and awesome G-d, it is a mitzvah to love Him and to fear Him," it is implied that the two titles, "honored" and "awesome," correspond to the two mitzvos of "to love Him" and "to fear Him." That the G-d is "honored" brings a person to love Him (as the Rambam writes in the following halacha (as above): "At the time that a person contemplates His wonderful, great actions and creations, and sees from them His wisdom, which has no value and no end, immediately he loves, praises, and glorifies, etc."—that when one attains the greatness of the wisdom of the Ribono shel Olam, this also leads to honoring and praising and glorifying Him), and "fear" comes from the fact that the G-d is "awesome," as is simple. Seemingly, in the aforementioned verse, it is said (only) "to fear the honored and awesome Name," which implies that both titles belong to and cause fear?

g. It can be said that the explanation for all of this is:

Love and fear are two mitzvos (and the Rambam counted them as two mitzvos in the count of the mitzvos). Furthermore, they are two different attributes, even to the point of being opposites, as is understood simply: love is a movement of drawing near to the beloved, and fear and dread is a distancing from the awesome

## 37

and the nullification before Him. According to this, one must understand why the Rambam included them in *Sefer HaYad* in one stroke. Seemingly, he should have said as with all mitzvos: "It is a positive mitzvah to love Hashem, as it is said, 'And you shall love Hashem your G-d'; it is a positive mitzvah to fear Him, as it is said, 'Hashem your G-d you shall fear'."

Therefore, it must be that the Rambam in *Sefer HaYad* is speaking of a love that is not the opposite of fear, but is in the same category.

The explanation for this is based on the precision of the Rambam’s language in this halacha, which reads: "And what is the path to His love and fear? At the time that a person contemplates His wonderful, great actions and creations, and sees from them His wisdom, which has no value and no end, immediately he loves, praises, and glorifies, and desires with a great desire to know the Great Name, as David said, 'My soul thirsts for G-d, for the living G-d.'" From the fact that he adds, "and desires with a great desire to know the Great Name, as David said, 'My soul thirsts for G-d, for the living G-d,'" it is understood that the core of the matter of love is that he "desires with a great desire to know the Great Name." Since he recognizes the greatness of Hashem ("sees from them His wisdom, which has no value and no end"), he feels his distance from Hashem, and this brings him to thirst and longing.

[And similarly, he writes later at the end of the matter, after he explained the details of *Maaseh Merkavah* and *Maaseh Bereishis*: "At the time that a person contemplates these things and recognizes all the creations, etc., and sees the wisdom of the Ribono shel Olam in all the creatures, and all the creations, he adds love to the Makom, and his soul will thirst and his flesh will yearn to love the Makom, blessed be He."]

This means that the definition of love here is not a feeling of cleaving and drawing near, but desire and thirst, which is a feeling and movement of distance—that he is far from Hashem and desires Him.

This is precise in the Rambam’s language, "and desires with a great desire to know the Great Name," for the knowledge of the Great Name is something impossible for a human being composed of matter and form, as the Rambam elaborates in the continuation of the chapter regarding the status of the angels, that even the lowest level of the ten levels of angels (those who speak with the prophets and appear to them in the vision of prophecy) "also knows the Creator with a knowledge that the power of human beings composed of matter and form cannot attain and know like it." And also, "the level (among the levels of the angels) that has no level above it except the level of the G-d, blessed be He," "cannot attain the essence of the Creator as He is, but its knowledge is too short to attain and know."

[And furthermore, the Rambam elaborated in Chapter 1 that even Moshe Rabbeinu could not attain the truth of His existence as it is, and his words are: "What is this that Moshe Rabbeinu sought to attain when he said, 'Show me, I pray You, Your glory'? He sought to know the truth of the existence of the Holy One, blessed be He... the existence of the Ribono shel Olam was separated in his heart from the rest of the existents, until he knew the truth of His existence as it is. And He, blessed be He, answered him that there is no power in the knowledge of a living man who is composed of body and soul to attain the truth of this matter in its entirety. And He, blessed be He, informed him of what no man knew before him and no man knew after him, etc."]

## 38

h. Based on this, it is understood why he includes love and fear together at the beginning of the halacha, "it is a mitzvah to love Him and to fear Him," and likewise when he explained, "What is the path to His love and fear? At the time that a person contemplates, etc." (in halacha 2)—the contemplation that brings one to "immediately he loves, etc.," he continues with this: "And when he thinks about these very things, immediately he recoils backward and fears, and knows that he is a small, lowly, dark creature, standing with light, meager knowledge before the One who is perfect in knowledge." For this fear is total nullification—nullification of knowledge—and it is the very thing that comes in the continuation of the movement of distance that is felt in the thirst of love—"desires with a great desire to know the Great Name."

And this is what the Rambam emphasizes immediately at the beginning of his words, "This honored and awesome G-d, it is a mitzvah to love Him and to fear Him, as it is said, 'And you shall love Hashem your G-d,' and it is said, 'Hashem your G-d you shall fear,'" that he wrote two titles, "honored" and "awesome," regarding love and fear, even though in the verse both are said in connection to fear—"to fear the honored and awesome Name." Because the "mitzvah to love Him" in the Rambam is not entirely different in its essential nature from the mitzvah of fear, for the "love" that is in the Rambam here also belongs (in its definition and content) to the movement of nullification, etc., that is in fear, as above. Therefore, both of them belong to the two titles, "honored" and "awesome."

i. And according to this, we have learned another great innovation in the words of the Rambam: that even though the human contemplation is "of His wonderful, great actions and creations, and he sees from them His wisdom, etc."—meaning in that which a person can know and understand and attain from the Creator—nevertheless, the love is for Hashem as He is above human knowledge and attainment, which is why the love is from a distance.

It can be said that the Rambam emphasizes this by beginning, "This honored and awesome G-d, it is a mitzvah to love Him and to fear Him," and concluding, "as it is said, 'And you shall love Hashem your G-d, etc.,'" for he is precise in writing "the G-d" (*Ha-Kel*) and not "the Name" (*Ha-Shem*) or "Hashem" or the like (which is unlike what is said in the verses he brings immediately: "And you shall love Hashem your G-d," "Hashem your G-d you shall fear"):

In explaining the status of the name *Havayah* relative to the other names, the Rambam writes in his book *Moreh Nevuchim* that "all of His names, may He be exalted, found in the books are all derived from the actions, and this is something that has no hiddenness, except for one name, and that is *Yud-Ke-Vav-Ke*, which is a name unique to Him, may He be exalted. Therefore, it is called the Ineffable Name; its meaning is that it points to His essence, may He be exalted, a clear pointing that has no partnership. But the rest of His holy names point with partnership because they are derived from actions that are found for us like them."

He continues, "This name (the Ineffable Name)... points to His essence, may He be exalted, in a way that not one of the created beings is partnered in that pointing, as they said about it, our Sages, 'My name that is unique to Me.' But the rest of the names all point to attributes, not to the essence alone, but to the possessor of attributes."

He concludes, "All these derived names were not created except after the creation of the world... for they are all names that were established according to the actions found in the world. But when you contemplate His essence, stripped and abstracted from any action, He will not have a derived name (from the action) at all, but one name unique to point to His essence."

Therefore, the Rambam wrote, "This honored and awesome G-d, it is a mitzvah to love Him and to fear Him," for the name *Kel* is the first name among the names

## 39

of the Ribono shel Olam that point to the "attributes" of the Ribono shel Olam "according to the actions found in the world" [as they come in the order of the Thirteen Attributes of Mercy, for the first name that points "to the possessor of attributes" (unlike the names *Havayah*) is the name *Kel*]—

To emphasize that even though the contemplation and attainment and recognition of the Creator belongs only to the actions of the Ribono shel Olam, or (in the Rambam’s language) as the Ribono shel Olam is the "possessor of attributes," not in His essence (to which the name *Havayah* points—the Ineffable Name, the unique name, and the name of the essence), nevertheless, the love itself is for His essence, may He be exalted, the truth of His existence, which is above actions and attributes.

j. And from all of the above, the difference in the definition of the mitzvah of the love of Hashem between *Sefer HaMitzvos* and *Sefer HaYad* is understood:

In *Sefer HaMitzvos*, he explains the general matter of love, which is why he explains that it is the recognition of Hashem, which is a movement of drawing near, as he emphasizes, "until we attain Him and derive pleasure from this attainment to the ultimate degree of pleasure." Therefore, he brings that the contemplation includes also the contemplation "of His mitzvos and His statements," for they too are included in the recognition of the Creator, for they are the command and statements of the Creator.
