[And therefore, the Rambam brings there (as above) that "this mitzvah also includes that we should call upon all people to His service, may He be exalted... when you love a person, you will tell of his praises and increase them and call people to love him"—for this matter of love belongs to the cleaving to Hashem, the matter of drawing near that is in love, for when a person recognizes the Ribono shel Olam and draws near to Him, this brings him to call upon people to love Him];

Whereas in *Sefer HaYad*, his intention is to explain and detail the particulars of the matters of the love of Hashem and His fear

## 40

that come in the continuation and as a result of the knowledge of Hashem (the foundation of foundations) which were explained there in the first chapter of *Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah*. Therefore, he details this matter in the mitzvah of love (which is equal to the mitzvah of fear) that belongs to the definition of distance from the truth of His existence

[and not the contemplation of His mitzvos and statements that pertains to drawing near—the knowledge and recognition of the Ribono shel Olam, and similarly the fact that one calls upon people to His service and to love Him, which is a result of the feeling of drawing near and pleasure from the recognition of the Creator]

—that the fact that a person cannot "attain" through the matter of knowledge (for there is no power in human intellect to attain the truth of His existence as it is), as he explained at length in the first chapter—behold, through these two mitzvos, love and fear, a person "connects" to the truth of His existence, as above, that love is the thirst and desire to know the Great Name.

(From a *sicha* of Shabbos Parshas Mishpatim, 5747)


## חמשה עשר באב

## Sicha 1

## 41

### Sicha 1

The Mishnah at the end of Taanis states: "There were no days as festive for the Jewish people as the fifteenth of Av, etc." The Gemara there provides several reasons for this holiday, and concludes: "From this point onward, whoever adds [to his Torah study] will have [his life] added to, and whoever does not add, etc." Rashi explains: "From this point onward—from the fifteenth of Av and thereafter—whoever adds nights to his days to engage in Torah study will have life added to his life." The Rashbam explains that "since the nights are lengthening and the days are shortening, one must engage in his studies at night as well."

We must examine the definition of this "addition" of Torah study at night from the fifteenth of Av onward. Seemingly:
If this refers to a *yoshev ohel* (a Torah scholar) who is "obligated to engage in Torah study day and night literally," as it is written, "And you shall speak of them when you sit in your house and when you walk on the way, etc.," and it is stated, "This book of the Torah shall not depart from your mouth, and you shall meditate in it day and night"—then in his study, there is no "addition" to his obligation of the mitzvah of *talmud Torah*?

And if this refers to a businessman who fulfills his obligation by setting fixed times for study during the day and night—and there are even those who fulfill their obligation by studying one chapter in the morning and one chapter in the evening—and he wishes to be *mehader* and add to his *talmud Torah* (beyond his obligation), it is not understood: Why do we not find this concept when the days are lengthening and the nights are shortening? For if one is to add [to his study] based on the time, then when the day is longer, he should add during the day! What is the

## 42

difference whether this addition is at night (from the fifteenth of Av onward, when "the nights are lengthening and the days are shortening") or during the day (when the days are lengthening and the nights are shortening)?

On the contrary: If a special reward is granted when one adds to his study at night (which is not a time of preoccupation with business), then *kal v'chomer* he deserves a special reward when he adds to his study during the day, when according to the Torah he is exempt from *talmud Torah* due to his preoccupation with business, and he "steals" from the time he spends on his livelihood to dedicate it to Torah study.
[It is very forced to say that this refers only to a businessman who cannot, under any circumstances, "steal" time to add to his *talmud Torah* during the day (even when the days are lengthening), and therefore, an addition to Torah study can only be envisioned from the fifteenth of Av onward when the nights are lengthening.]

Furthermore: The simple language of the Gemara implies that "from this point onward... whoever adds" is a general instruction for all of Israel, both *yoshvei ohel* and businessmen (of all types).

B. The Rema brings the matter of adding to Torah study at night from the fifteenth of Av onward as a continuation of the words of the Mechaber (whose source is the Rambam) regarding the great virtue of studying Torah at night: "Whoever wishes to merit the crown of Torah should be careful with all his nights and not waste even one of them in sleep, eating, drinking, conversation, and the like, but rather in words of wisdom and *talmud Torah*." The Rema adds (from the Rambam there): "For a person does not learn most of his wisdom except at night (and he concludes), and a person should begin to study at night from the fifteenth of Av onward, etc." (This is also copied by the Alter Rebbe in his *Hilchos Talmud Torah*).

According to this, seemingly, the definition of the addition to Torah study at night from the fifteenth of Av onward does not pertain to the general obligation of *talmud Torah*—rather, it belongs to the special *segulah* of studying Torah at night. Therefore, "whoever adds will have added to him," for he deserves a special reward for engaging in Torah study specifically at night.

But according to this, it is difficult: How can it be said, "And whoever does not add will be gathered... 'his mother will bury him'" ("he will die before his time")? Is it possible to say that because he lacks the special elevation of

## 43

studying Torah at night (when one learns most of his wisdom and merits the crown of Torah), such a punishment is deserved?!

C. This can be understood by first clarifying the precise language of the Rambam (mentioned above) that through studying Torah at night, one merits the "crown of Torah":
In his *Hilchos Talmud Torah*, the Rambam brings two conditions for how to attain the "crown of Torah": 1) "Whoever is moved by his heart to fulfill this mitzvah properly and to be crowned with the crown of Torah should not divert his attention to other matters, nor should he set his heart on acquiring Torah along with wealth and honor at the same time. Such is the way of Torah: eat bread with salt, drink water in measure, sleep on the ground, live a life of hardship, and labor in Torah." 2) "Even though it is a mitzvah to study by day and by night, a person does not learn most of his wisdom except at night; therefore, whoever wishes to merit the crown of Torah should be careful with all his nights, etc."

It should be noted that initially, the Rambam stated "to be crowned with the crown of Torah," whereas regarding engaging in Torah study at night, he wrote "whoever wishes to merit the crown of Torah."
The explanation for this is as follows:
Within the content of "crown of Torah" there are two matters: a) The crown that is in the Torah itself, meaning the exalted and special part of the Torah (as we find that specifically the *halachos* of the Torah are called "the crown of Torah"). b) The fact that the Torah is a crown for the person who studies it.

Now, although in general the Rambam's intent in this chapter is for the second aspect—that the Torah crowns the person who studies it (as explained elsewhere)—it can be said that in the sweetness of his language, the Rambam also hints at the first matter of the crown of Torah, namely, an exalted level within the Torah itself.
This is the difference between the two *halachos* mentioned above:
In the first *halachah*, "Whoever is moved by his heart to fulfill this mitzvah properly and to be crowned with the crown of Torah," the intent is to the definition of "crown of Torah" in the second sense mentioned above, where the Torah becomes his entire reality (and this is the meaning that the person is "crowned with the crown of Torah"). As the Rambam continues, "nor should he set his heart on acquiring Torah along with wealth and honor at the same time"—for then his words of Torah are like property he has acquired (to the point that "the Torah is his") which can be separated from the reality of the person studying and the words of Torah he has learned, since he is a separate reality who has "property"—rather, he should be crowned (surrounded) by words of Torah in such a way that the Torah is unified with his reality.

This is not the case with what he wrote at the end of the chapter, "A person does not learn most of his wisdom except at night; therefore, whoever wishes to merit the crown of Torah should be careful with all his nights, etc."—his intent is not that the Torah should be "his" crown, but "to merit" an exalted matter within the Torah itself, the "crown of Torah." Therefore, he changes his language and writes (a) "to merit," (b) "the crown of Torah"—which indicates that he is meriting something, and that thing is "the crown of Torah," a crown of the Torah [unlike before, where he wrote (a) "to be crowned," (b) "with the crown of Torah"—which emphasizes the action upon the person studying, that the Torah crowns him].

D. It can be said that the Rambam's intent here with "crown of Torah" is the essence and core of Torah (like a "crown" in its literal sense, which is something above the head), meaning not that the person understands and grasps the Torah, but the *dveikus* with the Giver of the Torah.
As explained at length in the *Bach*, the essence of engaging in Torah study is "that we should be engaged in Torah so that our soul may be empowered by the essence, spirituality, and holiness of the source from which the Torah originates... And if they were to engage in Torah with this intent, they would be a chariot and a sanctuary for His *Shechinah*, may He be blessed, for the *Shechinah* would literally be within them, for they are the sanctuary of Hashem, and the *Shechinah* would literally establish its dwelling within them." He explains there that this is also the content of the blessing of the Torah, "to thank Him for giving the Torah to His people Israel so that they may be attached to its holiness and to the *Shechinah*, may He be blessed. This is the intent of the blessing 'Who chose us from all the nations and gave us His holy Torah,' His precious vessel with which He would delight every day, so that our soul may be attached to the essence of the Torah's holiness and spirituality, and to bring the *Shechinah* down within us, etc."

According to this, the precision of the Rambam's language in this *halachah* is understood: "A person does not learn most of his wisdom... and he should not waste even one of them... except in *talmud Torah* and words of wisdom" (and in the *Tur* and *Shulchan Aruch* they placed "words of wisdom" before "*talmud Torah*")—for seemingly, what do "words of wisdom" have to do with this? Are we not talking about the wisdom of Torah?
Rather, the Rambam's intent in "words of wisdom" is the study of matters of *Pardes* (which are explained in the first four chapters of *Sefer HaYad*), which are called "words of wisdom" because they are

## 45

the deepest matters in the Torah (and as explained in the Rambam there, the great depth of those matters)—for through this study one merits the "crown of Torah." Because in order to feel the "spirituality and holiness of the source from which the Torah originates" (which is the "crown of Torah" as mentioned above), it is through the study of the matters of *Pardes* and the wisdom of Truth, in which the greatness of the Creator is discussed, etc.
[According to this, what the Rambam says, that the primary study at night is in "words of wisdom" ("most of his wisdom")—meaning matters of *Pardes* as mentioned—aligns with what is stated in the writings of the Arizal (and brought in the *poskim*) that the primary study of Torah at night is in *Aggadah* and the wisdom of Truth.]

E. According to this, the connection between "meriting the crown of Torah" and studying specifically at night is also understood:
The fact that one merits the "crown of Torah" by studying Torah at night is not (only) because at night one can dedicate himself more to study with the proper composure, when his study settles within him (similar to what is said in the *Midrash* regarding Moshe), but also and primarily because at night there is a special *segulah* that his study will be in a manner that connects (in addition to the wisdom of the Torah) to His *Shechinah*, may He be blessed.

## 46

[Therefore, the Rambam continues, "The Sages said, 'There is no song of Torah except at night, as it is said, Arise, cry out in the night,' and 'Whoever engages in Torah at night, a thread of kindness, etc.,'" to emphasize that the intent is not only the acquisition of words of Torah and composure in the wisdom of Torah, but to the "inner dimension of Torah," which is the "song of Torah," and it is this which causes a "thread of kindness" to be upon him.]

This is because *dveikus* with the Giver of the Torah is connected with studying Torah from a place of humility and *bitul*, for only in such study is true *dveikus* to the holiness of the Torah possible. The night is a time capable of arousing within oneself a feeling of humility and lowliness of spirit (and therefore it is a time for *cheshbon hanefesh* during the *Krias Shema* of the bed, *Tikkun Chatzos*, etc.).
Even though *bitul* is a prerequisite for every matter of Torah study, as explained by the Rambam here that "it is not found among the arrogant... but among the crushed and lowly of spirit," which is a preface to study, "Words of Torah do not endure in one who relaxes himself over them, nor in those who learn amidst luxury, etc., but in one who kills himself over them and constantly afflicts his body and does not give sleep to his eyes nor slumber to his eyelids"—all these are things connected with *bitul* and breaking the body and the like; however, to merit the "crown of Torah," the study itself must be from a place of *bitul*, humility, and lowliness of spirit, to connect and attach to the holiness of the Torah, and this is specifically through studying Torah at night (as mentioned above).

F. This is also the content of the reward of "whoever adds will have added to him"—"he will add life to his life"; and likewise, on the other hand, the punishment of "whoever does not add, etc.":
The addition of studying Torah at night (in addition to the addition in quantity, that he adds more time of Torah study) is primarily an addition in quality—that through this he merits the "crown of Torah," which is to attach to the Giver of the Torah. Therefore, this addition leads to "he will add life to his life," where the intent is—in addition to the addition in life literally—also an addition in quality,

## 47

more exalted life, similar to what is said, "He gives life to the living, He will give you life," meaning more exalted life within the life of this world, and similar to what is explained in *Rabbenu Gershom* that "he adds days to the World to Come," where it can be said that the intent is not that the addition is only to the life of the World to Come, but that the days of the World to Come exist within this world, while he is a soul in a body, as the Sages said, "You shall see your world in your lifetime."

And on the other hand—"whoever does not add will be gathered, etc.," because when he does not add to his Torah study at night, it indicates that "meriting the crown of Torah" does not concern him, and that his intent in Torah study is for the sake of grasping the wisdom of the Torah, and not that it is the wisdom of the Holy One, blessed be He, the holiness of the Torah, which is its true essence and definition, and for this reason, it should be the primary engagement of a person's Torah study. Without this, he lacks the essential vitality of a Jewish person, which is the essence of the holiness of the Torah. Therefore, he lacks this "life."
[Similar to the words of the *Bach* mentioned above in explaining the words of the Sages, "For what reason was the land destroyed? Because they did not bless the Torah first," where "it is very puzzling why such a decree went forth from before Him to punish them with such a great and high punishment for not blessing the Torah first, which is seemingly a light transgression." He explains that "they abandoned My Torah and did not walk in it—meaning the beginning of walking in the spirituality of the Torah from level to level so that the soul may attach to the essence of the holiness of the Torah, they did not walk in it... to engage in Torah for its own sake ('but rather for the sake of material things for their own benefit, to know the laws for the sake of business, and also to be arrogant to show their wisdom, and they did not intend to empower themselves and attach to the holiness and spirituality of the Torah and to draw the *Shechinah* down below to the earth')... they were punished that the *Shechinah* departed from the lower realms, and then the land was destroyed, it was scorched like a wilderness without a passerby," meaning it was ruined and remained material without the passing of the holiness of the *Shechinah* there, for the *Shechinah* departed completely from the land and ascended upward.]

G. According to all the above, the connection between the addition of Torah study at night and the holiday of the fifteenth of Av must also be explained.
The simple reason why "from this point onward" we add to Torah study at night is because from then on the nights lengthen and the days shorten. But according to this, it does not pertain to the content of the day (and the holiday) of the fifteenth of Av itself, but is something that begins from the fifteenth of Av onward. It can be said that it is logical that there is a connection between the addition to *talmud Torah* at night that begins from the fifteenth of Av and the content of the holiday.

## 49

in a manner that is decreasing? For it is a great principle that the service of Hashem must be in a manner of "ascending in holiness and not descending," Heaven forbid!
We must say that there is a difference between when there is no fullness of the moon in the first half of the month and the second half of the month: In the first half of the month, the lack of fullness of the moon indicates a deficiency, meaning that the light of the moon has not yet been completed, and one must elevate and add to the light; but in the second half of the month, when there is no fullness of the moon, it does not indicate a deficiency, Heaven forbid, but on the contrary—it has a virtue.

I. This can be understood according to the words of the Gemara regarding the general matter of the moon's diminution, "Go and diminish yourself," and its parallel in the service of the Jewish people—"For you are the fewest," "diminish yourselves," the matter of *bitul*.
The matter is as follows:
When the service of the Jewish people is in a manner where the moon is in its fullness, meaning that his light shines openly and with strength, both regarding himself and regarding the influence upon others (similar to the moon, whose purpose is "to shine upon the earth")—it is possible that a person may come to a feeling of *yeshus* (self-importance), knowing the great virtue of the service through which he shines upon the entire world; therefore, in continuation and together with this, the service must be in the matter of *bitul* ("Go and diminish yourself").
Meaning: Regarding the essence of the service, both regarding himself and regarding others—certainly, one must not diminish, Heaven forbid, in the matter of light; on the contrary, one must add and elevate in all matters of light and holiness. The change is only regarding the feeling of the person serving, for since he is the one acting and shining, he can come to a feeling of *yeshus*—therefore, regarding this feeling, the service must be "Go and diminish yourself," the *bitul*.
This is the fixed order in the service of man, that upon reaching the perfection of the action in the service of Hashem in light and revelation, he must immediately begin the service of "decreasing," the service of diminishing and nullifying the feeling of his own reality and the feeling of the virtue of his service, until the feeling is nullified completely, similar to the moon, which every month after the moon is in its fullness on the fifteenth of the month, the order of "decreasing" in revelation begins, until the light of the moon is hidden completely at the moment before the *molad*.
According to this, it is understood that even in the second half of the month, when the light of the moon is decreasing, the service is in a manner of "increasing"—an addition in *bitul*, for the feeling is increasingly diminished (nullified), until the ultimate *bitul*.

J. Now, since we are commanded to "ascend in holiness" always, we must say that the service that comes after the state of the fifteenth of the month, when the moon is in its fullness, is in continuation and addition to the ascent in the service of the first half of the month. Meaning: Not only is the service of the second half of the month in a manner of "increasing" (addition in the matter of *bitul*), but furthermore, the service in the second half of the month is more exalted than that of the first half of the month.
The matter is as follows:
The service in the first half of the month is an addition in the matter of light, and "light" is

## 50

a reality; whereas the service of the second half of the month is—an addition in *bitul*, in the negation of reality, through which one arrives at an elevation that is incomparable to the light—"You take Me," "taking" the "Luminary."
This is the matter of the *molad* of the moon at *Rosh Chodesh*—that through the service of *bitul* in the second half of the month until the ultimate *bitul* and the hiding of the moon completely (at the moment before the *molad*), then the matter of the "unification of the sun and the moon" is accomplished, the unification with the "Luminary," whose virtue is incomparable to the virtue of the light.
[For example—the difference between a student at a distance from his teacher who interprets his words and transmits them to others, and when he stands before his teacher and receives his Torah from him; it is understood that when he is before his teacher, he is completely *batel* so that he can receive his teacher's Torah, and then he is at the most exalted level.]

K. According to this, the connection of "from this point onward" we add to Torah study at night to the fifteenth of Av, in which the moon is in its fullness, is understood:
The content of the matter of studying Torah at night is to merit the "crown of Torah" as mentioned above, meaning to arrive at the "Luminary" in the Torah, the holiness of the Torah and the *Shechinah* in the Torah (as explained at length above), which is incomparably higher than the "light" of the Torah. Arriving at this is specifically through studying Torah at night, from a place of night, lowliness of spirit, and humility, the matter of *bitul*.
Therefore, specifically "from this point onward"—after the fullness of the moon of the fifteenth of Av, a more exalted perfection begins, the study of Torah at night as mentioned above, which comes through the matter literally, through which one arrives at the "Luminary" in the Torah.
It can be said that this is also the connection between the month of Av and its continuation, the month of Elul—for in the month of Elul is the time of the "King in the field," as the well-known parable of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe, that in Elul the Holy One, blessed be He, receives every one of the Jewish people with a pleasant countenance, like "a king who, before he comes to the city, the people of the city go out to meet him and receive his face in the field"—for the service of *bitul* in the second half of the month of Menachem Av (in which, and in its conclusion specifically, we bless the month of Elul)—the ultimate perfection in the matter of *bitul*, a *bitul* that comes after the ultimate ascent of "the moon in its fullness" as it is in the month of Menachem Av (as mentioned above)—is the "vessel" for the revelation of the "King in the field" in the month of Elul, similar to what is written, "I dwell in the high and holy place, and with him who is of a contrite and humble spirit"; "And to this one I will look, to the poor and broken in spirit" specifically,
And the Holy One, blessed be He, receives every one of the Jewish people "with a pleasant countenance, and shows a smiling face to all," and fulfills all their requests and the desires of their hearts for good and for blessing, for a good writing and sealing, and they are written and sealed immediately for life, and in a manner of "adding life to his life," until—the eternal and true life in the true and complete Redemption through Mashiach Tzidkeinu, speedily in our days, literally.

(From the Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Va'eschanan, 5745)


## עקב

## Sicha 2

## 60

...kofer” — meaning, the matter of teshuvah is in the category of taking a “bribe.”

Now, according to the aforementioned Midrash, we can understand the novelty in the verse before us, that “Hashem your G-d... takes no bribe” — for although we find the concept of taking a bribe with the Ribono shel Olam, this is solely regarding the matter of teshuvah. However, Hashem does not accept any other matter — even matters of Torah and mitzvos — as a “bribe.” As the Rambam writes (in the Commentary on the Mishnah, “that before Him there is no... nor taking of a bribe”): “He does not take a bribe of good deeds, such as if a person performs a thousand good deeds and one sin, that the Almighty should forgive that sin due to the multitude of his good deeds, and subtract one or more good deeds from his thousand; rather, he is punished for that one sin, and He rewards him for all those good deeds.”

Thus, we require an explanation (in addition to the above, as to how it is possible that Hashem, the G-d of truth, takes a bribe) — since taking a bribe is forbidden, what is the unique aspect of teshuvah for which this prohibition was “permitted,” and “the Ribono shel Olam takes (a bribe) from the wicked in this world: teshuvah and good deeds”?

B. Now, in Midrash Shmuel (on the aforementioned Mishnah), he explains the difference between the “bribe” of teshuvah and the “bribe” of mitzvos in general (as mentioned in the Rambam there): “The matter of teshuvah is one thing, and the matter of other mitzvos is another. For the reason that teshuvah atones is because when a person regrets the sin... through this teshuvah he cleanses the leprous plague of the sin. But one who has thousands of myriads of mitzvos and a minority of sins... and did not confess them, certainly those sins remain in their place, and how can they be removed without being removed?”

But according to this, it is not understood from the other side — since through teshuvah the sin is cleansed and nullified, why is (the atonement of sins through) teshuvah called by the name “bribe,” which implies giving a benefit in order to sway the judgment — for here, there is no (sin — the cause of the) judgment?

Seemingly, one could say based on what our Sages said, that through teshuvah out of ahavah, “willful sins become for him like merits.” This means that not only does teshuvah out of ahavah nullify the sin entirely (to the point that “the sin is uprooted from its inception”), but it transforms the sin into a merit — and this matter (that the sin should be transformed into a merit) has no place according to the law of the Torah.

[It can be said that this is one of the explanations in the words of our Sages : “They asked of the Torah: ‘A sinner, what is his punishment?’ She said to them: ‘He shall bring a guilt offering and be atoned.’ They asked of the Ribono shel Olam: ‘A sinner, what is his punishment?’ He said to them: ‘He shall do teshuvah and be atoned.’” The novelty in the answer of the Ribono shel Olam, “and he shall be atoned,” is that (through teshuvah out of ahavah) “willful sins become for him like merits”, and this has no place according to the Torah (in and of itself). Therefore, the “Torah” (in and of itself) could not give this advice, and only the Ribono shel Olam innovated that “he shall do teshuvah and be atoned”].

Therefore, teshuvah is called by the name “bribe,” because by performing teshuvah (out of ahavah), the Ribono shel Olam changes the (laws of the) Torah (— beyond the letter of the law), and makes the willful sins into merits.

However, it is forced to interpret the intent of the Midrash this way, because:
a) “Willful sins become for him like merits” is achieved only through the most elevated teshuvah, teshuvah out of ahavah (rabbah), and the Midrash implies that it refers to all forms of teshuvah.
b) The novelty of “willful sins become for him like merits” is not relevant to the atonement of the sin (because for that, the erasure and uprooting of the sin suffices) — and from the simple language of the Midrash (“He takes... until the gates of teshuvah are open, do teshuvah”), it is implied that it is speaking of this matter in teshuvah — which brings the atonement of the sin.

C. Therefore, it seems to me the opposite: the “bribe” in teshuvah is that there is no need for a “superior teshuvah” in its perfection for a complete atonement for the sin. Rather, any act of teshuvah effects “atonement and forgiveness of the sin, that he is forgiven entirely for what he transgressed against the King’s commandments when he did complete teshuvah, and they do not mention to him a thing or half a thing on the Day of Judgment to punish him for it, G-d forbid, in the World to Come, and he is released entirely from the judgment in the World to Come.”

That is to say: the erasure and uprooting of the sin entirely (to the point that no trace of it remains at all) is only through teshuvah out of ahavah. Therefore, there is room from the perspective of the letter of the law to say that as long as he has not reached this state, his sin is not erased, and it is impossible for his sin to be atoned for entirely. And this is the novelty in the “bribe” of teshuvah: that since the person has fulfilled the mitzvah of teshuvah according to the laws of the Torah — meaning that he resolved in his heart to abandon the sin and not do it again, with confession and a request for forgiveness — his sin is forgiven entirely.

And yet, this taking of a “bribe” is not contrary to the Torah’s command, “And you shall take no bribe” — for the fact that the atonement of teshuvah is in the category of a “bribe” is only regarding human beings, for a judge...

## 64

And according to the above, it can be said: The things that nullify a decree (and likewise the punishment for a sin) are divided into two types :

The three matters of “teshuvah, tefillah, and tzedakah” bring atonement (and nullification of the decree) by nullifying the *cheftza* (the object/essence) of the sin. As the Rambam phrases it, that “he distances himself greatly from the thing he sinned in.” This means that even after he has resolved in his heart not to do it again, he adds to distance himself “greatly from the thing he sinned in,” and the more he adds in this, his teshuvah is more superior and nullifies more of the trace of the sin.

[However, this alone is not enough, and in order to nullify the punishment entirely, one also needs tefillah and tzedakah. It can be said that this is similar to how, at the time the Beis Hamikdash was standing, they also needed a korban for atonement, which is like a “counselor who enters” before the King to appease Him.]

Whereas “changing one’s name” is an entirely different matter: the *baal teshuvah* becomes an entirely new existence, to the point that he becomes a different person, and consequently, there is no one here to punish, as mentioned above.

And this is also the reason why there must also be a “change of deeds” — and “he changes all his deeds for the good” — because only then does no trace remain of his previous existence (which is evident in his previous deeds), and he becomes an entirely new existence; he is a “different” person.

VI. According to all the above, one must also explain the reason why the atonement through teshuvah is not a “bribe” in a negative sense (even if it is not a “superior teshuvah” that erases and uproots the sin entirely). This is preceded by what is explained elsewhere, that the fact that teshuvah transforms the person into a new existence also touches upon the actual halacha — for through teshuvah, the person is transformed from a wicked person (who is disqualified from testimony, etc.) into a kosher person (“one who performs the deeds of your people”), to the point that through a thought of teshuvah in the heart, he is called a “completely righteous person”.

It is explained in this that even though this change (— that the person is transformed from wicked to righteous) is achieved through the fulfillment of the “mitzvah of teshuvah from the Torah, which is only the abandonment of the sin” [and to the point that even a thought of teshuvah in the heart suffices (as mentioned above) without verbal confession, as explained by the later authorities that even if his teshuvah does not help him to have his sin atoned, nevertheless he becomes a completely righteous person through “abandonment of the sin alone”], whereas a “change of name” to nullify a decree from a person, etc., must be in a manner that “he changes his deeds...

## 66

...from the judgment in the World to Come” — because the existence of the sinner has been nullified, and there is no one here to punish, as mentioned above.

VIII. According to this, one must explain another precision in the language of the aforementioned Midrash (Section A) — “And what is the bribe... teshuvah and good deeds.” For seemingly, what are “good deeds” doing here? After all, on the contrary, the Ribono shel Olam does not accept a bribe of “good deeds” (as mentioned above from the Rambam), but only of teshuvah?

[To add, that in Midrash Shocher Tov, the version is “teshuvah, good deeds, and tefillah” (or “teshuvah, tefillah, and tzedakah”), and it concludes, “until the gates of tefillah are open, do tefillah and teshuvah.”]

In Midrash Shmuel, he explains that “what he mentioned good deeds with teshuvah, the intent is to let us know that after the teshuvah, he will not return and repeat the sin... and they said to him, ‘This is like one who immerses while holding a creeping thing in his hand.’ And even according to the version of Shocher Tov, the intent of what he said ‘tefillah’ is not regarding the mitzvah of tefillah... but the intent is on the matter of tefillah and teshuvah. The true teshuvah is that he will pray to his G-d, etc., and ask from Him, may He be blessed, for forgiveness, pardon, and atonement, etc.”

But seemingly, this is not a sufficient answer — for what is the practical difference in the Midrash here (regarding the matter that teshuvah is a “bribe”) to emphasize that teshuvah without good deeds (and tefillah) is not correct teshuvah? Surely this matter belongs in the Laws of Teshuvah or in Midrashim that come to explain the essence of the matter of teshuvah.

And according to the above, it can be said that this relates to the matter of the “bribe” in teshuvah. For according to what we explained above, it is understood that the fact that teshuvah does not have the power (simply) to effect complete forgiveness — even though he has changed from wicked to righteous — is because this change has not yet penetrated all the details of the person’s affairs.

And this is what the Midrash is saying: that when a person’s teshuvah acts in such a way that the person also performs good deeds (and likewise engages in tefillah), in a manner that the point of teshuvah in the heart has begun to penetrate into the person’s deeds — this is a “bribe” that is accepted as if a “change of person” had been achieved in perfection (that “he changes all his deeds for the good”) and “he is forgiven entirely.”

(From the Sicha of 15 Av, 5742)

## Sicha 3

## 68

Simply put, it can be said that [the Yalkut] did not bring the other sources because this is the Midrash for this specific verse.

It can also be said, quite simply, that the Yerushalmi does not accept the Babylonian Talmud’s rejection of the teaching, "Always say to yourself, it literally means 'adornment' (hadras)." For we find in many places that we derive teachings from "Do not read it as such, but rather as such," while also acknowledging the simple meaning of the verse. Both are valid: the simple interpretation and the interpretation based on the "Do not read it as such" [and on the contrary, the rule is well-known that the teaching of "Do not read it as such" is not intended to uproot the simple meaning of the text, but to add to it]. Likewise in our case: even though we learn from "Worship Hashem in the beauty (hadras) of holiness" that one must adorn oneself with his garments ("literally adornment"), it is possible to derive from it also "Do not read it as 'hadras' (beauty), but as 'charadas' (awe/trembling)".

However, according to this, it remains difficult to understand: what is the reasoning of the Babylonian Talmud in rejecting the teaching derived from "Worship Hashem in the beauty of holiness"?

B. This can be understood by first addressing the following question, which seemingly points in the opposite direction:

Since there is an explicit verse, "Serve Hashem with fear," why did not all the Sages derive the teaching from this explicit verse (to the point that Rabbi Yehuda bar Levi was forced to say "Do not read it as such," as it is already explicit in the verse ("in beauty, but rather in trembling"))?

Therefore, it seems to me that the basis of the dispute regarding where we derive this law is not because they disagree on the interpretation of these verses, but rather that they disagree on the content of the "koved rosh" (seriousness/gravity) that must precede prayer. Each one selected the verse that speaks to the content of the "koved rosh" that, according to his opinion, is required.

[And this is the reason that the Babylonian Talmud concludes in accordance with Rabbi Nachman bar Yitzchak, because it holds that the definition of "koved rosh" is that which is emphasized in the verse, "Serve Hashem with fear." As for the Yerushalmi, there is no definitive conclusion, and there is room for both opinions (either that the definition of "koved rosh" is the content in "Worship... in trembling of holiness," or the content in "Serve Hashem with fear"). Whereas for the Yalkut, there is room to decide in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda bar Levi.]

Explanation of the matter:

## 69

Regarding the phrase, "One should not stand... except with koved rosh," Rashi writes, "Koved rosh — submission", meaning humility and bitul.

Based on this, the reason why the other Sages were not comfortable bringing the verse "Serve Hashem with fear" (like Rabbi Nachman bar Yitzchak) is explained: because the quality of fear (in a general sense) is not exactly the same as the concept of "submission" and humility. Although there is a connection between them and they are often interdependent—for one who fears another generally feels submissive before him —it is not necessarily so. For it is possible to have a state of fear and dread (because the other is stronger than him) without a feeling of submission and humility (except that due to his fear of him, he submits to him in practice).

Based on this, the general difference between the aforementioned verses (from which we learn that "one should not stand... except with koved rosh") is understood:

The first Sage brings the verse, "And she was bitter of soul"—because in this, the feeling of submission and humility is emphasized, for one who is in bitterness is also in a state of submission and humility.

However, the simple meaning of this verse is that Chana was "bitter of soul" because of her personal situation, that she had no children, and this has no connection to Hashem.

This is not the case with the verse, "And I... will bow toward Your holy sanctuary in Your fear," where the prayer is described with the term "I will bow," for bowing is toward Hashem, a bitul to Hashem. And furthermore, this bowing is "in Your fear", which is not just an act of bowing alone, but is a result of the fear of Hashem.

That is to say: this "fear" is not fear of punishment or even fear of sin (where one fears not only the punishment, but the sin itself, to act against His will)—rather, it is "Your fear," the fear of Hashem, a fear of shame, in recognizing the greatness of the Almighty.

## 70

And in the words of the Rambam : "At the time that he contemplates (the greatness of the Almighty)... he immediately recoils, fears, and knows that he is a small, lowly, and dark creature, standing with limited and meager knowledge before the One who is perfect in knowledge." "When a person contemplates these things... he will fear and dread his own lowliness, poverty, and insignificance... and he will find himself like a vessel full of shame and disgrace, empty and lacking." And this is the concept of "koved rosh" — submission (and humility) before Him.

[However, in this very point, Rabbi Yehuda bar Levi adds the teaching from the verse, "Worship Hashem in the beauty of holiness" — because in this, this level of fear and bitul is emphasized even more, as will be explained in section D.]

But Rabbi Nachman bar Yitzchak holds that it is sufficient before prayer to have submission in practice toward Hashem, which also comes through fear in a general sense (at whatever level it may be), and therefore he brings the verse, "Serve Hashem with fear."

C. It can be said that the basis of their dispute is the definition of "koved rosh" in the context of prayer, which can be interpreted in several ways (in the language of Rogatchover : as an active agent, an action, or a passive state):

1) "Koved rosh" regarding the person praying (the "active agent"): that in order for a person to be in a state where he can pray properly before Hashem, he must awaken within himself the feeling of submission, "koved rosh." In the language of the Rishonim, prayer must be "with submission and composure of mind, to intend the words that come out of his mouth."

2) "Koved rosh" regarding the object of prayer (the "action"): that the concept of prayer requires that the person be in a state of "koved rosh".

3) "Koved rosh" regarding the manner in which the prayer is received by Hashem (the "passive state," as it were): that in order for the prayer to be desirable before Him, and for Him to fulfill the request of the one praying, the person must be in a state of "koved rosh."

And in accordance with this is the distinction between the (aforementioned) verses from which we learn this:

The reason Rabbi Nachman bar Yitzchak holds that for the matter of "koved rosh," "fear" (in the verse "Serve Hashem with fear") is sufficient, is because he holds that "koved rosh" comes to prepare the person so that he will be in the state of composure of mind required for prayer. For this, it is sufficient that he feels the fear of Hashem at any level, because the movement of fear (even if it is only fear of punishment or fear of sin) acts upon the person so that when he stands before Hashem to ask for his needs from Him, he will clear himself of his other thoughts and pray with composure of mind and the desired intention.

The Sage who derives it from the verse, "And she was bitter of

## 71

soul," in which the feeling of bitterness is emphasized (as above)—holds that "koved rosh" acts to ensure that his prayer will be accepted by Hashem, as it is explicit in the verse, "And to this one I will look, to the poor and broken in spirit" (and as explained in many places that Hashem listens to the prayer of the poor).

Therefore, it makes no difference what the cause of the bitterness is—like in the verse "And she was bitter of soul," where the bitterness came because she had no children—because the feeling of humility causes Hashem to listen to the prayer and fulfill the requests.

Whereas for the Sage who derives it from the verse, "I will bow... in Your fear" (or from "Worship Hashem in the beauty of holiness")—which points to fear out of shame and bitul toward Hashem—this concerns the object of prayer itself. For the act of prayer requires that a person "see himself as if he is standing before the Shechinah", without which there is no object of prayer. And this feeling (that he "is standing before the Shechinah") is connected to the fact that the person is in a state of submission and humility, in a way that he is nullified before the majesty of the Almighty (before whom he stands).

D. With this, we can explain the reason why Rabbi Yehuda bar Levi was not satisfied with the verse, "And I... will bow... in Your fear," but rather derived it from, "Worship Hashem in the beauty of holiness, do not read it as 'beauty' (hadras) but as 'trembling' (charadas)." It can be said that his reason is primarily due to the content of the verse—that specifically in this verse, bitul to Hashem is emphasized to the greatest degree:

(a) "Charada" (trembling) is not fear in a general sense, but a bitul that penetrates his entire existence until it causes trembling. (b) "Trembling of holiness"—meaning that the person feels the holiness of Hashem, which brings him to trembling, a fear of shame as mentioned above [whereas in the term "Your fear" in a general sense, all types of fear of Hashem are included, even fear of punishment and fear of sin].

Based on this, it can be said that the fact that the verse says "beauty of holiness" (and Rabbi Yehuda bar Levi had to interpret, "Do not read it as 'beauty' but as 'trembling'"), is not only not a contradiction to the interpretation of Rabbi Yehuda bar Levi, but on the contrary, it is relevant to the matter of "koved rosh" [and as mentioned above, the rule regarding "Do not read it as such" is that it is not meant to uproot the simple meaning, but to add to it]—because in order to reach "trembling of holiness," a fear of shame, it is through the contemplation of the "beauty of holiness," the splendor and greatness of Hashem, as explained in the Rambam (mentioned above) that "What is the way... and His fear? At the time that a person contemplates His wondrous, great deeds and creations..." and in the words of the Rema that before prayer, a person must contemplate "the majesty of the Almighty and the lowliness of man."

E. It can be said that Rabbi Nachman bar Yitzchak, who holds that "koved

## 72

rosh" is the matter of fear (in a general sense), and Rabbi Yehuda bar Levi, who holds that it refers to fear of shame, absolute bitul ("trembling of holiness")—each follows his own line of reasoning:

In the Mishnah at the end of Sotah, several things were listed that ceased "from the time that such-and-such a Tanna died," and among them, "From the time Rabbi died, fear of sin ceased." And in the Gemara there, "Rabbi Nachman said, 'Do not say fear of sin [ceased], for I am here.'" And it is explained in other places that Rabbi Nachman’s intention is that it is impossible to say that the quality of fear of sin was completely nullified, for it is impossible otherwise [to the point that even in the lowest generations, and even in the generation of the footsteps of Mashiach (described in the Gemara there), the quality of fear of sin exists—it is just a lower level of fear].

And regarding this, Rabbi Nachman said, "For I am here"—that he possessed this level of fear of sin in a general sense, as is told in the Gemara that "The mother of Rabbi Nachman bar Yitzchak was told by the astrologers, 'Your son will be a thief.' She would not let him uncover his head, saying to him, 'Cover your head so that the fear of Heaven will be upon you...' One day... his cloak fell from his head... his inclination overcame him..."—meaning that (at the beginning of his service) his level of fear was such that he required a cloak (a constant reminder), and this level of fear of sin cannot be nullified ("Do not say fear of sin [ceased]"), and it is relevant to every single Jew in every place and at every time.

And here too, he follows his own line of reasoning: that the "koved rosh" required of every Jew for prayer is not a lofty level of fear (which is not equal for every soul), but rather fear in a general sense, as mentioned above.

And Rabbi Yehuda bar Levi, who holds that "koved rosh" is (not fear (fear of sin), but) "trembling of holiness," absolute bitul—follows his own line of reasoning, for he holds that "Humility is greater than all of them" (than all the virtues listed in the Baraisa of Rabbi Pinchas ben Yair), including fear of sin. Because in his opinion, the primary virtue is bitul and humility, as is explained in this matter that "Humility is greater than all of them" refers to humility that is bitul in essence—and therefore he holds in our case that this is the virtue of "koved rosh" that must be in prayer, "trembling of holiness."

It should be added that also in the fact that Rabbi Yehuda bar Levi holds that koved rosh concerns the very object of prayer (as above)—he follows his own line of reasoning, for he said, "Come and see how great are the lowly of spirit before Hashem... Whoever has a lowly mind, the verse considers it as if he had offered all the sacrifices, as it is said, 'The sacrifices of Hashem are a broken spirit.'"

For "prayer is in place of the service [of sacrifices]", but in this very point, "prayers were instituted corresponding to the daily offerings" (which are only burnt offerings, which atone only for positive commandments), whereas through humility, "the verse considers it as if he had offered all the sacrifices." From this it is understood that when a person humbles himself in his prayer, this effects an incomparable elevation in his prayer,

## 73

that it is not just a "service" of a burnt offering alone, but a service of "all the sacrifices."

It can be said that according to Rabbi Yehuda bar Levi, this is the content of koved rosh, which comes to effect that a person's prayer should be equivalent to all the sacrifices, and it is understood that for this, an extremely lofty level of submission and humility is required, fear of shame as mentioned above.

F. One can add an explanation of Rabbi Yehuda bar Levi’s opinion on "koved rosh," based on the interpretation of the Maggid : "'One should not stand... except with koved rosh.' Do not pray for something that you lack... pray for the 'heaviness' (koved) that exists in the 'Head'... in the Shechinah, as it were"; "that all the intention of his prayer should be to influence the Shechinah of His strength... 'koved rosh' means the 'heaviness' of the 'Head of all heads'".

Seemingly, this is difficult to understand, for this is the opposite of the definition of prayer according to Halacha, whose essence is specifically the request for a person's needs ? The explanation for this can be said to be that the Maggid’s intention is that a person must be at a lofty level in the service of Hashem, where he is completely nullified before Him, to the point that his entire existence is nothing but his bitul to Hashem. And consequently, this is "the request for his needs"—because he needs nothing for himself, and all his concerns and needs are nothing but "the need of the High One," that the will of the Shechinah be fulfilled. Therefore, he prays for the "heaviness" and the lack of the Shechinah, as it were, the "Head of all heads."

It can be said that this is the level of Rabbi Yehuda bar Levi who entered alive into Gan Eden. For such a matter—that the physical body should ascend to Gan Eden—is a result of the fact that the person is so nullified before His holiness that physical things have no place for him at all, and his body with all its affairs is nothing but a vessel for his soul (which is "a part of Hashem from above, literally"), and therefore such a body can enter alive into Gan Eden (a place of spirituality).

And this is what Rabbi Yehuda bar Levi holds, that "koved rosh" means the level of fear of shame, which is absolute bitul; and then his prayer is from "koved rosh"—"the Head of all heads," as mentioned above.

G. Based on all of the above, we can explain the dispute between the Babylonian Talmud and the Yerushalmi (mentioned in section A)—that the Babylonian Talmud concludes that the teaching is from "Serve Hashem with fear," and in the Yerushalmi, both teachings are brought as a conclusion ("Worship Hashem in the beauty of holiness" and "Serve Hashem with fear"):

Bavel is a state of exile, where there is no revelation of holiness, to the point that even regarding the Babylonian Talmud they said, "He has made me dwell in darkness", a state of darkness, concealment, and hiding—and therefore in the Babylonian Talmud it concludes (like the opinion of Rabbi Nachman bar Yitzchak) that we derive "koved rosh" from "Serve Hashem with fear,"

## 74

which is fear of sin (as explained at length above), because in the state of "Bavel" (darkness), it is emphasized that one cannot demand of a Jew more than this level in the fear of Hashem ;

Whereas in the Jerusalem Talmud—the Torah of Eretz Yisrael, where "the eyes of Hashem your G-d are always upon it, from the beginning of the year to the end of the year", it is easier to reach higher levels in the fear of Hashem, and therefore the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda bar Levi was also brought there (and on the contrary, this opinion was brought first) that we derive it from "Worship Hashem in the beauty of holiness, in trembling of holiness".

It can be said that the fact that the Yalkut (on the verse "Worship...") brings only the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda bar Levi is because in the part of the Midrash that is in the Torah—where "most of the secrets of the Torah... are hidden in the Aggadah" —the inner content of "koved rosh" is emphasized, and therefore there is room to decide (only) in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda bar Levi, that "koved rosh" means the bitul of "trembling of holiness" (and even to "koved rosh" according to the interpretation of the Maggid—"koved rosh"—"the Head of all heads").

It can be said that from the perspective of the inner soul that is in every person, it is within the ability of everyone to reach this level as well, at least "a trace of it and the edge of its extremity."

And then the prayer is heard, that it is influenced, as it were, "in the Shechinah of His strength," that the primary lack—that of "the Shechinah in exile"—will be fulfilled "from above", through the fulfillment of the promise, "And Hashem your G-d will return your captivity," "that Hashem returns with them," in the true and complete Redemption, speedily in our days, literally.

(From the Sicha of 13 Tammuz, 5735)


## ראה

## Sicha 1

### 75

A. The Rambam writes: "Produce purchased with *maaser sheni* money may not be redeemed at a distance; rather, they must be brought up and eaten in Yerushalayim, unless they have become *tamei* with *av hatumah*." He continues in the next *halacha*: "This is a stringency regarding produce purchased with *maaser* money, as opposed to the *maaser* produce itself."

The Radbaz writes that the source for this law is in the *Mishnah* in *Maaser Sheni*: "Produce purchased with *maaser sheni* money that became *tamei* may be redeemed. Rabbi Yehuda says: It must be buried. They said to Rabbi Yehuda: If *maaser sheni* produce itself, when it becomes *tamei*, may be redeemed, is it not logical that produce purchased with *maaser* money, when it becomes *tamei*, should also be redeemed? He replied: You cannot compare it to *maaser sheni* produce itself, which may be redeemed even when pure and at a distance; would you say the same for produce purchased with *maaser* money, which may not be redeemed when pure and at a distance?"

The Radbaz writes: "We learn that produce purchased with *maaser sheni* money may not be redeemed at a distance according to all opinions. This is what our teacher [the Rambam] meant when he wrote, 'This is a stringency regarding produce purchased with money, etc.,' that they may not be redeemed at a distance unless they have become *tamei*. And we find in many places that the *tafel* (secondary) is more stringent than the *ikar* (primary)."

The Radbaz’s statement that this law is similar to what we find in many places—that the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*—is sourced in the *Gemara* in *Zevachim*, where it states that we find the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar* regarding the law of redeeming *maaser sheni*: "And *maaser* itself may be redeemed, whereas produce purchased with *maaser* money may not be redeemed, as we learned in the *Mishnah*: 'Produce purchased with *maaser* money that became *tamei* may be redeemed; Rabbi Yehuda says it must be buried'" (though this example is later rejected in the *Gemara*, as will be discussed in section 5).

However, there are two interpretations of the *Gemara* (regarding the principle that in the law of *maaser* redemption we find the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*), corresponding to two versions of the *Gemara*:

1) According to the version and commentary of Rashi (and as proven in *Tosafos* from the *Tosefta*), the proof is from the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda: produce purchased with *maaser* money that became *tamei* may not be redeemed (but must be "buried"), whereas *maaser sheni* produce that became *tamei* may be redeemed even in Yerushalayim. Thus, the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*.

2) "There are books that record the version here as: 'If it became *tamei*, yes; if it did not become *tamei*, no,' and they bring a proof from the Rabbis that the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar'." That is, the proof is from produce purchased with *maaser* money that is pure, where the law is that it may not be redeemed even outside of Yerushalayim (for it is only redeemed if it became *tamei*), whereas *maaser* produce itself (even when pure) may be redeemed outside of Yerushalayim.

Seemingly, this is the view of the Rambam [according to the aforementioned interpretation of the Radbaz, that for the Rambam this law is included in the category of "the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*"]—for the Rambam explicitly states here that the stringency of produce purchased with *maaser* money relative to *maaser* produce itself concerns the redemption of pure produce (which is according to the Rabbis, as mentioned above).

### 76

Regarding the explanation of the reasoning of Rashi (and those who agree with him) who hold that the proof in the *Gemara* is not from the opinion of the Rabbis (whose view is the *halacha*), but only from the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda: *Tosafos* writes that "this [the fact that produce purchased with *maaser* money cannot be redeemed outside of Yerushalayim, unlike *maaser* produce itself] is not called 'the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*,' because *maaser* produce itself, when pure, may be redeemed at a distance in order to purchase [redeem] it with money in Yerushalayim for cattle and sheep, etc. But if one purchased produce with it outside of Yerushalayim to eat in Yerushalayim, it would not be redeemed, just as he would not redeem what he had purchased in Yerushalayim." [The Radbaz writes similarly there (in his second interpretation) that "this is as if he purchased them in Yerushalayim, where they are not subject to redemption... (and he concludes) and this reason is sufficient even for the one who says we do not treat the *tafel* more stringently than the *ikar*."]

Based on this, if one were to say that the Rambam holds that this law (that purchased produce is not redeemed like *maaser* produce itself) is because "the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*," one must conclude that he does not hold by the reasoning that produce purchased with *maaser* money "is as if he purchased them in Yerushalayim."

B. We must explain the logic of this dispute by first defining the nature of *maaser sheni* redemption, which can be explained in several ways:

1) It is a *heter* (permission) for the person, as the literal text of the *pesukim* suggests: "If the way is too long for you, so that you are not able to carry it... then you shall turn it into money, and bind the money in your hand, and go to the place..." Even though the (primary) *mitzvah* of *maaser sheni* is to eat the *maaser* produce itself in Yerushalayim, when it is a burden for a person to bring the produce to Yerushalayim ("if the way is too long for you"), he is permitted to redeem the produce with money, and use that money to buy produce in Yerushalayim and eat it there.

2) The redemption of *maaser sheni* produce is not a *heter* for the person, but rather this is the *mitzvah* of *maaser* according to the letter of the law. When "the way is too long for you, so that you are not able to carry it," the Torah says that one redeems the produce with money, etc. That is: the Torah established two ways to fulfill the *mitzvah* of *maaser* from the outset: a) Bringing the produce itself to Yerushalayim; b) Redeeming the produce and bringing the money to Yerushalayim in order to buy produce there, etc., and eating it there.

And within this, one could say there are two approaches:
a) The law of *maaser* redemption is a detail within the general law of eating *maaser* produce in Yerushalayim.

### 78

...*maaser sheni* itself" is because "its *mitzvah* has already been completed, and it is no longer subject to redemption."

3) According to the first interpretation (in the Radbaz) of the Rambam’s view, that the fact that produce purchased with *maaser* money is not redeemed falls under the category of "the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*" (and not like the reasons above that "this is not called 'the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*'"): one could say (like the third approach above) that the law of redeeming *maaser* produce is a *mitzvah* in its own right, which is completed immediately upon their redemption (and not merely a preparation for eating *maaser* produce in Yerushalayim). Therefore, it is not possible to say that when he purchased produce with this money, it is as if he purchased them in Yerushalayim and its *mitzvah* was completed—because bringing the money and purchasing produce in Yerushalayim is not the completion of the *mitzvah* of redemption, but rather a part of the general *mitzvah* of bringing *maaser* produce to Yerushalayim and eating it there (as mentioned above, that this *mitzvah* has two modes: bringing the *maaser* produce itself, or bringing the money and buying the produce in Yerushalayim). Consequently, after he redeems the *maaser* produce, the obligation of redemption is immediately completed, and from that moment, the general obligation applies to him to bring the money or the produce purchased with it to Yerushalayim, etc. (similar to the obligation to bring *maaser* produce itself to Yerushalayim), and the obligation of bringing and eating is not completed until after the produce is eaten in Yerushalayim.

And since, despite this, the purchased produce is not redeemed like *maaser* produce itself, it follows that the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*.

D. With this, we can also explain the intent of the Rambam in writing, "This is a stringency regarding produce purchased with *maaser* money, as opposed to *maaser* produce itself"—for seemingly, it is not understood what the Rambam adds with these words to the law regarding the purchased produce (that produce purchased with *maaser sheni* money is not redeemed) which he had already written in the previous *halacha*?

Based on the above, we can say that with this, the

### 79

Rambam comes to emphasize that there is a novelty here: that there is a stringency in produce purchased with *maaser* money compared to *maaser* produce itself. That is, the Rambam’s intent is to highlight the novelty that the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*. With this, the Rambam defines the law of *maaser* redemption as being like the third approach above, that there is an element of "the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*," as discussed at length.

E. However, it still needs to be understood:
How can one say that the Rambam holds that this law (that produce purchased with *maaser* money is not redeemed) contains the element of "the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*"—when the *Gemara* there concludes that one should not bring a proof from this law that "the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*," because "there, its *kedushah* is not strong enough to capture the redemption money" [meaning that the fact that one cannot redeem produce purchased with *maaser* money is not due to their stringency, but on the contrary, because their *kedushah* is a light *kedushah* ("its *kedushah* is not strong") which does not have the power to capture the redemption funds]?

Simply, one could say:
(a) Since this is not a *psak halacha* but (only) a reason for the *halacha*, the Rambam chose the simpler reason (according to the principles of the Rambam, where he writes the simpler explanation, etc.).
(b) That the Rambam holds that this was only the *kas d'hava* (initial thought) in the *Gemara*, but according to the *maskana* (conclusion), we maintain that it is included in the category of "the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*" (as the Radbaz says, "And we find in many places that the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*").

But to explain further, one could say that what the Rambam wrote here is because the content of the *mitzvah* of *maaser sheni* is connected with this rule that we find, that the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*.

F. It has been explained elsewhere the difference between a *bechor* and *maaser* (behemah): the *kedushah* of a *bechor* is from the womb and is not dependent on human action; whereas the *kedushah* of *maaser* is dependent on human action, for the person counts the flock, and then "the tenth shall be holy," and similarly it is in *maaser sheni*, that the application of its "*kedushah*" is through the act of separation.

That is, a *bechor* is *kedushah* from Heaven, and *maaser* is *kedushah* created by man.
However, in detail, there are also two matters in *maaser* itself: (a) the human action, which is the counting (the separation), (b) the *kedushah* from Heaven that comes afterward.

### 80

And according to what we explained above, one could say that there is a novelty in *maaser sheni*—that it has not only the *mitzvah* of separation and the *kedushah* that comes through it from Heaven, but also the *mitzvah* of redemption (as mentioned above, that this is not merely a permission and ability to bring the produce to Yerushalayim, but it is (also) a law and obligation in the fulfillment of the *mitzvah* of *maaser*), where the application of the *kedushah* through the redemption comes through the redemption, the action of man.

And this is what the Rambam wrote, that there is a "stringency in produce purchased with *maaser* money compared to *maaser* produce itself":
"*Maaser* produce itself" is *kedushah* that comes from Heaven, whereas "produce purchased with *maaser* money" is *kedushah* that comes through the action of man (that the man redeemed the *maaser*, and furthermore—subsequently exchanged the money for other produce), and as the man finds himself at a "distance" (specifically) from Yerushalayim, a *kedushah* added to the *kedushah* of the rest of Eretz Yisrael (as the *Mishnah* says).

And even though, simply speaking, the *kedushah* that comes from Heaven is a greater *kedushah* than that which comes through the action of man, and in the style of our Sages, the action of man is "his *kav*" (measure), only one *kav*, and it is not comparable to the "nine *kavim*" of his fellow (Hashem, as it is written, "Your friend and your father's friend do not forsake"), the *kedushah* that comes from Heaven—nevertheless, there is a merit in "his *kav*," the action of man specifically. The reason for this is because "You yearn for the work of Your hands," that the will and desire, as it were, of Hashem is "for Me, the Children of Yisrael are servants," specifically for the work of man below, even though it is only "one *kav*."

And since specifically in the *kedushah* that comes through the action of man there is an additional merit as mentioned, therefore there is a "stringency in produce purchased with *maaser* money" relative to "*maaser* produce itself," which is the *kedushah* that comes from Heaven. And this is in addition to the "stringency" in the very addition of *kedushah* by man, for when one repeats and adds *kedushah*, this is *l'akeva* (a requirement for validity) (for in *kodshim*, "the Torah repeated it to make it *l'akeva*").

[And this is the very reason for that which "a person prefers his own *kav* (his own work) over nine *kavim* of his fellow (Hashem)"—because specifically in this is the desire from Above. And from this it was drawn down (similar to "the kingdom of earth is like the kingdom of Heaven") regarding one's fellow among human beings.]

G. With this, we can explain further the reason why in the words of the Rambam (according to the first interpretation in the Radbaz) the matter of "the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*" is emphasized specifically regarding *maaser* redemption—because this is also the content of the "rule" that "we find in many places that the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*":

The "*ikar*" is the *kedushah* from Heaven, and the "*tafel*"—the *kedushah* through the action of man. And this is sweetened, that it is called "*tafel*" relative to the *kedushah* from Heaven, because the fact that the action of man sanctifies—this comes from Heaven, (through the power of the Torah—) Torah from Heaven.

And this is what "we find in many places that the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*," similar to the matter that the words of the Scribes are more stringent than the words of

### 81

the Torah, even though the words of the Scribes themselves come—are learned or at least hinted at—in the words of the Torah.

And one could say that in the phrase "the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*" it is also hinted that the stringency, etc., in the action of man (*tafel*) relative to the *kedushah* that comes from Heaven (*ikar*)—this is not a stringency in the action of man from his own perspective, but that it comes from the *ikar* (from Heaven).

(Similar to what is explained regarding the matter and phrase "the power of the son is greater than the power of the father," that even this power in the son which is greater than the power of the father, comes and is drawn "from the power of the father.")

H. Based on the above, we can explain further the content of the difference between the two interpretations in the Radbaz (regarding the law that one does not redeem produce purchased with *maaser* money)—(a) because we find that the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*, (b) because it is considered as if he purchased them in Yerushalayim—for one could say that these are two matters (and stages) in the elevation of the *kedushah* that comes through the action of man relative to the *kedushah* that comes from Heaven:

According to the first interpretation, that produce purchased with *maaser* money is "*tafel*" relative to *maaser* produce itself—it turns out that the *kedushah* that comes through the action of man is *tafel* relative to the *kedushah* from Heaven, which is the "*ikar*"; but since "You yearn for the work of Your hands," therefore there is a "stringency" and merit in the action of man relative to the *kedushah* from Heaven—the *tafel* is more stringent than the *ikar*.

Conversely, according to the second interpretation in the Radbaz (that produce purchased with *maaser* money "is as if he purchased them in Yerushalayim")—the produce purchased with *maaser* money is not "*tafel*," but on the contrary, it has the elevation of the completion and perfection of the *kedushah* of *maaser*, "it is as if he purchased them in Yerushalayim" (where the produce is sanctified in a way (similar to *kedushat haguf*) that it is impossible to redeem them).

And this is a second stage in the elevation of the "work of Your hands" of man, that in the action of man himself there is the merit of "his *kav*" (the *kedushah* that comes through the action of man), and also the "nine *kavim* of his fellow," which is the perfection of the elevation in the action of man, that there are not two separate *kedushos* here, the *kedushah* through the action of man and the *kedushah* from Heaven, but they become one *kedushah*.

And one could say that the fact that "You yearn for the work of Your hands" is because Yisrael and the Holy One, blessed be He, are all one, as it were.

And for this reason, even the action and work of man when he is at a "distance," and which depends (seemingly) entirely on the man—"it is as if he purchased them in Yerushalayim," "he shall appear whole," the ultimate perfection of *kedushah*.

And one could say that this matter is hinted at in the two extremes in *maaser sheni* in general:
On one hand, "it is high-level property, as it is written 'It is for Hashem,' and therefore it is not acquired as a gift, etc., and one does not betroth a woman with it, and one does not sell it, etc.," and on the other hand—the *mitzvah* of eating it is not for the *Kohanim* or the *Levi'im* or the like, but rather the owners themselves eat it,

Because in the *mitzvah* of *maaser sheni*, the two extremes are united: the *kedushah* of the High One, and this itself—in the *kedushah* of the action of man itself.

(From the *Sicha* of *Shabbos Parshas Re'eh* 5745)

## Sicha 2

### 82

1. Regarding the verse "You shall surely open your hand to him," Rashi explains: "You shall surely open" — "even many times." Subsequently, regarding the verse "You shall surely give to him," he explains — "even one hundred times".

However, in the Sifrei before us, it states "one hundred times" for both: "And from where do we know that if you opened once, you open even one hundred times? The verse says, 'You shall surely open your hand to him.'... And from where do we know that if you gave to him once, you give to him even one hundred times? The verse says, 'You shall surely give'".

Simply put, one could say the reason for Rashi’s interpretation is the literal meaning of the text: the first verse teaches us only "many times," and the second verse, "you shall surely give," comes to add "even one hundred times."

But this is difficult, for in the literal meaning of the text, one could explain that the first verse ("You shall surely open") speaks (primarily) of the obligation of *tzedakah*, while the second verse ("You shall surely give") refers (primarily) to the *mitzvah* of *gemilut chasadim*. For this verse follows the statement, "Beware lest there be a base thought in your heart, saying, 'The seventh year, the year of *shemitah*, is approaching,' and your eye will be evil against your needy brother, and you will not give him, etc." Regarding the year of *shemitah*, the concern is in connection with *gemilut chasadim*, as stated previously, "And this is the matter of the *shemitah*: every creditor shall release his hand, etc. He shall not exact from his neighbor, etc." And regarding this, it continues, "You shall surely give, even many (one hundred) times," also in *gemilut chasadim*.

Similarly, we find in the commentaries that they wrote that both *derashot* in the Sifrei are necessary: "One for giving as *tzedakah*, and one for lending at a time of need, and it teaches us that even lending at a time of need is considered *tzedakah* for him, even though he will be repaid."

[There is another version in the Sifrei: "And from where do we know that if you opened once, you will eventually open many times? The verse says, 'You shall surely open your hand to him'". And regarding the verse "You shall surely give," "I have only one time; from where do I know even one hundred times? The verse says, 'You shall surely give.'" This means that "You shall surely open" is not a command but a promise and a blessing, "that if you opened once, you will eventually open many times".

Although it is obvious that this is not the literal meaning of the text.

And from Rashi’s language in both verses, "even many times" and "even one hundred times," it is understood that he interprets both as commands, according to the literal meaning of the text.]

### 83

2. There is room to say, based on the difference in the content of the verses: In the verse "You shall surely open," it says, "sufficient for his need, which he lacks." Even though "you are not commanded to make him rich", nevertheless, one must give him everything "which he lacks," "even a horse to ride upon and a servant to run before him" — and for this, one is obligated "even many times." Whereas in the verse "You shall surely give," it speaks only of giving per se — and for this, the obligation is "even one hundred times."

One may add a reason for this law: since the verse "You shall surely open" speaks of abundant giving to fulfill all the needs of the poor person, one is not commanded "one hundred times," for the Torah speaks of the majority, and the text speaks of the common occurrence, where such giving does not occur "one hundred times," but only "many times," so that he should not say that since he already gave the poor person "sufficient for his need, which he lacks," he has fulfilled his obligation. This is not the case in the verse "You shall surely give," where there is no such emphasis on "sufficient for his need, which he lacks." Rashi explains "even one hundred times," because in simple giving, a small gift, it is possible that it will be "even one hundred times."

But it still requires explanation, for ultimately, since the obligation of giving to the poor is (as in the first verse) "sufficient for his need, which he lacks," it is understood that what is said in "You shall surely give" (in the subsequent verse) also includes the obligation of giving *tzedakah*, which was discussed in the previous verse, in the manner of "sufficient for his need, which he lacks."

3. It seems simple to say, beginning with the precision in Rashi’s wording, that in the first verse he quotes from the text only the words "You shall surely open," while in the second verse he quotes "You shall surely give to him" (even though the teaching "even one hundred times" is only from the words "You shall surely give").
