The explanation for this may be: in the preceding text it says, "If there be among you a needy person, etc., you shall not harden your heart nor close your hand from your needy brother," and regarding this it continues, "You shall surely open, etc." And the verse "You shall surely give" comes (as mentioned above) in continuation of the fear that "the seventh year, the year of *shemitah*, is approaching, and your eye will be evil against your needy brother, and you will not give him, etc."

### 84

From this it is understood that the text "You shall surely open" (which comes after the warning against hardening the heart and closing the hands) deals with the opening of the heart and hand of the giver. In contrast, the text "You shall surely give" speaks (primarily) of the giving to the poor person and the borrower.

Therefore, specifically in the verse "You shall surely give," Rashi quotes from the text also the word "to him," and not in the verse "You shall surely open," to inform us that here the main emphasis is on the action "to him" — on the recipient of the *tzedakah*.

[According to this, it is understood simply why specifically in the second verse Rashi explains the word "to him" — "between him and you", and not in the verse "You shall surely open" (even though in the first verse the word "to him" is not strictly necessary, as it could have said, "For you shall surely open your hand and lend him enough for his need which he lacks") — because only in the second verse is the main emphasis on the giving to the recipient. Therefore, he emphasizes "to him" — "between him and you," how the manner of giving must be in practice "to him," to the recipient. This is not the case in the verse "You shall surely open," where the main point is (not the giving from the giver to the recipient, but) the opening of the heart and hand of the giver. Therefore, one should not be precise with the word "to him" in relation to the manner of giving (and perhaps one could say that the word "to him" here means "for his sake," that he opens his heart for the sake of the poor person).]

According to this, one can add: the fact that in the verse "You shall surely give to him," Rashi explains "even one hundred times," and in the verse "You shall surely open" he writes "even many times":

In the text "You shall surely open," where the main emphasis in the command is not on the quantity of the gift to the recipient but on the opening of the heart and hands of the giver, i.e., the quality of the giving from the side of the giver, there is no place to explain "even one hundred times," because this is appropriate only when speaking of the gift to the recipient, where the text emphasizes that this act must be "even one hundred times," but not so much when speaking of the content of the opening of the heart and hands of the giver.

Rather, there it teaches us "even many times," that even though the text commands the giver of *tzedakah* to "surely open," it is not enough that it be only once, the first time he gives *tzedakah*, but a person must strive that every act of *tzedakah* be in the manner of "you shall surely open your hand," the opposite of hardening the heart and closing the hands.

4. One should add regarding the *halachah*.

One must inquire: is giving *tzedakah* from a willing heart and a cheerful countenance part of the essence of the *mitzvah* of *tzedakah*, whereas with less than this, even if he fulfilled the *mitzvah*, it is only in the manner of a partial fulfillment of the *mitzvah*? Or is the essence of the *mitzvah* of *tzedakah* the giving, and a cheerful countenance is an additional "merit" in the giving ?

The Rambam, in *Hilchos Matnos Aniyim*, in explaining the *mitzvah* of *tzedakah*, writes : "It is a positive commandment to give *tzedakah* to the poor as is fitting for the poor person, if the giver’s hand can afford it, as it is said, etc.," and he proceeds to explain the details of the laws of giving *tzedakah* and supporting the poor and their manner. And after several chapters, he elaborates on the greatness of the merit of *tzedakah*, that it is a sign of a righteous person, the seed of Avraham Avinu, and no person becomes poor from *tzedakah*, and that its giving must be with a cheerful countenance and with joy, and he mourns with him over his trouble, and he proceeds to list eight levels of *tzedakah*. And in these levels themselves — in the two last (lowest) levels, he writes : "Less than this: that he gives him less than is fitting, with a cheerful countenance. Less than this: that he gives him with sadness." It is explicit that even this (giving with sadness) is the *mitzvah* of *tzedakah* —

Especially since the Rambam divided them into two chapters (and with several chapters in between), and in the first chapter, when he explains the essence of the positive commandment, he did not mention the merits regarding the manner of its giving, but wrote them in a separate chapter — it follows that according to the Rambam, the "positive commandment" is giving *tzedakah* to the poor as is fitting for the poor, i.e., that the act of giving in practice according to the need of the poor is the entire definition of the *mitzvah*, but the manners of its giving and the giving with a cheerful countenance are (additional) merits in the *mitzvah* of *tzedakah*. Therefore, even when he gives with sadness (it is explicit in the Rambam that) he has fulfilled the *mitzvah* of *tzedakah*.

### 86

But according to the above in Rashi, in the difference between "You shall surely open" and "You shall surely give," that the text "For you shall surely open" comes in continuation of the command of *tzedakah*, "You shall not harden your heart nor close your hand from your needy brother" (as Rashi explains : "There is a person who is distressed whether to give or not... there is a person who opens his hand and closes it, etc."), that the giving should be in the manner of "you shall surely open," with the will and desire of the heart, with a cheerful countenance ; and the text "You shall surely give" comes to warn and negate "and your eye will be evil against your needy brother, and you will not give him," i.e., the act of giving in practice — it follows that the beginning and the start of the command of the *mitzvah* of *tzedakah*, "You shall surely open," is (not only on the act of giving itself but also) on giving it with a willing heart.

And regarding this, Rashi explains "even many times," that even giving *tzedakah* in this manner must be many times — for this is the essence of the *mitzvah* of *tzedakah*.

However, he then adds that even for a person who lacks the willing heart to the point that it could occur to him not to give ("and your eye will be evil against your needy brother, and you will not give him"), nevertheless the Torah commands "You shall surely give," meaning that he should not lack (at least) the act of giving in practice. According to this, it is understood why it continues and adds in the text, "And your heart shall not be grieved when you give to him, for because of this thing Hashem your G-d will bless you in all your work, etc." — for it speaks of a type of *tzedakah* giver who lacks so much in the willing heart that he needs to be promised "for because of this thing, etc.," that he will profit and receive reward for this.

5. According to the above, one can also explain the allusion in the "wine of Torah" in Rashi, the change between what Rashi wrote regarding the verse "You shall surely open" —

### 88

That they say a limited number, "one hundred times," implies that this has some limitation.

[And as explained in *Likutei Torah*, the number "one hundred" indicates the perfection of the *hishtalshelus*, for the *hishtalshelus* is divided in general into the number ten — ten *sefiros*, ten powers of the soul, etc., and their perfection, when each of them is included of ten, is the number one hundred, as he brought from *Chiddushei Aggados* of the Maharsha in the interpretation of the Sages : "He who reviews his study one hundred times is not comparable to one who reviews his study one hundred and one times," for "the number one hundred reaches until *nefesh*, *ruach*, and *neshamah*, which are levels of *yechidah* rich in hundreds, etc., but he who reviews one time more than this is a *chelek Eloka mima'al*."]

In contrast, the expression "many times," where no specific number is mentioned, can include both few and many, and applies to any number that may be, and this also alludes to the absence of limitation, above *hishtalshelus*.

And this is one of the differences between Rashi on "You shall surely open" — "even many times" — and his commentary on "You shall surely give" — "even one hundred times":

"You shall surely give" is also said in the language of a "source," for the act of the *mitzvah* of a person of Israel in any situation he is in — according to the well-known ruling of the Rambam, it contains the "source" — the truth and internality of his will, that "he desires to do all the *mitzvos* and to distance himself from the *aveiros*" [and especially the act of *tzedakah*, for "we are obligated to be more careful in the *mitzvah* of *tzedakah* than in any positive commandment, for *tzedakah* is a sign of a righteous person, the seed of Avraham Avinu, etc., and the throne of Israel is not established and the religion of truth does not stand except through *tzedakah*, as it is said, 'In *tzedakah* you shall be established'"]].

But since this does not come and penetrate into his soul’s powers down to action, for in the externality and in the revealed, a situation of "and your eye will be evil against your needy brother, etc." is possible for him, and there is a need to inform him that it is for his good, "for because of this thing Hashem your G-d will bless you, etc." as mentioned above, therefore the allusion and the action that comes from the source is "even one hundred times," which is a specific and limited number.

But when a person chooses the giving of *tzedakah* from the source of his soul, and this shines in him in a revealed way — "You shall surely open" with all his soul’s powers down to his power of action — this is alluded to in the expression "many times" as a merit — without any limitation at all.

And through the fulfillment of the *mitzvah* of *tzedakah*, and especially in the days of the month of *Elul* in which one increases *tzedakah*, and as explained in the *Roshei Teivos* of *Elul*, "a man to his neighbor and gifts to the poor," and the combination of the Name *Havayah* that shines in the month of *Elul* emerges from the final letters of the verse "And *tzedakah* shall be for us, for" —

Each and every one merits to be written and sealed in the book of the completely righteous, until — the true and complete *Geulah*, for "Israel is not redeemed except through *tzedakah*, as it is said, 'Zion shall be redeemed with justice, and those who return to her with *tzedakah*'" — very soon.

(From the *sicha* of *Shabbos Parshas Re'eh*, 5743)


## ראה - אלול

## Sicha 1

## 89

### Sicha 1

A. The positive commandment of giving *tzedakah* is explicitly stated in our Parsha: "If there will be among you a needy person... you shall not harden your heart... you shall surely open [your hand]..." (as stated by the Rambam and the Chinuch).

Based on the well-known principle that all the Parshiyos in the Torah have a connection to the time in which they are read, it is understood that there is a special connection between the mitzvah of *tzedakah* and the month of Elul—for we always read Parshas Re'eh in proximity to Rosh Chodesh Elul (on Shabbos Mevarchim Elul, or—as is the calendar arrangement this year (5748)—on Rosh Chodesh Elul). This means that in the month of Elul, one must increase the performance of the mitzvah of *tzedakah* more than in the rest of the year.

We can find a source for this from the precision of the Rambam’s language in *Hilchos Teshuvah* regarding the Aseres Yemei Teshuvah. He writes:

"Even though the blowing of the Shofar on Rosh Hashanah is a divine decree, it contains a hint, meaning: 'Awaken, you sleepers... and improve your ways...' Every person must view himself... as if he is half-innocent... [If] he performs one mitzvah, he tips the scales for himself and for the entire world to the side of merit, and brings about salvation and rescue for himself and for them... Because of this matter, all the House of Israel have the custom to increase *tzedakah* and good deeds and to engage in mitzvos from Rosh Hashanah until Yom Kippur more than in the rest of the year, and it is the custom of all to rise at night during these ten days and to pray in the synagogues with words of supplication and contrition until the day breaks."

From the fact that the Rambam singles out the custom to "increase *tzedakah*" from the general rule of "good deeds and engaging in mitzvos," and furthermore, that he places it before the other matters, it is proven that even though during these days there must be a strengthening in all matters of Torah and mitzvos, first and foremost we must "increase *tzedakah*."

From this, we can learn something similar regarding the month of Elul, since it is a microcosm of the Aseres Yemei Teshuvah in several respects: (1) Elul is the month of *teshuvah*, similar to the Aseres Yemei Teshuvah; (2) just as these ten days are a preparation for the "sealing" of Yom Kippur (as explained in the commentaries, that this is the reason for increasing *tzedakah*, etc., during these days, because "the *beinoni* is held in suspense until Yom Kippur; if he does *teshuvah*, he is sealed for life")—so too, the entire

## 90

month of Elul is a preparation for the "writing" (the beginning of the judgment) of Rosh Hashanah; (3) and furthermore: the entire forty days from Rosh Chodesh Elul until after Yom Kippur are a continuation and a single entity of the days of *teshuvah*, as explained in the *Bach*: "The verse 'I am to my Beloved and my Beloved is to me' (Ani L'dodi V'dodi Li) has the initials for Elul, and the final letters add up to forty, corresponding to the forty days from Rosh Chodesh Elul until Yom Kippur, for during these forty days of *teshuvah*, [the prayer] is accepted... His Beloved is close to accepting *teshuvah* out of love."

From this, it is understood that just as in the Aseres Yemei Teshuvah there is a special matter of "increasing *tzedakah*," so too in the month of Elul, the initial strengthening in matters of Torah and mitzvos during this month must be in the area of *tzedakah*—"to increase *tzedakah*." It can be said that this is also hinted at by the fact that the combination of the Name of *Havayah* that shines in the month of Elul emerges from the final letters of "*u'tzedakah* *tihyeh lanu ki*."

One must understand: How does the mitzvah of *tzedakah* differ from all other matters of Torah and mitzvos, such that the strengthening in the month of Elul (and the Aseres Yemei Teshuvah) is specifically in the matter of *tzedakah*?

B. Seemingly, one could say that this is because the giving of *tzedakah* atones for sin, as it is written, "And break your sin with *tzedakah*," and as the Alter Rebbe elaborates in *Iggeres HaTeshuvah*.

Based on this, we can explain the precision of the language "to increase *tzedakah*." For seemingly, according to *halacha*, there is a measure for *tzedakah*: the mandatory obligation is a tenth (*maaser*), a fifth (*chomesh*) is the preferred way to perform the mitzvah, but not more than that, as the Rambam writes, "Whoever distributes... should not distribute more than a fifth." However, here, since we are dealing with *tzedakah* that is for the redemption of the soul, "one need not be concerned about the prohibition of 'do not squander more than a fifth,' for it is not called squandering in such a case, since he is doing it to redeem his soul...". This is why the Rambam specifies "to increase *tzedakah*" and does not set a limit for this *tzedakah*—because for this *tzedakah*, there is no measure.

But on the face of it, it is forced to interpret it this way, for the Rambam details the custom to "increase"—"in *tzedakah* and good deeds and to engage in mitzvos." It does not appear that there are two separate matters here: an increase in *tzedakah* as part of the act of *teshuvah*, and an increase in good deeds and mitzvos to tip the scales of judgment to the side of merit. It is more logical that he is explaining nothing other than the tipping of the scales of judgment to the side of merit, and that even within that same category of good deeds and engaging in mitzvos whose intent is to tip the scales to the side of merit, there is an emphasis and a superiority in the increase of *tzedakah*.

C. This will be understood by first noting the Rambam’s precision there—that he wrote about the custom of increasing prayer and supplication at night in a separate clause ("and it is the custom of all to rise at night, etc.")—for seemingly, he should have said in one clause, "to increase *tzedakah*, etc., and to rise at night, etc."

The explanation for this is as follows:
In the general reception of a person's needs from Hashem, there are two modes, as stated in the Gemara regarding the verse, "Hearken to me, you stout-hearted, who are far from *tzedakah*": "The entire world is sustained by *tzedakah*, while they are sustained by 'force' (strength/merit)": (1) "The entire world" receives its needs from Hashem only by way of *tzedakah* and the kindness of Hashem, and not by virtue of any merit they possess, for they are not worthy of it based on their deeds; (2) The *tzaddikim* are "sustained by 'force,' by the merit they possess, and they are called 'far from the *tzedakah* of Hashem'", because they are sustained by the merit of their good deeds.

In the *avodah* of Hashem, these two modes correspond (generally) to the distinction between prayer and the engagement in Torah and mitzvos: The matter of prayer is a request and supplication, "The poor man speaks with supplications", that Hashem should have mercy on him and fulfill his requests even though he is not worthy of it, and in the language of *Chazal*, a "free gift." However, through the engagement in Torah and mitzvos, a person receives his needs (not as *tzedakah*, but) according to the law, as we were promised in the Torah, "If you walk in My statutes and keep My mitzvos... I will give your rains in their time...".

Based on this, when Hashem judges a person, and "they weigh the person's merits and sins", reason dictates that when they judge him to merit, it is not because of mercy and *tzedakah*, but according to his standing.

Therefore, one might have thought that during the Aseres Yemei Teshuvah, the primary emphasis should be on increasing mitzvos and good deeds in order to merit in judgment, and there is no place for a request for mercy, that it should be "by way of *tzedakah*," beyond the letter of the law.

[And even though regarding prayer we find that "even though *tzaddikim* have their good deeds to rely upon, they only ask of the Almighty as a free gift"—this is only appropriate regarding prayer and request, where they do not need to demand from Hashem but only to ask of Him, may He be blessed, by way of a free gift (even though they can rely on their good deeds). This is not the case when the subject is judgment, where Hashem weighs the person's merits, etc., and judges him according to his deeds; there is no place to mix into this a request for mercy and *tzedakah*, etc.].

## 92

But the Rambam teaches us that in addition to the custom to increase in matters of merit (*tzedakah* and good deeds and mitzvos), the Jewish people also have the custom "to rise at night... and to pray... with words of supplication... until the day breaks". It can be said that the very fact that they merit in judgment according to the letter of the law is itself in the category of "*tzedakah*" from Above, because the deeds of human beings (the created) do not "take up space" before Him, may He be blessed (as it is written, "If you are righteous, what do you give Him?"). Furthermore, even the performance of good deeds (by the human) is through the power of Hashem, as it is written, "Who has preceded Me that I should pay him?". Therefore, the very fact that Hashem (lowers Himself, as it were) judges the person and considers his good deeds as his merit in judgment—this is kindness and *tzedakah* from Him, may He be blessed, and for this, one needs supplications, etc.

Therefore, the Rambam separated these customs into two clauses, because they are two separate matters in the actions of the Jewish people to merit in judgment: The first clause is the things that the Jewish people do to tip the scales of the balance, and the second clause is the prayer and supplication, because for the very reason that these things should tip the scales to the side of merit, they require the mercy of Hashem, as mentioned above.

[It can be said that this is also the reason for the change in language in the two clauses—in the first clause "all the House of Israel have the custom," and in the second clause "it is the custom of all"—which implies somewhat that the second matter, the custom to rise, etc., is a simple practice for everyone, more so than the first matter. And according to the above, it is understood, because the request for mercy is relevant to every single Jew, even those who did not increase (as much) in mitzvos and good deeds (who were unable to increase, etc.). On the other hand, even those who did increase in mitzvos and good deeds more than in the rest of the year have the custom to increase in the request for mercy ("words of supplication"), because they too need to know and feel that their merit in judgment is in the category of the *tzedakah* of Hashem, for which they need requests and supplications.]

With this, we can also explain why the Rambam singled out "to increase *tzedakah*" in the first clause from the general rule, and placed it before the other matters—because in the increase of *tzedakah*, it is also emphasized that the person feels that he needs to merit in judgment only because of the *tzedakah* of Hashem, and therefore he increases in *tzedakah* so that Hashem will also conduct Himself with him in a manner of *tzedakah*, measure for measure. That is to say, the fact that a person feels that everything he merits from Above is in the category of *tzedakah* and a free gift must be expressed not only in his prayer, but also in his deeds whose intent is the tipping of the scales of judgment to the good according to the law; even in them, one must emphasize that this is beyond the letter of the law from Above.

D. Explaining the connection of "*tzedakah*" to the judgment of Rosh Hashanah more deeply (and according to the inner dimension of the matters):

Regarding the definition of the essence of the *avodah* of Rosh Hashanah, *Chazal* say that Hashem said: "Say before Me on Rosh Hashanah: *Malchiyos* (Kingship), *Zichronos* (Remembrances), and *Shofaros* (Shofar blasts). *Malchiyos*—so that you may crown Me over you; *Zichronos*—so that your remembrance may come before Me for good; and with what? With the Shofar."

Based on this, it has been explained in many places that the primary *avodah* of Rosh Hashanah is the crowning of Hashem as King over Israel ("so that you may crown Me over you"), and thereby as King over the entire world.

This is not a contradiction to what is explicitly stated, that Rosh Hashanah and Yom Kippur are days of judgment, for both are present in them: From Hashem’s perspective, the true content of these days is that they crown Him as King, a matter that pertains primarily (as it were) to Hashem, and as the language goes, "Say before Me... so that you may crown Me over you," that Hashem requests this from the Jewish people, because this is a matter that pertains to Him; and from the perspective of the created, as Hashem has, as it were, lowered Himself to sit and judge all the inhabitants of the world, these are days of judgment and trial where they weigh the person's merits and sins.

Accordingly, it can be said that the fact that there must be an increase in good deeds, etc., is from the perspective of the second matter of these days, but from the perspective of Hashem Himself, as He is above being King over a people, there is no place at all for the judgment and trial of the people, for that is only when He sits on the throne of His kingdom and judges the people. And in order to bring about this matter—that Hashem should lower Himself to be King over a people—this is done through the feeling among the people of their complete *bitul*, that even when, from the perspective of the "calculation" of merits and sins, the person is a *tzaddik* and merits in judgment (and it is obvious that he did *teshuvah* as required), nevertheless, he prays with supplications, and furthermore, he increases in *tzedakah* in order to merit the *tzedakah* of Hashem—and this brings about "so that you may crown Me over you".

E. Just as it is with emphasis and in the "King's Palace" during the Aseres Yemei Teshuvah, so too it is in miniature (in the "field") also in the *avodah* of the month of mercy and forgiveness—the month of Elul, which also contains these two matters:

On one hand, it is the month of mercy and forgiveness, to the point that "there are those who increase in saying *selichos* and supplications from Rosh Chodesh Elul and onwards" (similar to the words of the Rambam for the Aseres Yemei Teshuvah), and on the other hand, this pertains to meriting in judgment. As is emphasized in the language of the *Tur*, "And whoever increases in asking for mercy, it is a merit for him," which seemingly is a contradiction, for the content of asking for mercy is that even though he has no merits, he asks that Hashem have mercy on him, which is the opposite of "it is a merit for him." Rather, the way to merit during these days is through the feeling that he is always in need of asking for mercy from Hashem, that He should grant him *tzedakah* and a free gift.

And likewise, on the other hand, even in this, that they increase in the *avodah* of *teshuvah* and good deeds [as has been explained many times the hint for this—that in the initials of "Elul"...


## אלול

## Sicha 1

1. Regarding the blowing of the shofar in the month of Elul, the Rebbe Rayatz instructed (as printed in *Hayom Yom*), "During the day (the first day of Rosh Chodesh), one blows in order to practice, and one begins blowing after davening from the second day of Rosh Chodesh." Seemingly, this requires clarification: what is the meaning of "blowing in order to practice" during the first day of Rosh Chodesh? On the face of it, it is difficult to understand: if one does not know how to blow, it is obvious that one must practice (so what is this teaching us?) and subsequently, one will also blow on that same day; and if one already knows how to blow, then there is no need to blow (on the first day of Rosh Chodesh) "in order to practice." Simply put, one could say that the intention of the Rebbe Rayatz is to decide between the differing opinions as to when the blowing of Elul begins—whether on the first day of Rosh Chodesh Elul (30th of Menachem Av) or on the second day of Rosh Chodesh (1st of Elul) —and the decision is that the obligation of the Elul blowings begins from the second day of Rosh Chodesh. However, in order to satisfy those who hold that one begins on the first day of Rosh Chodesh, one "blows in order to practice"—meaning that one blows, but not as an obligation, only to practice. Yet, the phrasing "blows in order to practice" requires explanation, rather than simply "one blows," which would imply a custom, an obligation, or the like. Therefore, it appears that there is a unique dimension to a blowing that is "only to practice," and this blowing is relevant to the first day of Rosh Chodesh Elul, as will be explained.

2. Now, based on what has been explained regarding the conduct of "blowing in order to practice," we must examine a case where the first day of Rosh Chodesh Elul falls on Shabbos (as in this year [ednref_2_*]), when it is impossible to blow: If you say that the custom to blow for practice is as a preparation for the blowings that follow (from the second day of Rosh Chodesh), logic dictates that in such a calendar configuration, one should practice on Erev Rosh Chodesh. However, based on what was explained, that the (primary) intent of the blowing on this day (the first day of Rosh Chodesh) is to satisfy the opinions that this is the time the Elul blowings begin (since that is the beginning of the forty days that Moshe ascended the mountain), then there is no relevance to this blowing on Erev Rosh Chodesh Elul. Accordingly, in such a calendar configuration, the beginning of "blowing in order to practice" is missing. But simply put, one could say that there is no deficiency in this, for it is like any other Shabbos in the month of Elul, on which there is no blowing at all. In the inner dimension of the matter, this is understood based on the explanation of the Alter Rebbe regarding why we do not blow on Rosh Hashanah when it falls on Shabbos. The inner reason is that on Shabbos there is no need for the blowing of the shofar (in the community), because what is accomplished through the blowing of the shofar is accomplished automatically by the Shabbos itself (which is *mikdasha v'kayama*). The same applies to our case: the blowings of the month of Elul, and what those blowings were intended to accomplish on this Shabbos, is accomplished automatically by the Shabbos itself.

The simple reason is: the blowings of the month of Elul come to warn the people to perform teshuvah, and our Sages have said that Shabbos is an acronym for *Teshuvah*. This means that the day of Shabbos itself brings a person to teshuvah [although the teshuvah of Shabbos is a most exalted level, "Teshuvah Ila'ah," which is above the mention of sins, etc., as explained at length in *Iggeres HaTeshuvah* and elsewhere]. However, based on the above (at the end of section 1), it is not *glatt* (smooth) to explain it this way regarding the blowing of the first day of Rosh Chodesh Elul—because the reason for this blowing is not to warn to do teshuvah, but rather, as explicitly stated in the words of the Rebbe Rayatz, "to practice." How can it be said that Shabbos (by its own nature) accomplishes this "practice"?

3. The explanation for this can be given by first citing the explanation of the Alter Rebbe regarding the custom to blow one final blast after *Ne'ilah* on Yom Kippur. This blast is akin to the blowing of the "great shofar" of the future, for the blowings of Rosh Hashanah (which consist of one hundred sounds, in various types of sounds: *tekiah, shevarim, teruah*) are "shofar" in the general sense, but the great blast that is blown at the conclusion and seal of the Days of Awe, after the *Ne'ilah* prayer of Yom Kippur, which is one simple blast, is the ultimate refinement of the shofar—a reflection of the "great blast" of the future. The explanation of this: it is explained in many places that the inner content of the blowing of the shofar is the *kala penima'ah* (inner voice) of the soul, which is an inner and "simple" cry, a "simple sound" specifically. [And this is the (inner) explanation of the halachic ruling that "all sounds are valid for the shofar"—because the essence of the shofar is the simple sound specifically, and therefore the "configuration" of the sound is irrelevant, only the essence of the shofar's sound.] This is the concept of the "one blast" that is blown after the *Ne'ilah* prayer, which is one simple blast, for its essence is the inner cry from the essence of the soul, which is above any "configuration," even above the "configuration" of the *tekiah-shevarim-teruah* of Rosh Hashanah, which is also called a simple sound relative to the sounds of musical instruments [although to reach this simple sound of Yom Kippur, one must first blow the hundred sounds of Rosh Hashanah, and through this, one arrives later at the ultimate refinement of the shofar, the one blast of the *Ne'ilah* of Yom Kippur].

4. Now, according to the well-known principle of "general, particular, and general," just as there is a "general" that comes after the particulars, so too is there a "general" that comes before and as a preface to the particulars. It is the same in our case: just as after the particular hundred sounds of Rosh Hashanah one arrives at the "general"—the general content of the shofar—the one simple blast (a simple sound specifically) of the *Ne'ilah* of Yom Kippur, so too is it at the beginning of the forty days, where before the *avodah* of the particulars of the shofar blowing (including those of the month of Elul, where one blows ten sounds), there must be the starting point, the "general" that precedes the particulars, in which the general content of the particulars that follow is emphasized. It can be said that this is what is meant by "blowing in order to practice"—that it is analogous to the one blast blown at the conclusion of the forty days. As we see simply, when one begins to blow in order to practice, the beginning of the practice is that a simple sound emerges (and afterwards one learns the particular sounds of *tekiah, shevarim, teruah*)—for it is the point of the essence of "all the sounds" of the shofar, the simple sound that is above the hundred sounds of Rosh Hashanah.

5. Based on the above, that the intent of "blowing in order to practice" is not merely the practice of how to blow in the literal sense (which one could do even before the first day of Rosh Chodesh Elul), but primarily the beginning of the matter of the soul's cry, a simple sound—it is also understood regarding a calendar configuration where the first day of Rosh Chodesh Elul falls on Shabbos. Even though it is impossible to blow in the literal sense, the content of "blowing in order to practice" can, and therefore must, be fulfilled even on Shabbos. When a person performs his *avodah* in accordance with the inner content of the day of Shabbos—*Shabbos* being an acronym for *Teshuvah*—which is *Teshuvah Ila'ah* as mentioned above, he arrives at a simple sound from the essence of his soul. But if he knows in his soul that for some reason he did not perform this *avodah* on this Shabbos, he must perform it as mentioned. It can be said that when the first day of Rosh Chodesh Elul falls on Shabbos, just as Shabbos "helps" regarding Rosh Chodesh, so too is it the reverse: the unique quality of the day of the first of Rosh Chodesh Elul (the day Moshe ascended the mountain, according to the opinion that one begins blowing on the first day of Rosh Chodesh Elul) "helps," so that the *Teshuvah Ila'ah* of the day of Shabbos (in general) will be in a more exalted manner, reaching an inner cry from the essence of his soul, akin to the "great shofar" of the future.

(From the Sicha of the first day of Rosh Chodesh Elul, 5742)


## שופטים

## Sicha 1

A. Rashi at the beginning of our Parsha states: "Judges and officers (Shoftim v’Shotrim): Judges are the dayanim who issue the legal rulings, and officers are those who drive the people to follow their instructions, beating and binding them with a rod and a strap until they accept upon themselves the ruling of the judge."

Commentators have noted that Rashi’s addition—that "judges are the dayanim who issue the legal rulings"—is seemingly redundant, as it is well-known that every dayan issues rulings. They explain that Rashi intends to clarify why the Torah also requires "officers." Thus, he explains that the dayan only issues the verdict and nothing more, as he does not personally coerce the litigant; the officer, however, is the one who coerces and drives the person to fulfill the judge’s instructions.

However, this requires clarification : The role of an "officer"—even in the context of the judicial system—was already known from earlier in Parshas Devarim : "And I took the heads of your tribes, wise men... and officers for your tribes." There, in addition to the appointment of "judges" ("wise men..."), "officers for your tribes" were also appointed. As Rashi explains there: "And officers I appointed over you for your tribes; these are those who bind and beat with a strap according to the instruction of the dayanim." Why, then, was it necessary for Rashi to explain this again here?

Even more puzzling is that Rashi is more expansive here, in this second instance, than he was in Parshas Devarim. There, he wrote concisely: "These are those who bind and beat with a strap according to the instruction of the dayanim." Here, he adds: 1) At the beginning of the explanation: "who drive the people" (evidently intending to explain that the word "shoter" here is derived from the term *rediyah*—driving/ruling). 2) At the end of the explanation: "until they accept upon themselves the ruling of the judge."

There are also several changes in Rashi’s phrasing: (a) In Parshas Devarim he wrote "according to the instruction of the dayanim," whereas here he writes "following their instructions". (b) In Parshas Devarim he wrote "those who bind and beat," while here he reverses the order: "beating and binding". (c) In Parshas Devarim he mentioned only a "strap," while here he adds "with a rod and a strap".

B. Therefore, it seems that for Rashi, the "officers" here and the "officers" in Parshas Devarim are not one and the same; rather, they are two types of officers.

In Parshas Devarim, the "officers" refer to those who execute the Beis Din’s instructions in practice. The judges determine the punishment, and the officers are the ones who carry out the punishment.

In our Parsha, the "officers" refer to those whose role is to threaten the litigant so that he accepts the judge’s ruling. This is not about executing a Beis Din’s sentence, but about coercing the litigant to accept the obligation to fulfill the Beis Din’s ruling (for example, if a Beis Din ruled that a person is obligated to pay money to his fellow and he refuses to pay, the officers coerce him to pay the money).

With this, the differences in Rashi’s phrasing between the two locations are explained:

In Parshas Devarim, Rashi writes that the officers are "those who bind and beat... according to the instruction of the dayanim," meaning they are actively carrying out the instructions of the dayanim. Therefore, he wrote "those who bind and beat with a strap"—in accordance with the order of the punishment of lashes, where one first binds the person being lashed and then beats him with a strap.

In contrast, in our Parsha, Rashi writes that the officers are "those who drive the people... until they accept upon themselves the ruling of the judge." This is the second type of officer—those who coerce the litigant ("drive the people") "until they accept upon themselves the ruling of the judge." Therefore, he did not write "according to the instruction of the dayanim" here, but "following their instructions," meaning their role is not (only) to execute a Beis Din’s order ("the instruction of the dayanim"), but to coerce the litigant to accept the command (and authority) of the judges (in general). If he refuses to accept the judge’s command, the officers "beat and bind... until he accepts upon himself the ruling of the judge."

This also explains the reasons for the other changes in Rashi’s language:

a) In our Parsha, he wrote "beating and binding" (rather than "binding and beating" as in Parshas Devarim)—because here the intent is to coerce the litigant to accept the ruling. Rashi therefore lists the two modes of coercion from the lighter to the more severe: "beating (and if that does not suffice—) and binding" (meaning, placing him in restraints, etc.).

b) In Parshas Devarim, he mentioned only a "strap" and not [a rod]...

C. Therefore, Rashi explains here that the intent is those "who drive the people following their (the judges') instructions... until they accept upon themselves the ruling of the judge." These "drivers" are not related to the actual trial (neither to the ruling nor to the actual execution of the sentence), but to the driving of the people so that they will fear the judges and accept their rulings, as explained above at length.

D. However, one still needs to explain the other side:

Since it is possible to interpret "officers" as "those who drive the people," how does Rashi know to interpret "officers" in Parshas Devarim as "those who bind and beat... according to the instruction of the dayanim"? Especially since we find in several places (as mentioned above) that the judges themselves carry out the judgment in practice, how does Rashi know that there was a special appointment for such officers?

It can be said that Rashi hints at the necessity of his interpretation through the extra word in his language there: "And officers—I appointed over you for your tribes; these are those who bind..." Seemingly, the innovation in Rashi there is that the officers are a separate appointment (and not that the dayanim whom He gave as "heads over you" are themselves the officers). He should have said, "And officers—I appointed for your tribes." What is his intent with the word "over you" (*aleichem*)—is it not obvious that the appointment is over Bnei Yisrael?

Rather, with this, Rashi hints at the necessity of his continuation: "these are those who bind... according to the instruction of the dayanim." If the "officers" were merely "those who drive the people... until they accept upon themselves the ruling of the judge," then the content of this appointment would not be authority over Bnei Yisrael, but rather assistance to the judges [since, as it concerns a litigant who does not obey, he needs officers to threaten the litigant to accept the judge's ruling]. If so, the verse should have said, "And officers to (assist) your judges"?

Therefore, Rashi explains that the intent is "those who bind and beat... according to the instruction of the dayanim." Based on this, it is understood that this is an appointment over Bnei Yisrael ("over you"), because they are two parts of the appointment of judges (over Bnei Yisrael): 1) Wise men, etc.—who teach Bnei Yisrael the law; 2) The officers who execute and perform the judgment in practice.

The reason we do not find that "officers" were appointed in the days of Moshe Rabbeinu to drive the people to accept the judge's ruling can be explained as follows: (According to the *peshat*) in the days of Moshe, when the judges were appointed, they were not needed, because Bnei Yisrael feared the judges and did not require officers to threaten them. In contrast, our Parsha is a commandment for all generations, in any situation that may arise; therefore, they were commanded to appoint "officers"—"those who drive the people... until they accept upon themselves the ruling of the judge" (so that they would be prepared if the situation required a leadership of coercion).

E. Based on the above, the Gemara’s discussion in Maseches Sanhedrin can be explained:

("And these are the judgments) which you shall set before them"—should it not have said "which you shall teach them"?...These are the tools of the dayanim. When Rav Huna would go out to judge, he would say: "Bring me my tools: a rod, a strap, a shofar (for *shamta* and *nidui*—Rashi), and a sandal (for *chalitzah*—Rashi)." "And I commanded your judges at that time"—Rabbi Yochanan said: "Be diligent regarding the rod and the strap" (so that they cast fear upon the public for the sake of Heaven—Rashi).

One must explain: Why did Rabbi Yochanan mention only the "rod and the strap" and omit the other tools of the dayanim mentioned previously—the "shofar and the sandal"?

Regarding the "sandal," which is for *chalitzah*, it can be answered that it is not relevant to casting fear upon the public. But it is not understood why he omitted the "shofar".

Based on what we have established (in explaining Rashi’s approach to the Torah), it can be said that the two statements in the Gemara deal with two different matters:

The reference to "tools of the dayanim" refers to the tools needed for the activities of the Beis Din: a sandal for *chalitzah*—specifically in Beis Din; (and in Rashi in the Shas there) "a strap, for lashes; a rod, for the beating of rebellion, etc. ; a shofar, for *shamta* and *nidui*."

In contrast, the statement "be diligent regarding the rod and the strap" refers to the officers, whose role is to cast fear upon the public (in the language of Rashi in our Parsha—"those who drive the people... with a rod and a strap... until they accept upon themselves the ruling of the judge"). Therefore, he did not mention the "shofar" here for *nidui* and *shamta*, as those are not the actions of the officers, but of the dayanim.

[This is also the opinion of the Rambam, that the role of the "officers" (of our Parsha) is that the people should accept the judgment upon themselves. He writes : "Officers are those who possess a rod and a strap, and they stand before the dayanim, circulating in the markets, the streets, and the shops to regulate prices, etc., and to strike (or: to correct) any distortion... and anyone in whom they see a distortion of a matter, they bring him to the Beis Din, and they judge him according to his wickedness"—this indicates that these officers are not those who execute the verdict of the judges, but they are "those who possess a rod and a strap... circulating in the markets and the streets" to correct the conduct of the people and bring "a distortion of a matter" before the Beis Din (and the Beis Din "judges him according to his wickedness")].

F. In this way, another question regarding the commandment to appoint "judges and officers" is simply resolved:

All those who count the mitzvos counted the appointment of judges and officers as a single mitzvah, even though they are seemingly two separate acts—the appointment of judges and the appointment of officers (as emphasized in the language of the Braisa : "From where do we know that we appoint judges for Israel? The Torah says 'Judges you shall give.' From where do we know [we appoint] officers for Israel? The Torah says 'Officers you shall give'"). Why were they not counted as two mitzvos?

Some explain that judges and officers are mutually dependent, and therefore they are counted as one mitzvah (as the Rambam writes that if the parts of a mitzvah are mutually dependent, they are counted as one mitzvah). They bring proof from what is said in the Sifrei : "If there are officers, there are judges; if there are no officers, there are no judges." And in the Tanchuma : "If there is no officer, there is no judge. How so? Once a person is found liable in Beis Din to his fellow, if there is no officer to extract it from him, once he departs from the dayan, the dayan has no power to do anything for him unless he hands him over to the officer." Thus, "through both of these together, the judgment is completed."

However, it is forced to explain it this way, for in practice, it is not mentioned in the Rambam that judges and officers are mutually dependent. Simply put, even if one cannot (for whatever reason) appoint officers, one is still obligated to appoint judges.

[Simply put, the intent of the Sifrei (and the Tanchuma) is not a legal requirement that "if there are no officers, there are no judges," but only regarding the utility of the matter—that without officers, it is possible that the judicial process will not be brought to fruition. But in practice, one is obligated to appoint judges even without officers. It is possible that the judicial process will be brought to fruition even without the (threat of) officers. Furthermore, and primarily, there is a need and necessity for "judges" to provide instruction in all matters of Torah (most of which are not related to punishment, etc.).]

Seemingly, it can be explained based on the words of the Rambam (regarding *techeiles* and white threads, which are counted as one mitzvah even though they are not mutually dependent) "that even parts that are not mutually dependent are sometimes..."

(As "judges," in the simple sense, also have the role of executing their judgment, as mentioned above). The reason can be said to be that "the ultimate purpose of wisdom is teshuvah and good deeds". If the study does not lead to action, it is lacking (not only in action, but) in the study itself, as explained elsewhere.

According to this interpretation, the role of the "officers" is when a person’s evil inclination overcomes him and there is a need to crush it so that it does not distract him from Torah and avodah. Then he needs an "officer"—to arouse his good inclination against his evil inclination, and to "strike it" (with the striking of words) "saying to it, 'You are evil, wicked, loathsome, abominable, and vile, etc.'", through which the control of the evil inclination is nullified.

Based on this, the language of the prophecy "And I will restore your judges as at first, etc." is understood—"officers" are not mentioned—because then the evil will be nullified, and there will be no need for "officers," only for "judges"—the study of Torah and the fulfillment of mitzvos, "going from strength to strength" within holiness itself.

(From a Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Shoftim, 5725)

## Sicha 2

### 106

1. The Rambam writes: It is a *gezeiras hakasuv* (a scriptural decree) that a *beis din* may not execute or flog a person based on their own confession; it must be based on two witnesses. This means that even though we hold that a litigant’s admission is equivalent to a hundred witnesses, this applies only to monetary obligations, but not to incurring the death penalty or lashes through one’s own confession.

The Radbaz writes there: "It is possible to provide a slight rationale: because a person’s soul is not his own property, but the property of Hashem, as it is stated, 'The souls are Mine.' Therefore, his admission is ineffective regarding something that is not his. Lashes are half of a death penalty, but his money is his own, and that is why we say that a litigant’s admission is equivalent to a hundred witnesses. And yet, I admit that this is a decree of the King of the World, and one should not question it."

Seemingly, one must analyze the words of the Radbaz: How are souls, which are the property of Hashem, different from the fact that the entire world is "the property of Hashem," as it is written, "To Hashem belongs the earth and its fullness"—or in the language of the *Chinuch*, "Everything is under the authority of the Master of all"—which includes a person’s money, as the verse states, "The silver is Mine and the gold is Mine, says Hashem"?

We must therefore conclude that even though the entire world is in the ownership of Hashem, there is nevertheless a fundamental difference between one’s soul and one’s money regarding the authority of the human being.

2. The explanation for this is as follows:
A person’s soul and life were entrusted by Hashem to the person only as a deposit, not as his own property. As our *Admur* rules in his *Shulchan Aruch*, "A person has no authority over his own body at all to strike it, etc." Based on this, the fact that a person is warned not to damage his body is not merely a matter of prohibition, but also a matter of the laws of ownership—that he has no authority (*ba'alus*) over his body (because the body is the property of Hashem, which resides with the person as a deposit).

## 107

This is not the case with a person’s money, which was entrusted to the person in such a way that he becomes the owner of this money (similar to what is stated, "And the earth He gave to the children of man"). The fact that "one must be very careful with his wealth not to lose it, ruin it, or damage it" is not because the money is not his, but rather it is a prohibition, as he would be violating the negative commandment of *bal tashchis* (do not destroy).

This does not contradict the verse "The silver is Mine and the gold is Mine, says Hashem" (and similar verses)—because Hashem’s ownership, which is a consequence of the fact that He, may He be blessed, created the world and sustains it, is a general ownership. Even after money is given to a person, it does not leave the ownership of Hashem, to the point that He can take it from him. [Similar to the language of Rashi (regarding the land, and Eretz Yisrael in particular): "The entire land belongs to Hashem; He created it and gave it to whomever He saw fit. By His will He gave it to them, and by His will He took it from them and gave it to us"—for even though initially "He gave it to them" as a complete gift, subsequently "by His will He took it from them and gave it to us"].

Since a person has ownership over his money, the law regarding it (the money) is that "a litigant’s admission is equivalent to a hundred witnesses." This is not the case with anything concerning his body and soul, which remain entirely in the ownership of Hashem and are found with the person only as a deposit. Therefore, the person is not considered the "owner" of them, and thus we do not obligate him to lashes or the like based on his own word.

3. We can bring proof for this distinction between a person’s soul (and body) and his money from the laws of *berachos* (blessings of enjoyment):
Our Sages said (and it is brought as *halacha*), "It is forbidden for a person to derive benefit from this world without a blessing, and anyone who derives benefit from this world without a blessing has committed *me'ilah*... as if he derived benefit from the consecrated property of Heaven (*kodshei shamayim*), as it is stated, 'To Hashem belongs the earth and its fullness.'"

However, one is only obligated in *berachos* on something that his body derives benefit from (eating, drinking, etc.). This is not the case when a person derives monetary benefit

## 108

(receiving money, etc.); no blessing was instituted for this.
Seemingly, this is difficult to understand: How is bodily benefit different from monetary benefit? In both cases, the person is deriving benefit from this world, regarding which it is said, as mentioned above, "To Hashem belongs the earth and its fullness." Why does he not commit *me'ilah* by "deriving benefit from the consecrated property of Heaven" when he derives monetary benefit without a blessing?

[Although we find various *mitzvos* performed with a person’s assets, the purpose of which is (as explained in the books on the reasons for the *mitzvos*) to instill in the person the recognition that "To Hashem belongs the earth and its fullness" (such as the *mitzvah* of *Shemitah* (this year), the essence of which is "that he should see from man's actions... as if nothing is specifically in his possession, but rather everything is under the authority of the Master of all"; and likewise priestly gifts, tithes, etc.—the content of which is "so that he may know that everything is His")—it is nevertheless understood and simple that this is not at all similar to the *berachos* of enjoyment, for the *mitzvah* is that the person, of his own volition, declares his field ownerless during *Shemitah*, etc.

It should be noted that these *mitzvos* concern only a portion of a person’s assets, whereas the law of *berachos* of enjoyment is that it is forbidden to derive any benefit (even the slightest amount) without a blessing. Seemingly, this is not relevant to our discussion.]

Based on what we explained earlier—regarding the difference in the definition of a person’s ownership over his money versus his soul and body—we can say:
Regarding a person’s soul and body, which are "the property of Hashem" in such a way that the person has no ownership over them, their enjoyments are likewise like them—the property of Hashem. Therefore, they are "consecrated property of Heaven" that it is forbidden to enjoy without a blessing.

The fact that it is stated in the *Gemara* that only before the blessing are they "consecrated property of Heaven," but after the blessing they are in a state of "And the earth He gave to the children of man" ("It is for the children of man")—does not mean that through the blessing they were transformed into the person’s property (for even afterwards we say, "Blessed is He from Whose [food] we have eaten"). Rather, it means that through

## 109

the blessing, the status of "consecrated property of Heaven" (which is forbidden for benefit) is removed, and it becomes permitted to derive benefit from them. However, he is not deriving benefit from his own, but from Hashem’s, and Hashem gives them to him in a way that it should be his, even to the point that he may eat (consume) them, etc.

This is not the case with a person’s assets and money, which (in and of themselves) are not in the category of "consecrated property of Heaven" from the outset, because they were entrusted to the person’s ownership. Therefore, no blessing is needed to permit monetary benefit [except that in order for the person to recognize that his ownership does not, Heaven forbid, negate the (general) ownership of Hashem over his money, *mitzvos* were given to him regarding his money, etc., "so that he may know that everything is His" (as mentioned above)].

4. But seemingly, this itself requires a reason: How is a person’s money different, in that it is given to him in a way that he has ownership over it, while his soul and body remain "the property of Hashem," to the point that "a person has no authority over them at all"?
Seemingly, one could say that this is the intent of the Radbaz at the end of his words: "And yet, I admit that this is a decree of the King of the World, and one should not question it," because the aforementioned difference between a person’s soul and his money is not an intellectual matter. Rather, it is a "decree of the King of the World" that He granted authority and ownership to man over his money, but not over his soul and body.

However, in the language of the Radbaz, "It is possible to provide a slight rationale" for this as well:
From Hashem’s perspective, there is no difference at all between a person’s soul and body and his money, because the entire world and its fullness belong to Hashem equally. But there is a practical difference between them from the perspective of the person:
Regarding his body and soul—they were given to him in a way that Hashem’s ownership is recognizable in them, for every day he says, "The soul that You have placed within me," he is obligated in *Torah* and *mitzvos*, etc., and the holiness of the soul is felt in his body. And even in the body

## 110

of a Jewish person, there is holiness. Since they were created in a way that Hashem’s ownership is recognizable in them, it is impossible for the person to have the status of ownership over them.
This is not the case with his money, which is in a state where it lacks (this) holiness and Hashem’s ownership is not recognizable in it. And since the *Torah* was given specifically down here on earth (in a place where Hashem’s ownership, as mentioned above, is not recognizable), therefore, in the laws of the *Torah*, a person has authority and ownership over his money and assets [except that he must know and recognize that this ownership itself was given to him from Heaven (Hashem), for "To Hashem belongs the earth and its fullness," as mentioned above].

5. Based on this, we can explain another distinction between monetary law and capital law:
"By *Torah* law, monetary law and capital law are the same regarding *drishah v'chakirah* (inquisitorial examination of witnesses)... And what is the reason they said that monetary law does not require *drishah v'chakirah*? So as not to lock the door before borrowers" ("that they will refrain from lending, lest he deny it and the witnesses make a mistake, and their testimony will not be precise, and he will lose").

Now, simply put—the reason for this is that by "Torah law," monetary law also requires *drishah v'chakirah*, and the *Torah* was not concerned about "locking the door before borrowers."
In general—the reason for the multitude of matters that the Sages made as a fence for the *Torah*, and for the decrees of the Sages, is that they were concerned about stumbling blocks and errors, etc., whereas by *Torah* law, there is no such concern.
One could say this is because the laws of the *Torah* are based on the principle that all Jews are presumed to be righteous (*b'chezkat kashrut*), and their conduct is consistent with this, and there is no room for concern, etc., and in our case, for the concern that the borrower "might deny it" and the lender will need witnesses.
But when the number of deceivers increased, etc., and there is a concern that the borrower might deny it, etc., then the Sages saw a need to enact ordinances so that "you will not lock the door before borrowers." And similarly regarding all the concerns and decrees—when they saw that the earlier generations were like angels and the later ones like human beings, and if the earlier ones were like human beings, the later ones, etc.

It could be said that the change in the situation due to the decline of the generations is (not only in that the number of deceitful borrowers increased, etc., but) also from the perspective of the lender—a concern that the lender will refrain from lending because he is afraid "lest the borrower deny it," etc.
But from the perspective of "Torah law" (which relates to the state of a Jewish person as he should be, like angels and like human beings), there is no room for this concern.

## 111

One could explain this matter:
From the perspective of "Torah law"—a person’s entire wealth is not entirely in his authority and ownership, but only that part which he uses for permitted matters (in the language of the *Mishnah*, "all your deeds"). Since it was entrusted to him to use as he wishes (for matters permitted according to the *Shulchan Aruch*), it is in his authority and ownership. This is not the case with the money with which a person must fulfill *mitzvos*; from the outset, it was not given to the person’s ownership, and it is only with him as a deposit (like his soul) so that he can fulfill the desires of Heaven with it. Similar to the explanation of the Rebbe Rayatz regarding *tzedakah* (and loan money): "That which the wealthy man grants to the poor man is not his at all; it is Hashem’s, and it is with him as a deposit for a set time and period."

Therefore, there is no room then for the concern that "you will lock the door before borrowers"—because when the opportunity to fulfill the *mitzvah* of *gemilut chasadim* (through a loan) comes his way, he will certainly hasten to fulfill Hashem’s command to lend to the poor man, and he will not refrain from lending due to the concern that "he might deny it," since he knows and recognizes that the money is not his, but Hashem’s, Who commanded him to lend to the poor (and brought this poor man to him).

One could say that this is the hint in the statement, "By *Torah* law, monetary law and capital law are the same, etc.": When a person’s standing is as it should be ("Torah law"), he feels that there is truly no difference between the matters of his soul and his money, knowing that even the money given to his authority is Hashem’s.
But after the decline of the generations, etc. (that the world also "descended" and became more material), the state of the person (and the world) changed in such a way that there is a difference between "capital law" and "monetary law." He does not feel that his money is not his, but Hashem’s, and therefore, "they said that monetary law does not require *drishah v'chakirah* so as not to lock the door before borrowers."

6. Based on this, another detail in this *halacha* becomes clear:
The *halacha* that monetary law does not require *drishah v'chakirah* applies, according to many opinions, not only to loans and admissions (where the concern of "locking the door before borrowers" applies), but also to "robberies, assaults, a wife’s *ketubah*, inheritances, and gifts—in all of these, we also do not require *drishah v'chakirah*, so as not to lock the door before robbers" (who would rob based on the assumption that witnesses who coordinate their testimony will not be found).

Seemingly, according to these opinions, one must ask: Why did the *Shas* cite the reason of "not locking the door before borrowers" (which applies only to loans and admissions), and not (also) the reason of "not locking the door before robbers" (especially since this reason also applies to loans and admissions—to lock the door before one who wants to deny a loan based on the assumption that coordinated testimony will not be found)?
Based on the above, one can say that in this, we are being taught a novelty regarding "Torah law"—that in the conduct required by "Torah law," not only is there no room for the concern of robbery and denial, etc., but furthermore, there is no difference

## 112

for the person (the lender) between "capital law" and "monetary law," because he recognizes that his money is not his, but Hashem’s.
This is emphasized by the reason of "not locking the door before borrowers" (that he does not lock the door before the borrower due to concern, etc.), as explained at length above.

7. It is written, "You are My witnesses, says Hashem," that the Jewish people testify, as it were, to His existence, may He be blessed, in the world.
The matter of testimony is to reveal something hidden. For regarding something visible to all, there is no need for testimony, and even regarding something that will be revealed in the future, the *Torah* did not require testimony. Rather, testimony is for "something completely hidden."

So too in our case: The fact that the Jewish people are called "witnesses" (of Hashem) is because their task is to reveal Hashem’s existence even in the matters of the world, that even in the "world" (*olam*, related to *he'elem*—concealment), which conceals and hides Hashem’s ownership—to the point that according to *Torah*, its matters were given to the authority and ownership of man—one must reveal that everything exists only "from the truth of His existence", and therefore, in truth, there is no (real) existence other than Him, may He be blessed, "there is none else besides Him".

As is known, the story of one of the great Chassidim of the *Admur* Hazaken (R' Binyamin Kletzker), who was a timber merchant. Once, when calculating his business dealings, etc., upon reaching the "total" of the entire calculation, he wrote: "There is none else besides Him."
It is understood and simple that the fact that the aforementioned Chassid wrote this is because that is how he truly felt; that this was the total of the business he conducted in practice. That is,
the matter of "there is none else besides Him" was permeated throughout his entire existence and all his affairs. Therefore, at the moment he reached the "total" in his business affairs, he wrote the (absolute) truth: "There is none else besides Him."

And more deeply—starting with the question: Since the matter of "there is none else besides Him" was always engraved on the heart of the aforementioned Chassid—why did he write it only on the last line of the calculation, while in the previous lines he recorded the details of the numbers and goods, etc., as usual? He should have written it in every single line (instead of the numbers of the goods, etc.)!
The explanation for this—that on the contrary, this is the novelty (in the aforementioned story):

## 113

Certainly, it is a fact that every one of the Jewish people (who are presumed to be righteous) has the ability to feel that "there is none else besides Him," by contemplating well how "all existences... exist only from the truth of His existence" and the like. However, he can reach this recognition when he is contemplating, etc., during prayer and study, while he is separated and removed from the affairs of the world. But when a person is busy with business affairs, etc., which by the nature of the world conceal and hide His existence, may He be blessed, it is difficult for him to feel the matter of "there is none else besides Him" at that time.

This is the emphasis in the aforementioned story: that the Chassid was then, according to the command to engage in his livelihood, in a state where he wrote all the details of his accounts precisely, etc. That is, his occupation then was not in matters of contemplating the greatness of the Almighty, etc., but in business affairs. However, because he was entirely permeated with the recognition of "there is none else besides Him," even while doing this calculation, the recognition came to complete revelation in the conclusion of the calculation, that the "total" and the purpose of all his business and affairs is—"there is none else besides Him".

Through this *avodah*, to reveal in the entire world that there is a Ruler to this palace, to the point of the recognition that "there is none else besides Him"—we merit a standing and state where there will be no need for the matter of "testimony" mentioned above. For the prophecy will be fulfilled: "And the glory of Hashem shall be revealed, and all flesh together shall see that the mouth of Hashem has spoken," in the true and complete Redemption through *Mashiach Tzidkeinu*, speedily and in our days, truly.

## Chapter 3

### 114

A. The Rambam writes in *Hilchos Melachim* : "King Moshiach is destined to arise and restore the kingdom of the House of David to its former state, etc. And anyone who does not believe in him, or who does not await his coming, denies not only the other prophets, but the Torah and Moshe Rabbeinu, for the Torah has testified about him, as it is written : 'Then Hashem your G-d will return your captivity and have mercy on you, and He will return and gather you, etc. If your outcasts are at the ends of the heavens, etc., Hashem your G-d will bring you.' And these things that are explicitly stated in the Torah include all the matters that were said by all the prophets. Even in the *Parshah* of Bilaam it is said, and there he prophesied about the two *Moshiachs*: the first *Moshiach*, who is David, etc., and the final *Moshiach* who will arise from his descendants, etc." (He then brings several verses from the *Parshah* of Bilaam and interprets them as referring to David and King Moshiach).

In the subsequent *halacha*, the Rambam continues: "Also regarding the cities of refuge, it says : 'If Hashem your G-d expands your border, and you add for yourself three more cities, etc.' This has never occurred, and the Holy One, blessed be He, did not command in vain. However, regarding the words of the prophets, there is no need for proof, for all the books are filled with this matter."

One must examine: what was lacking in the initial proofs (from the verse "Then Hashem... will return" in *Parshas Nitzavim* and from the *Parshah* of Bilaam) that necessitated the Rambam to add the proof from the verse regarding the "cities of refuge"?

The necessity for the first two proofs is understood, because the Rambam’s intent here (in *Hilchos Melachim*) is regarding the *belief* in King Moshiach. That is to say, in addition to the obligation to believe in the general concept of the *Geulah*—that the Holy One, blessed be He, is destined to redeem Israel (in whatever manner)—one is obligated to believe in King Moshiach (with the details of his affairs and his virtues as specified by the Rambam in this chapter). This is as the Rambam’s language precisely states: "And anyone who does not believe in *him*." This is not explicitly stated in the verse "Then Hashem your G-d will return your captivity... and gather you... and Hashem will bring you," but only in the "Parshah of Bilaam," where "there he prophesied about the two *Moshiachs*," David and King Moshiach. [For this reason, he also brings an extensive proof, with several verses that speak of them, because these verses also hint at the details of the affairs and virtues of King Moshiach (which one is obligated to believe in), as explained elsewhere].

On the other hand, he was not satisfied with the proof from the *Parshah* of Bilaam. Furthermore, he brought it only as a second proof. This is because the Rambam’s intent here is not merely to bring proofs from the Written Torah regarding the coming of

### 115

Moshiach, but to prove that "anyone who does not believe in him... denies... the Torah and Moshe Rabbeinu, for the Torah has testified about him." This testimony is not only according to the interpretation of the verses of the Torah found in the Oral Torah [for even "one who denies its interpretation, which is the Oral Torah, is included in the category of 'denying the Torah'"], but rather "these things are explicitly stated in the Torah". The prophecies of Bilaam, however, were stated by way of parable and riddle, in language that cannot be said to be "things explicitly stated in the Torah". This is unlike the first verse, in which the matter of *Geulah*, the ingathering of the exiles, etc., is explicitly stated.

However, it requires explanation (as above): what is added by the proof from the verse regarding the cities of refuge ?

It should also be noted that the Rambam wrote this proof in a separate *halacha*. Seemingly, he should have included it in the previous *halacha* together with the first two proofs.

B. It appears that in this proof from the cities of refuge, there is a fundamental innovation regarding the first two proofs: by the fact that the Torah commanded, "If He expands... and you add..." (that in the days of Moshiach we are obligated to add to the cities of refuge, in the words of the Rambam : "In the days of King Moshiach, we add three others to these six"), the matter of the coming of Moshiach becomes one of the conditions of a *mitzvah* in the Torah.

That is to say: even though the matter of the future *Geulah* is explicitly stated in the Torah (and the Rambam, in his *Commentary on the Mishnah*, counted the belief in this among the foundations and principles of the Torah), it is nevertheless not a part of the *mitzvos* of the Torah. For we do not find in the Torah a command to believe in the *Geulah* (and it is only because it is a matter explicitly stated in the Torah that anyone who does not believe in it is a "denier... of the Torah and Moshe Rabbeinu"). But through the command of "If He expands... and you add," the matter of the *Geulah* becomes one of the details of a *mitzvah* in the Torah.

And by becoming a part of a *mitzvah* in the Torah, it also possesses the strength inherent in the *mitzvos*. As the

### 116

Rambam writes in *Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah* : "It is a clear and explicit matter in the Torah that it is a *mitzvah* that stands forever and for all eternity; it has no change, no subtraction, and no addition." "Mitzvah" means the commands of the Torah, as the continuation of the Rambam’s words states: "For we are commanded to perform all the words of the Torah forever." He wrote similarly in *Hilchos Melachim* there, in the continuation of the matter regarding King Moshiach: "The essence of the matter is thus: this Torah, its statutes and its judgments, are for all time and for all eternity, and we do not add to them nor subtract from them (and anyone who adds or subtracts, or who reveals facets in the Torah and removes the matters of the *mitzvos* from their literal meaning, is certainly a liar, a wicked person, and an *epikoros*)."

Therefore, since the matter of the *Geulah* is a detail in the *mitzvah* of the cities of refuge, just as the cities of refuge "is a *mitzvah* that stands forever, etc., it has no change, etc.," so too it is impossible for there to be a change, *chas v'shalom*, in this promise.

Based on this, the precision of the Rambam’s language in this proof, "and the Holy One, blessed be He, did not command in vain", is also understood—that his intent is to emphasize that since this is a matter of a *mitzvah* ("the Holy One, blessed be He, commanded"), it necessitates that it is impossible for there to be a change, *chas v'shalom*, in this, because "the Holy One, blessed be He, did not command in vain."

C. For further clarification:
Regarding the promises of the Holy One, blessed be He (through a prophet), there are certain modes in which a change can occur, such as "words of retribution that the prophet says," which can be annulled because "the Holy One, blessed be He, is slow to anger and abundant in kindness, etc. [or because] it is possible that they did *teshuvah* and were forgiven". Likewise, even regarding a promise of good, if it was only "between the Holy One, blessed be He, and the prophet," it is possible that it will not be fulfilled if "sin causes it".

From this it is understood that even when it is clear that the prophet’s promise will be fulfilled [as the Rambam writes : "Any matter of good that the Almighty decrees, even if on a condition, does not return," and as he explained in his *Commentary on the Mishnah* that when "the Holy One, blessed be He, tells a prophet to promise people good tidings," the matter must be fulfilled, and it is not possible for it to be annulled due to sin]—this is not because a promise made through a prophet is inherently incapable of change, but because these promises do not actually undergo change (as explained in the Rambam there, that this is so that one can test if he is a true prophet).

### 117

This is unlike the eternity of the Torah, which "stands forever and for all eternity," which is because it is above the category of change. That is to say, it is impossible for it to change, for just as "I, Hashem, have not changed", in that change is not applicable to Him, *yisbarach*, *chas v'shalom*, so too in the Torah of the Holy One, blessed be He (which is His wisdom and His will), the category of change is not applicable.

From this it is understood regarding the promise of the *Geulah*: even though, because it is a promise of good and what the Holy One, blessed be He, told the prophet "to promise people good tidings" cannot be annulled due to the cause of sin, as mentioned above [and furthermore, from the outset it is not applicable in this case that sin would cause it, for (in the words of the Rambam) "the Torah has already promised that Israel will eventually do *teshuvah* at the end of their exile and immediately they will be redeemed, as it is written : 'And it shall be when all these things come upon you... and you return to Hashem your G-d... and Hashem your G-d will return, etc.'"; that is to say, we were promised on this very thing, that Israel will do *teshuvah* (and there will not be a situation where "sin causes it")]—nevertheless, the certainty of this is only from the perspective of the parameters and modes of prophecy. This is unlike the fact that the promise of the *Geulah* is a part and detail of a *mitzvah* in the Torah, which causes the parameter of the eternity of the Torah to apply to it—an eternity in which no annulment is applicable.

D. However, seemingly there is still room for the question:
The rule of the eternity of the Torah applies to all parts of the Torah, and especially to the Five Books of the Torah, as the Rambam writes that "the Five Books of the Torah... are never annulled," that everything written in the Torah of Moshe is eternal and exists forever. If so, since the promise of the Holy One, blessed be He, regarding the *Geulah* is explicitly stated in the Torah of Moshe, the parameter of the eternity of the "Five Books of the Torah" already applies to it.
