This is called (in the Zohar) "Teshuvah Ila'ah," and this teshuvah is not born of bitterness, but "with great joy".

Accordingly, we must say that the words of mussar in Parshas Vayelech, even though they literally pertain to teshuvah for sins, are not only not in contradiction to the feeling of joy, but on the contrary, they awaken "great joy."

A hint to this can be found in the fact that the mussar in Parshas Vayelech is connected to the writing of the song of Ha'azinu. Although the content of the song is words of mussar, "so that the people will return in teshuvah", it is called a "song" (shirah), which denotes joy (to the extent that this parsha is from the song of the Leviim over the korban, and a song is only recited over wine). This is because the mussar of Parshas Ha'azinu brings a person to joy, as has been explained at length elsewhere.

Based on this, we can explain (by way of hint) the precision of Rashi’s language in our parsha regarding the words of mussar—"And My anger will flare... and I will abandon them and I will hide My face from them..."—where he explains "and I will hide My face" as "as if I do not see their distress."

With this, Rashi hints that even when the conduct of Yisrael causes "And My anger will flare...

### 195

and I will abandon them and I will hide My face," Hashem does not truly abandon Bnei Yisrael, chas v'shalom. Rather, it is only "as if I do not see," but the truth is that Hashem sees and feels the distress of Yisrael. Furthermore, "in all their distress, He is distressed", and He is present in that very distress. On the contrary, the entire intent of the concealment of the face is only to awaken Bnei Yisrael to return to Hashem, as will be explained at length in section 6. This emphasizes the magnitude of Hashem's love for Bnei Yisrael in every state they are in.

This is also the connection and relevance to the Aseres Yemei Teshuvah, as explained by our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe regarding the saying of our Sages that during the Aseres Yemei Teshuvah, Hashem is "close" to Bnei Yisrael: "He is called close, yet He has distanced Himself and hidden from us. It is like a son who is with his father in his house, but the father hides his face from him so that the son will take to heart the love, and his love will be revealed with greater intensity. When the son realizes that the father is right there and is hiding his face from him, his love will certainly grow in a state of revelation."

**B.** It has been explained many times that in addition to the hints in Rashi’s commentary on the Torah, his primary task is to explain the simple meaning of the verse (*peshuto shel mikra*).

In the present case, we must understand Rashi’s interpretation that "and I will hide My face" means "as if I do not see their distress."

The Re'em explains that "the concealment of Hashem's face is the absence of His providence, and it is called 'hiding the face' because anyone whose face does not see a thing does not pay attention to it. This is what the Rabbi [Rashi] intended by saying 'as if I do not see their distress.'"

However, this requires explanation: Seemingly, the simple interpretation of the concealment of the face from Above is an expression of a state of anger, etc.—the opposite of the blessing "May Hashem shine His face upon you", which denotes Hashem's joy in Bnei Yisrael, as Rashi explains there: "He will show you a smiling face, a bright face."

Just like the conduct of a father with his son: when the father shows his son a smiling face, it indicates the revelation of his love for him, and when he is angry at him, he hides his face from him.

Why did Rashi abandon this interpretation for the concealment of the face from Above—which is (a) consistent with the beginning of the verse, "My anger will flare," and (b) something that pertains to the general concept of "face"—and instead interpret it as referring to the absence of His providence, which pertains (only) to the "eyes of Hashem" (and not to the general concept of "face")?

### 196

**C.** We also need to explain the precision of Rashi’s language, "as if I do not see *their distress*." Seemingly, since Rashi's intent is to explain the concealment of Hashem's face as the absence of providence, he should have said "as if I do not see *them*." Why does he limit the absence of sight (and providence) only to "their distress"?

This implies and suggests that Rashi does not intend the absence of providence that is the *cause* of the evils and distress [as explained in the Moreh Nevuchim and other commentators, that when Bnei Yisrael are in a state of concealment of the face and absence of providence, they are left to "chance," and consequently, evils and distress befall them, as the verse continues, "Because my G-d is not in my midst, these evils have found me"]. Rather, he refers to the absence of providence *after* the evils and distress have already come, such that even then, Hashem acts "as if I do not see their distress" (meaning, not that He does not provide for Bnei Yisrael, but that He does not see the distress, and consequently does not turn to their cry, etc., to save them).

[Based on this, Rashi’s language "that I do not see" is also precise. Seemingly, he should have said "that I do not provide" (which is the main point). Why does Rashi say "that I do not see," leaving the student to understand that he intends the absence of providence, "because anyone whose face does not see a thing does not pay attention to it"?

Based on the above, it is understood that Rashi does not intend the absence of Hashem's providence over Bnei Yisrael, which is the cause of the distress (to which the term "I do not provide" would apply), but rather the absence of seeing the distress (that has already come), as mentioned above.]

This is puzzling: The verse explicitly states, "And I will hide My face from them, and it will be for consumption, and many evils and distresses will find them," meaning the concealment of Hashem's face comes *before* the state of "it will be for consumption and they will find them." On the contrary, it is the *cause* of the evils. Yet Rashi interprets it the opposite way, that "and I will hide My face" is *after* they have found the evils and distress?

Seemingly, one could say this is because in the divine punishments we have learned in the verse until now, we do not find (in the simple meaning of the verse) that they come due to an absence of providence. On the contrary, the punishment of the wicked comes specifically through His providence, as He judges the wicked and sentences them to the appropriate punishment. Furthermore, a severe punishment for a severe sin is described in the verse (not with the term "hiding the face," but) with the term "I will set My face" (and the like), which means, as Rashi explains, "I will turn away from all My affairs and occupy Myself with him," which is the intensity of providence. Therefore, in this case as well, Rashi does not interpret "and I will hide My face" as the cause of the punishment.

However, this is not a sufficient excuse, for one could say that here we are dealing with a punishment more severe than those mentioned until now, as explained by the commentators, that this is "measure for measure": "Corresponding to the abandonment with which they abandoned Hashem [as written in the previous verse, 'and has abandoned Me and broken My covenant'], He said

### 197

'and I will abandon them and I will hide My face from them,' meaning, I too will abandon them and not provide for them. This decree is harsher than all other decrees... In all the curses of the covenant, you will not find the concealment of the face nor abandonment, only providential punishments. Here, He decrees as a recompense for their abandonment that He will abandon them and hide His face from them."

**D.** We must also understand: why does Rashi add the word "as" ("as if I do not see...")?

The commentators write that Rashi is bothered by the question: Is there, chas v'shalom, anything hidden from Him? Therefore, he explains "as if I do not see."

But seemingly, this is not enough, as this emphasis is superfluous. Even if Rashi had written "that I do not see their distress," it would be understood that he does not mean it literally—that the matter is hidden from Him—but rather that Hashem is not providing for them to save them.

As we find explicitly in Rashi’s commentary earlier in Parshas Shemos, on the verse "And Elokim saw..."—"He did not avert His eyes." And similarly on the verse "I have surely seen the affliction of My people... I have known their pains"—"I have not averted My eyes and I will not seal My ears from their cry." Seemingly, how could "averting the eyes" apply to Him such that he needed to negate it with "I have not averted My eyes"? It must be that it is self-evident that averting the eyes means the absence of providence (to the point that Rashi did not need to explain it).

Furthermore, and most importantly: even if you say that "I do not see their distress" is not appropriate language for Above because it literally implies the matter is hidden from Him, Rashi should have written (similar to his language in Parshas Shemos mentioned above) "and I will hide My face—I will avert My eyes" (especially since this is the language of the verse).

**E.** The explanation for this is simple:

The verse states two things—"and I will abandon them" (and afterwards) "and I will hide My face from them." One must understand: after Hashem abandons Bnei Yisrael, chas v'shalom, and is not present with them, what is the addition afterwards that He will also hide His face from them? It should have been the opposite: "and I will hide My face from them" (which applies even when He is in their midst), and furthermore—"and I will abandon them"?

With a human being, one could say that first he leaves the place (but can still look at it from afar), and afterwards he adds even more, hiding his face so as not to look (even) from afar.

But with Hashem, where "and I will abandon them" means the absence of providence—is the absence of providence described by the term "and I will abandon them" (which denotes total abandonment) not much greater than the absence of providence of "and I will (only) hide My face"?

From this, Rashi concludes that the intent of the verse here is two types of punishment: (a) "and I will abandon them"—the absence of providence that causes distress and evils

### 198

(as the verse continues, "Because my G-d is not in my midst [i.e., 'and I will abandon them'], these evils have found me"); (b) "and I will hide My face from them," which is after the distress has already come, where Hashem conducts Himself with Yisrael "as if I do not see their distress."

This simply resolves why it is impossible to interpret the concealment of the face as an expression of a state of anger (as in section A above)—because anger causes "and I will abandon them" (as explicitly stated here: "And My anger will flare... and I will abandon them"). If that were the intent, it should have been stated before "and I will abandon them."

**F.** Based on this, Rashi’s addition, "as if I do not see their distress," is also understood. For otherwise, it is puzzling: if you say that even after the distress comes, Hashem averts His eyes from them and does not see the distress of Yisrael—and in Rashi’s language (mentioned above in Parshas Shemos) that He seals His ears from their cry—then the door is locked against the one who returns! This is the opposite of the purpose of the punishments, which come to bring a person to teshuvah, as the verse says, "Do I desire the death of the wicked? Rather, that the wicked return from his way and live." How can it be said that Hashem does not see their distress?

Therefore, Rashi explains "as if I do not see their distress"—that the conduct of averting the eyes from the distress is not a true averting of the eyes (on Hashem's part), but only an illusion of the recipients. To Bnei Yisrael, it seems that Hashem does not see, but in truth, Hashem sees and sees, and awaits the teshuvah of Yisrael. [On the contrary, the very fact that Hashem hides His face in a way that to Bnei Yisrael it seems He does not see their distress is only to awaken them all the more to return to Hashem.]

In other words: the absence of providence that causes evils and distress is a reality, for from this, evils and distress come (and one does not say about this "as if I do not provide," because this conduct leads to actual results). This is not the case regarding seeing the distress of Yisrael (after it has already come)—there is no actual change here regarding Yisrael. Rather, as soon as they do teshuvah, it is revealed that He truly saw their distress, and He turns to them and saves them. The entire matter of "and I will hide My face" is only from the perspective of Bnei Yisrael, to whom it seems (due to the multitude of distresses) that Hashem does not see their distress.

It can be said that this is also the precision of the language "and I will hide My face" (*hester*)—and not "and I will cover My face" (*kisui*), etc.

The difference between the term "covering" and the term "hiding" is that in "hiding," it is emphasized that this is in relation to another. One cannot say that one hid something without relating to the thing from which it was hidden. This is not the case with the term "covering," which applies even when it does not relate to another thing. Accordingly, the difference between covering the face and hiding the face is that when one covers his face, a change is effected not only regarding the other (that the other does not see him), but also regarding himself, before him, that they are covered. This is not the case when one hides his face; the change (can be) only for the other, who does not see the face, but regarding the face itself, there is no change at all.

This is what Rashi explains: "and I will hide My face" means "as if I do not see their distress." This is only a hiding (and not a covering), because in truth, Hashem certainly sees their distress and does not avert His eyes from Bnei Yisrael at all. Rather, Hashem's seeing is in the manner of "and I will hide My face", such that it seems to Bnei Yisrael that Hashem does not see their distress, as mentioned above.

### 199

From this it is understood that immediately, when Bnei Yisrael turn to Hashem (the opposite of their conduct of "He has abandoned me," which caused "and I will abandon them"), the matter of "and I will abandon them and I will hide My face" is nullified immediately. Hashem reveals Himself to Bnei Yisrael face-to-face, with great affection and love revealed for all to see, and bestows upon Bnei Yisrael all the blessings from His full, open, holy, and expansive hand, in a way of good that is specifically visible and revealed.

(From the Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Vayelech 5742)


## אב, חודש

## Av, Month

 (The constellation of Leo). (As above).


## ה' אב

## 5 Menachem Av


## ט' אב

## Tisha B'Av

(The weeping of Bnei Yisrael regarding the sin of the Meraglim).

## אור ומאור

## Light and the Source of Light

 The distinction between "light" (*ohr*) and "the source of light" (*maor*) is a fundamental concept in Chassidus. The *maor* is the essence, while the *ohr* is the radiance that emanates from it. Seemingly, the *ohr* is merely an extension of the *maor*, possessing no independent existence of its own; it is simply the *maor* as it is perceived outside of itself. Yet, in truth, the relationship between them is more complex. 

When we speak of the *ohr* as it exists within the *maor*, it is entirely nullified—it is not a separate entity at all. It is only when the *ohr* extends outward that it becomes a distinct manifestation. This process of extension is what allows for the possibility of *hispaalus*—the capacity for the recipient to be affected by the light. If the light remained solely within the *maor*, it would be inaccessible. Thus, the *ohr* serves as the bridge, the medium through which the essence of the *maor* is communicated and experienced. 

This is the deeper meaning behind the concept of *tzimtzum*. Through *tzimtzum*, the infinite radiance of the *maor* is contracted, allowing for the emergence of *ohr* that can be contained within the framework of *Seder Hishtalshelus*. Without this contraction, the *ohr* would be so intense that it would be indistinguishable from the *maor*, rendering the existence of separate worlds impossible. Therefore, the *ohr* is not merely a byproduct; it is a necessary development, a deliberate act of *avodah* on the part of the *maor* to facilitate the revelation of *Elokus* within the created order.

## גורל

## Goral

The advantage of *goral* (lottery) is that it transcends the limitations of human intellect and reasoning. When a person is faced with a situation where they must make a decision, and they are unable to arrive at a clear conclusion through their own *sechel* (intellect)—or even when they have reached a conclusion, but there remains a lingering doubt—they turn to *goral*. By doing so, they are essentially saying: "I am placing my trust in Hashem, who is beyond all limitations, to guide me toward the correct path."

This is the essence of *goral*: it is a mechanism that bypasses the *sechel* entirely. It is not a process of calculation or weighing pros and cons; rather, it is an act of *bitul* (nullification) of one’s own limited perspective, allowing the *ratzon* (will) of Hashem to be revealed. Because *goral* operates above the realm of *sechel*, it can reach into the deepest levels of *Elokus* and draw down a *hashpaah* (influence) that is not bound by the natural order of the world.

This is why *goral* is often associated with the concept of *mesiras nefesh*. Just as *mesiras nefesh* involves a person going beyond their own natural inclinations and self-interest to connect with Hashem, *goral* involves a person going beyond their own intellectual capacity to connect with the *ratzon* of Hashem. In this way, the *goral* serves as a conduit for a *giluy* (revelation) that transcends the ordinary *hishtalshelus* (chain of descent), bringing about a result that is truly *l’maalah min ha-teva* (above nature).

## דירה בתחתונים

## A Dwelling in the Lower Worlds

 The concept of "a dwelling in the lower worlds" refers to the purpose of the creation of the world: that Hashem desired a dwelling place in the lower realms. This means that the lowest level of existence—the physical world, which is seemingly distant from Elokus and even conceals it—should be transformed into a place where the Ohr Ein Sof is revealed.

This is accomplished through the avodah of the Jewish people, specifically through the performance of mitzvos with physical objects. By taking a physical object—such as leather for tefillin or wool for tzitzis—and utilizing it for a mitzvah, one draws down the Ohr Ein Sof into that physical object. Through this, the object ceases to be merely a mundane, physical entity and becomes a vessel for Elokus.

This is the ultimate intent of the entire Seder Hishtalshelus. Although the process involves a series of Tzimtzumim, descending from the highest levels down to the lowest, the purpose of this descent is not for the sake of the lower worlds themselves. Rather, the descent is for the sake of the ascent—that through the avodah of the Jewish people, the lowest level is elevated and transformed into a "dwelling" for the Ribono shel Olam. 

Thus, the physical world, which initially appears to be the antithesis of Elokus, becomes the very place where the essence of the Ohr Ein Sof is revealed. This is the realization of the goal of creation: that the lowest level should be equal to the highest level, as the dwelling of the King is in the place that is most precious to Him—the place where His essence is manifest.

## כולל חב&quot;ד

## Kollel Chabad

 The concept of Kollel Chabad is rooted in the fundamental principle of the Alter Rebbe, which is that the primary *avodah* of a Chossid is to assist a fellow Jew—not only in spiritual matters, but in material matters as well. This is based on the teaching that "the material needs of a fellow Jew are my spiritual obligations."

This is the essence of the *hiskashrus* between the Rebbe and the Chassidim: the Rebbe provides the *hashpaah*—the spiritual life-force—and the Chassidim, through their *avodah* of *ahavas Yisroel*, act as the vessels to bring this *hashpaah* down into the world, specifically through the support of those in need. By providing for the physical sustenance of *anash* and the wider community, the Kollel serves as a conduit for the *brachos* of the Rebbe to be manifest in a tangible, revealed way. 

Thus, the work of Kollel Chabad is not merely an act of charity; it is an integral component of the *avodah* of *hafatzas hamaayanos*. When a Jew is relieved of the burden of material distress, they are then free to engage in their own *avodah* of *dveikus* and *bitul* to Hashem. In this sense, the physical support provided by the Kollel is the foundation upon which the spiritual edifice of the individual is built.

## כפר חב&quot;ד (ישוב)

## Kfar Chabad (Settlement)

 The establishment of Kfar Chabad is a matter of great importance, as it is a place where Torah and mitzvos are practiced in a manner that serves as a beacon for the entire country. 

The very name "Kfar Chabad" carries a profound significance. It signifies that the entire essence and atmosphere of the settlement must be permeated with the spirit of Chabad—the path of *chochma, binah,* and *da'as*. This is not merely a geographic location; it is a center for *avodah* and *hiskashrus* to the Rebbe, where the influence of the *neshamah* is felt in every aspect of daily life.

When we speak of such a settlement, we are speaking of a place that must embody the concept of *dirah b'tachtonim*—making this physical world a dwelling place for Hashem. Through the *avodah* of the residents, through their *tefillah*, their *limud haTorah*, and their dedication to *hafatzas hamaayanos*, they transform the mundane into the holy. This is the true purpose of Kfar Chabad: to be a living example of how a community can be built entirely upon the foundations of *bitul* and *mesiras nefesh*, ensuring that every action is directed toward the ultimate goal of the coming of Moshiach.

## לכתחילה אַריבער

## Lechatchilah Ariber

 The concept of *Lechatchilah Ariber*—which the Rebbe Rashab famously articulated—is not merely a directive to overcome obstacles. It is a fundamental shift in the nature of one’s *avodah*.

Seemingly, one might argue that the standard path of *avodah* is to navigate the challenges of the world, step by step, in accordance with the *seder hishtalshelus*. In this approach, one encounters a difficulty, assesses the situation, and then applies the appropriate *avodah* to resolve it. This is the path of *b'di-avad*—or at best, a measured, incremental ascent.

But *Lechatchilah Ariber* demands something entirely different. It dictates that one should not even acknowledge the obstacle as a factor that necessitates a detour or a gradual approach. Instead, one must transcend the limitation entirely, leaping over it from the outset.

And with this we can understand the deeper meaning of this *avodah*. It is not just a matter of "strength" or "determination" to push through. Rather, it is an expression of the soul’s essential *bitul* to Hashem. When a Chossid recognizes that the entire reality of the world—including the very obstacles that appear to impede his *avodah*—is nothing more than a manifestation of *Ohr Ein Sof*, he realizes that these limitations possess no independent existence. 

Therefore, when one acts with the consciousness of *Lechatchilah Ariber*, he is not "fighting" the world; he is operating from a level that precedes the *tzimtzum* that created the obstacle in the first place. He is connecting to the *atzmus* of Hashem, where no limitation can exist. This is why the *avodah* is described as "leaping"—it is a movement that bypasses the constraints of the lower worlds, drawing down the light of *Atzilus* directly into the realm of *Asiyah*. 

Ultimately, this is the essence of the *shaliach’s* mission: to transform the world not by being consumed by its challenges, but by revealing that the world itself is a vessel for *Elokus*. By choosing to go *Lechatchilah Ariber*, the Chossid demonstrates that his *hiskashrus* to the Rebbe and his *dveikus* to Hashem are not bound by the parameters of time, place, or circumstance.

## מותרות

## The Virtue of "Sanctify Yourself in That Which Is Permitted to You"

The concept of "sanctify yourself in that which is permitted to you" implies that even regarding matters that are technically permissible—where there is no halachic prohibition—one should nevertheless exercise a degree of restraint and holiness. This is not merely an act of asceticism or a rejection of the physical world; rather, it is an avodah of refinement. 

By choosing to elevate the mundane through the lens of holiness, one demonstrates that the purpose of the permitted is not for the sake of the physical pleasure itself, but to serve as a vessel for Elokus. When a person engages with the world in a way that is sanctified, they reveal that even the "permitted" is fundamentally rooted in the desire to connect with Hashem. Thus, the act of sanctification transforms the permitted from a neutral state into an opportunity for avodah, ensuring that one’s involvement in the physical realm remains entirely directed toward the ultimate goal of bringing the light of the Ein Sof into the lower worlds.
